Meaning of 'Sayyid' and Its Application to the Prophet
Sanusi explains the etymology, lexical meanings, and prophetic application of 'Sayyid'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic origins and theological significance of calling the Prophet Muhammad 'Sayyid'.

Core Idea
The title 'Sayyid' for the Prophet is a borrowed term, not absolute lordship.

This lesson explores the word 'Sayyid' as applied to the Prophet Muhammad. Sanusi begins with its morphological root and lexical meanings, showing its appropriateness for the Prophet. He then examines hadith evidence and clarifies that calling the Prophet 'Sayyid' does not imply divine lordship, which belongs exclusively to Allah.

Guided Path
1
Understand the etymology of 'Sayyid'
Read the morphological breakdown: how 'saywid' became 'sayyid' through phonetic rules.
Notice the rule of converting waw to ya'.
2
Learn the lexical meanings
Identify the three meanings: one with a large army, a leader, and a perfect person needed in hardships.
All these meanings fit the Prophet.
3
Examine the hadith evidence
Read the cited hadiths, especially 'I am the sayyid of the children of Adam' and the Prophet's response to being called 'sayyid'.
4
Understand the theological nuance
Contemplate the explanation that the Prophet is sayyid metaphorically, while Allah is the true Sayyid.
This guards against misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مقدمة المحشي
محمَّدِ
من ساد يسود سيادةً فهو سيد، وأصله سيود بكسر الواو، قلبت ياءً؛ : لتحركها واجتماعها مع الياء الساكنة ،قبلها، ثمَّ أدغمت فيها؛ لاجتماع المثلين.
والقاعدة: أنَّ الذي يدغم هو الذي يقلب ويردُّ من جنس المدغم فيه، لكن لمَّا كانت الياء أخفَّ من الواو قلبت الواو ياء مطلقاً . اللغة أيضاً على من كثر سواده؛ أي: جيشه، أو المتولي ويطلق في
للسَّواد ؛ أي: الجماعة الكثيرة، وعلى الكامل المحتاج إليه عند وكلُّ هذه المعاني مناسبة لمقامه
الشدائد،
وإطلاق السيّد عليه هل ورد في الأخبار ؛ منها : رواية أحمد والترمذي وابن ماجه عن أبي سعيد : «أنا سيد ولد آدمَ يوم القيامة ولا فخر»(۱)، وغير ذلك من الأحاديث المتواترة.
وقوله لمن قال له يا سيّد: «السَّيِّدُ) هوَ الله (۲)، فمعناه أَنَّه : صلى الله عليه وسلم الحقيق بالسيادة، وإطلاقها على غيره إنَّما هو بطريق العارية؛ فالمقصود منه : إعلام الجاهل بالحقيقة، فتدبّر .
(۱) سنن الترمذي (٣١٤٨) ، سنن ابن ماجه (٤۳۰۸)، مسند الإمام أحمد (۲/۳). (٢) أخرجه أبو داود (٤٨٠٦) ، والنسائي في السنن الكبرى» (۱۰۰۳) من حديث عبد الله بن الشخير الله
Introduction of the commentator: Muhammad. From 'sada yasūdu sayyidatan' so he is a sayyid; it originally was 'sayyidun' with a kasra on the waw, which was changed to a yā' because of its movement and its meeting with a silent yā' before it, then they were assimilated due to the meeting of two identical letters. The rule is that what is assimilated is what is changed and returned to the type of the assimilated one, but since the yā' is lighter than the wāw, the wāw is changed into a yā' absolutely. It is also used in the language for one whose 'sawād' (i.e., his army) is great, or the one who undertakes the leadership for the 'sawād' (i.e., the large group), and for the perfect one who is needed during hardships, and all these meanings are appropriate for his station. Its application to him as 'sayyid' is indeed found in reports: among them is the narration of Ahmad, al-Tirmidhī, and Ibn Mājah from Abū Sa'īd: 'I am the sayyid of the children of Adam on the Day of Resurrection and I do not boast' (1), and other such frequently narrated hadiths. And his statement to the one who said to him 'O Sayyid': 'The Sayyid is Allah' (2), its meaning is that he, peace be upon him, is the one truly deserving of lordship, and its application to others is only by way of borrowing; so the intended purpose is to inform the ignorant of the truth, so contemplate. (1) Sunan al-Tirmidhī (3148), Sunan Ibn Mājah (4308), Musnad al-Imām Ahmad (2/3). (2) Reported by Abū Dāwūd (4806) and al-Nasā'ī in al-Sunan al-Kubrā (1003) from the hadith of 'Abd Allāh ibn al-Shikhkhīr (may Allah be pleased with him).
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the word 'sayyid' as it applies to the Prophet Muhammad. The commentator begins with a morphological breakdown of the word, tracing its root from 's-y-d' and explaining the phonetic change from 'saywid' to 'sayyid'. He then lists the lexical meanings of 'sayyid': one with a large army, a leader of a community, and a perfect person relied upon in times of hardship—all of which are fitting for the Prophet. The commentator then addresses the prophetic hadiths that call the Prophet 'sayyid', citing the famous 'I am the sayyid of the children of Adam' hadith. He also notes the Prophet's own statement to a companion who called him 'sayyid', where the Prophet redirected the absolute lordship to Allah alone. The commentator explains that the Prophet is truly deserving of 'sayyid' in a metaphorical sense, while the ultimate reality of lordship belongs to God. The footnote provides the chains of transmission for these hadiths.

Key Terms
السيد
السيادة
العارية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Calling the Prophet 'Sayyid' implies he shares divine lordship with Allah.
Correction: The Prophet is sayyid metaphorically; absolute lordship belongs only to Allah, as the hadith clarifies.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why did the Prophet tell someone not to call him 'sayyid'?
A) Because he was humble and disliked titles.
B) Because the title 'sayyid' truly belongs only to Allah.
C) Because he only allowed being called 'sayyid' after his death.
D) Because 'sayyid' means 'king' and he was not a king.

Key Takeaway
The Prophet Muhammad is called 'Sayyid' in a borrowed sense, while the true Sayyid is Allah alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you address the Prophet as 'Sayyiduna' (our master) in your prayers or speech, does your heart feel a sense of awe that might unintentionally blur the line between revering a prophet and reserving absolute lordship for Allah? What practical step can you take today to internalize that true sayyid is Allah alone?
?
The Prophet himself corrected a companion who called him 'Ya Sayyid' by redirecting the title to Allah. How often do you check your own speech for phrases that might, even subtly, elevate created beings to a status that belongs only to your Creator? What specific phrase will you be more mindful of starting now?
?
This lesson teaches that applying 'sayyid' to the Prophet is a 'borrowing' to inform the ignorant. If you were to explain this truth to someone who unknowingly ascribes absolute lordship to the Prophet, would your own understanding be clear enough? What is one thing you could study further to deepen your tawheed and guard against even unintentional shirk?

Definitions of Mu'jiza, Bāhira, Āl, and Aṣḥāb
Sanusi defines mu'jiza, explains bāhira, and clarifies āl and aṣḥāb.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definitions of mu'jiza, bāhira, āl, and aṣḥāb as used in classical Sunni kalam and how they relate to the praise of the Prophet.

Core Idea
A mu'jiza is a challenge‑accompanied, norm‑breaking event confirming prophethood.

Sanusi's commentary on the opening praise unpacks several key terms that are foundational for understanding prophethood and miraculous confirmation. Before we can discuss how miracles function, we need a clear definition of what a mu'jiza is and what distinguishes it from other extraordinary events. This passage provides that definition, along with clarifications on the terms used in blessings upon the Prophet.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the source passage once in Arabic, then follow the English translation. Notice how each term is introduced and explained sequentially.
The passage moves from mu'jiza to bāhira to āl and aṣḥāb.
2
Identify the core definition of mu'jiza
Find the three conditions given for a mu'jiza: khāriq li al-ʿāda (breaks the norm), ʿalā yad muddaʿī al-nubuwwa (at the hand of a prophethood claimant), and muqāran bi al-taḥaddī (accompanied by a challenge).
3
Understand the relationship between āl and aṣḥāb
Notice that āl means 'all followers' and aṣḥāb (Companions) is a subset. The conjunction is of the specific to the general.
This resolves a common misunderstanding about who is included in blessings.
4
Reflect on the hadith about blessings
The commentator cites a hadith that shows the formula for sending blessings includes āl. This explains why the text uses the phrase 'ulī al-manāqib'.
The hadith is authentic, from al‑Bukhārī and Muslim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (المؤيَّد) أي: المقوّى من التأييد؛ وهو التَّقوية.
قوله : (بالمعجزات): جمع معجزة؛ وهو الأمر الخارق للعادة الواقع على يد مُدَّعي النبوة، المقرون بالتحدي، وسيأتي ذلك(۱).
قوله : (الباهرة) : أي : الغالبة للخصم.
قوله : (وعلى آله) : المراد بالآل : جميع الأتباع، فعطف (الأصحاب) من عطف الخاص على العام.
وقوله : (أولي المناقب ... إلخ) : نعت لـ ( الأصحاب)، وأتى الشارح بهذه الصيغة ؛ لما في الحديث قال بعض الصَّحابة كيف نصلي عليك يا رسول الله؟ فقال: «قولوا : اللَّهمَّ صلّ على محمد وعلى آلِهِ» رواه الشَّيخان (٢) .
(۱) انظر (ص۳۰۷).
(۲) صحیح البخاري (٦٣٥٧) من حديث كعب بن عجرة لله ، صحيح مسلم (٤٠٥) من حديث بشير بن سعد الله الله ، وفي المطبوع (وآله)، ورواية «الصحيحين»: (وعلى آل محمد).
His statement: (al-mu'ayyad) that is, the strengthened, from al-ta'yīd which is strengthening. His statement: (bi al-mu'jizāt) plural of mu'jiza; it is an event that breaks the norm (khāriq li al-'āda) occurring at the hand of a claimant of prophethood (muddā'ī al-nubuwwa), accompanied by challenge (al-taḥaddī), and this will come later (1). His statement: (al-bāhira) that is, the one that overcomes the opponent. His statement: (wa 'alā ālihi) what is meant by al-āl is all followers, so the conjunction of (al-aṣḥāb) is a conjunction of the specific to the general. And his statement: (ulī al-manāqib ... etc.) is an adjective for (al-aṣḥāb), and the commentator came with this formula because of the hadith: some of the Companions said: 'How should we send blessings upon you, O Messenger of God?' He said: 'Say: O God, send blessings upon Muhammad and upon his āl' narrated by al-Bukhārī and Muslim (2). (1) See p. 307. (2) Sahīh al-Bukhārī (6357) from the hadith of Ka'b ibn 'Ujra, may Allah be pleased with him; Sahīh Muslim (405) from the hadith of Bashīr ibn Sa'd, may Allah be pleased with him; and in the printed version (wa ālihi), and the narration of the two Sahīhs: (wa 'alā āli Muhammad).
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage provides definitions and explanations of key terms in the opening praise. First, 'al-mu'ayyad' is defined as 'strengthened' (from al-ta'yīd, meaning strengthening). Then 'al-mu'jizāt' (miracles) is defined: it is a singular 'mu'jiza', which is an extraordinary event that breaks the natural norm, occurring at the hand of one who claims prophethood, and it is accompanied by a challenge (to produce something similar). A footnote refers to a later discussion. Next, 'al-bāhira' means 'that which overcomes the opponent' (al-ghāliba li al-khaṣm). Then 'wa 'alā ālihi' is explained: 'āl' means all followers, so the mention of 'aṣḥāb' (Companions) is a conjunction of the specific to the general—the 'āl' includes the Companions and beyond. Finally, the phrase 'ulī al-manāqib' is described as an adjective for 'aṣḥāb', and the commentator uses this formula because of the hadith about how to send blessings upon the Prophet, which includes 'āl'. The footnote gives the exact references from al-Bukhārī and Muslim.

Key Terms
المعجزة
التحدي
الآل
الأصحاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'āl' refers only to the Prophet's blood relatives or family.
Correction: The passage clearly states that 'āl' means all followers (atbāʿ), which includes the Companions and all later followers. The term 'aṣḥāb' is a specific subgroup, not a separate category.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, a mu'jiza is defined as an event that:
A) Is performed by any righteous person.
B) Breaks the norm, occurs at the hand of a prophethood claimant, and is accompanied by a challenge.
C) Is supernatural but does not require a challenge.
D) Occurs only to prove the miracles of saints.

Key Takeaway
A mu'jiza is a divinely‑given, norm‑breaking event with a challenge that confirms the truth of a prophethood claim, while 'āl' encompasses all followers of the Prophet.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the salat upon the Prophet, do you consciously include 'āl' as all followers, including yourself? How does that affect the sincerity of your prayer?
?
The mu'jiza is described as 'overwhelming the opponent' (bāhira). In what areas of your life are you still resisting the overwhelming truth of Islam? What would it take for you to surrender completely?
?
The Prophet's miracles were accompanied by a challenge. Has the truth of his prophethood ever challenged you personally—not just intellectually, but in a way that demands a change in your daily choices?

Meaning and Derivation of the Basmala
Al-Sawi explains the derivation and meaning of the basmala.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic derivation of the basmala from 'wasm' and the different interpretations of the annexation in 'bismi Allah'.

Core Idea
The basmala is derived from 'wasm' and can refer to the name or the named.

Before diving into the creedal content, al-Sawi clarifies the linguistic structure of the phrase we use to begin any action. The basmala is not just a word; it has a derivation that links it to the concept of a 'sign' or 'mark'. This lesson unpacks that derivation and the flexible meaning of the annexation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on basmala derivation
Start with the Arabic text about the basmala's origin. Notice how it explains the transformation from 'wasm' to 'bism' by dropping the fa and adding hamzat wasl.
Pay attention to the root letters: waw, sin, mim.
2
Understand the meaning of 'wasm'
The text says 'asluhu wasm' meaning 'its origin is wasm'. This is the triliteral root meaning a mark or sign. The basmala is a sign of the named entity.
3
Examine the annexation possibilities
The passage discusses whether the annexation in 'bismi Allah' is for clarification (li al-bayan) or in the sense of lam. It also mentions that the intended meaning could be the word itself or the named entity.
This shows that both interpretations are valid, as seeking blessing through the name is similar to seeking it through the named.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
معنى البسملة
لأنَّه علامة على مسمَّاه وأصله وسم، فخفف بحذف فائه ثمَّ عُوِّض عنها همزة الوصل .
والمراد به هنا المسمَّى ؛ أي: مستعيناً بمسمى الله .
والإضافة للبيان .
و(الله): علم
قوله : (لأنه علامة) : أي : دالٌ .
قوله : وأصله) وسم) : أي : على وزن (فعل) بفتح الفاء، فالواو فاء الكلمة، والسِّين عينها، والميم لامها .
قوله : (ثم عوّض عنها همزة وصل) : أي : توصُّلاً للنُّطق بالسَّاكن . قوله : (والمراد به هنا ... إلخ) : ليس بمتعين؛ لجواز أن يراد به اللفظ الدَّالُ على ذات الله ؛ لأنَّه يتبرَّك ويُستعان بالاسم، كما يُتبرك بالمسمى، والإضافة على هذا بمعنى اللام.
قوله : (والله : علم على الذَّات ... إلخ) : أي : شخص جزئي، قال السعد وليس من باب الغلبة التحقيقيَّة ولا التَّقديريَّة، والغلبة أن يكون للفظ شمول لأفراد فيحصل له بحسب الاستعمال تخصيص ببعض أفراده؛ فإن وجد له أفراد فاختصّ ببعضها ؛ كانت الغلبة تحقيقيَّة ؛ كالنَّجم اسم لكل كوكب، ثمّ غلب على الثَّريا وإن لم يوجد له إلّا فرد؛ كانت الغلبة تقديرية، خلافاً لقول الخلخالي
The meaning of the Basmala: Because it is a sign of its named entity, and its origin is 'wasm', then it was lightened by dropping its fa', then compensated with a hamzat wasl. What is meant here is the named entity, i.e., seeking help through the named entity 'Allah'. The annexation is for clarification. And 'Allah' is a proper noun. His saying: 'Because it is a sign' means an indicator. His saying: 'And its origin is wasm' means on the pattern of fa'l with fatha, so the waw is the fa of the word, the sin its 'ayn, and the mim its lam. His saying: 'Then compensated with a hamzat wasl' means in order to pronounce the consonant. His saying: 'What is meant here... etc.' is not determined; it is possible that what is meant is the word indicating the essence of Allah, because one seeks blessing and help through the name, just as one seeks blessing through the named entity, and the annexation in this case is in the sense of lam. His saying: 'Allah is a proper noun for the Essence... etc.' means it is a particular individual. Al-Sa'd said: It is not of the category of actual predominant usage nor estimative predominant usage. Predominant usage is when a word has generality over individuals, and then through usage it becomes particularized to some of its individuals. If it has individuals and becomes particularized to some of them, it is actual predominant usage, like 'al-najm' (the star) which is a name for every planet, but then became predominant for Pleiades. And if it has only one individual, it is estimative predominant usage, contrary to the statement of al-Khalkhali.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the basmala and the name 'Allah'. It first defines the basmala as derived from 'wasm' (mark) by dropping the fa and adding hamzat wasl. It then clarifies that in 'bismi Allah', the annexation is explanatory, and 'Allah' is a proper noun ('alam). The commentary goes through each phrase: 'lahu' (it) meaning the basmala is a sign; 'asluhu wasm' gives the triliteral root; 'thumma 'uwwida 'anha hamzat wasl' explains the phonetic change. On 'al-murad bihi huna al-musamma', it notes that it is not certain—it could mean the word itself seeking blessing, or the named entity. Then it addresses 'Allah' as a proper noun for the Essence, a particular individual (shakhs juz'i). It cites al-Sa'd's distinction between actual predominant usage (ghalaba tahqiqiyya) and estimative predominant usage (ghalaba taqdiriya): actual when a word with multiple individuals becomes specialized to some (like 'al-najm' for Pleiades), estimative when it has only one individual and thus cannot be said to be used predominantly. This shows that 'Allah' is not a case of predominant usage but a true proper noun for the Essence.

Key Terms
بسملة
وسم
همزة الوصل
علم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the original root word for 'basmala' according to the passage?
A) بسم
B) وسم
C) اسم
D) سمو

Key Takeaway
The basmala is derived from 'wasm' and can refer to the name of Allah or the named Essence itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Bismillah' (بِسْمِ اللَّهِ) before an action, do you consciously intend to connect your action to the very Essence of Allah, or is it a mere formula? How can you turn this phrase into a real act of seeking His help and presence?
?
The basmala is derived from 'wasm' (a mark). What mark does your 'Bismillah' leave on your deeds? Are your actions truly distinguished by His name, or is it just a habit?
?
Scholars debate whether blessing comes through the name or the named. In your heart, do you treat the Name of Allah with the same reverence as His Essence? Does your utterance of 'Allah' bring you to a state of awe and trust, or has it become a routine?

What Is Kalam?
intermediate
7 min
Allah as a Proper Noun and Predominant Usage
Al-Sawi establishes that 'Allah' is a proper noun and distinguishes it from predominant usage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why 'Allah' is a proper noun for the Essence and the distinction between actual and estimative predominant usage.

Core Idea
Allah is a unique proper noun, not a case of predominant usage.

After explaining the basmala, al-Sawi turns to the name 'Allah' itself. He affirms it is a proper noun for the Essence and addresses a subtle linguistic discussion about whether it falls under categories of predominant usage. This lesson clarifies why it does not.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on 'Allah' as proper noun
The passage states clearly that 'Allah' is a proper noun (علم) for the Essence, a particular individual. This means it is not derived or used for multiple things.
Note that al-Sawi references al-Sa'd and al-Khalkhali.
2
Understand actual predominant usage
Al-Sa'd defines actual predominant usage as when a word originally applies to many individuals but through use becomes restricted to one. Example: 'al-najm' (the star) used specifically for Pleiades.
This category requires multiple potential referents.
3
Understand estimative predominant usage
Estimative predominant usage is when a word has only one referent from the start, so it cannot truly be a case of predominance. It is estimated as such only hypothetically.
Al-Khalkhali disagreed and considered 'Allah' to be estimative predominant usage, but al-Sa'd rejects that.
4
Connect to 'Allah'
Since 'Allah' names the unique Divine Essence, it has only one individual. Therefore it is neither actual nor estimative predominant usage—it is a pure proper noun.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
معنى البسملة
لأنَّه علامة على مسمَّاه وأصله وسم، فخفف بحذف فائه ثمَّ عُوِّض عنها همزة الوصل .
والمراد به هنا المسمَّى ؛ أي: مستعيناً بمسمى الله .
والإضافة للبيان .
و(الله): علم
قوله : (لأنه علامة) : أي : دالٌ .
قوله : وأصله) وسم) : أي : على وزن (فعل) بفتح الفاء، فالواو فاء الكلمة، والسِّين عينها، والميم لامها .
قوله : (ثم عوّض عنها همزة وصل) : أي : توصُّلاً للنُّطق بالسَّاكن . قوله : (والمراد به هنا ... إلخ) : ليس بمتعين؛ لجواز أن يراد به اللفظ الدَّالُ على ذات الله ؛ لأنَّه يتبرَّك ويُستعان بالاسم، كما يُتبرك بالمسمى، والإضافة على هذا بمعنى اللام.
قوله : (والله : علم على الذَّات ... إلخ) : أي : شخص جزئي، قال السعد وليس من باب الغلبة التحقيقيَّة ولا التَّقديريَّة، والغلبة أن يكون للفظ شمول لأفراد فيحصل له بحسب الاستعمال تخصيص ببعض أفراده؛ فإن وجد له أفراد فاختصّ ببعضها ؛ كانت الغلبة تحقيقيَّة ؛ كالنَّجم اسم لكل كوكب، ثمّ غلب على الثَّريا وإن لم يوجد له إلّا فرد؛ كانت الغلبة تقديرية، خلافاً لقول الخلخالي
The meaning of the Basmala: Because it is a sign of its named entity, and its origin is 'wasm', then it was lightened by dropping its fa', then compensated with a hamzat wasl. What is meant here is the named entity, i.e., seeking help through the named entity 'Allah'. The annexation is for clarification. And 'Allah' is a proper noun. His saying: 'Because it is a sign' means an indicator. His saying: 'And its origin is wasm' means on the pattern of fa'l with fatha, so the waw is the fa of the word, the sin its 'ayn, and the mim its lam. His saying: 'Then compensated with a hamzat wasl' means in order to pronounce the consonant. His saying: 'What is meant here... etc.' is not determined; it is possible that what is meant is the word indicating the essence of Allah, because one seeks blessing and help through the name, just as one seeks blessing through the named entity, and the annexation in this case is in the sense of lam. His saying: 'Allah is a proper noun for the Essence... etc.' means it is a particular individual. Al-Sa'd said: It is not of the category of actual predominant usage nor estimative predominant usage. Predominant usage is when a word has generality over individuals, and then through usage it becomes particularized to some of its individuals. If it has individuals and becomes particularized to some of them, it is actual predominant usage, like 'al-najm' (the star) which is a name for every planet, but then became predominant for Pleiades. And if it has only one individual, it is estimative predominant usage, contrary to the statement of al-Khalkhali.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the basmala and the name 'Allah'. It first defines the basmala as derived from 'wasm' (mark) by dropping the fa and adding hamzat wasl. It then clarifies that in 'bismi Allah', the annexation is explanatory, and 'Allah' is a proper noun ('alam). The commentary goes through each phrase: 'lahu' (it) meaning the basmala is a sign; 'asluhu wasm' gives the triliteral root; 'thumma 'uwwida 'anha hamzat wasl' explains the phonetic change. On 'al-murad bihi huna al-musamma', it notes that it is not certain—it could mean the word itself seeking blessing, or the named entity. Then it addresses 'Allah' as a proper noun for the Essence, a particular individual (shakhs juz'i). It cites al-Sa'd's distinction between actual predominant usage (ghalaba tahqiqiyya) and estimative predominant usage (ghalaba taqdiriya): actual when a word with multiple individuals becomes specialized to some (like 'al-najm' for Pleiades), estimative when it has only one individual and thus cannot be said to be used predominantly. This shows that 'Allah' is not a case of predominant usage but a true proper noun for the Essence.

Key Terms
الله
علم
الغلبة التحقيقية
الغلبة التقديرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think 'Allah' is a common noun like 'god' that became specific through predominant usage.
Correction: The passage clarifies that 'Allah' is a proper noun from the beginning, not a common noun that underwent specialization.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the difference between actual predominant usage (ghalaba tahqiqiyya) and estimative predominant usage (ghalaba taqdiriya)?
A) Actual involves multiple individuals; estimative involves only one.
B) Actual is for stars; estimative is for Allah.
C) Actual is used in Arabic; estimative is used in other languages.
D) There is no difference; both are the same.

Key Takeaway
Allah is a unique proper noun for the Essence, distinct from categories of predominant usage because it has always designated only One.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah', do you truly recognize it as the unique, unmatched name of the One Essence, or do you sometimes let your heart treat it like a common title for a distant deity? How can you deepen your awareness of His unique identity every time you utter His name?
?
If Allah's name is a proper noun that has always referred only to Him alone, what does that demand from you in terms of the exclusivity of your worship and love? Are there any 'partners'—even hidden ones like status, wealth, or relationships—that you inadvertently treat as having a share in what belongs only to Allah?
?
The scholars took great care to defend the precise meaning of Allah's name from subtle errors like predominant usage. How much care do you take to guard your understanding of who Allah is from influences that dilute His majesty? What is one practical step you can take today to align your heart's focus more exclusively on the unique Being named 'Allah'?

The Basmala and Wajib al-Wujud: Clarifying the Named Essence
Commentary on Basmala referring to the Essence and defining Wajib al-Wujud

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of the Basmala as referring to the Essence that is necessarily existent, and why the attribute 'Necessary Existence' is mentioned only to identify that Essence, not as part of the name.

Core Idea
The Basmala names the Essence alone; 'Necessary Existence' identifies which Essence.

The Basmala (Bismillah) is the first thing Muslims say before any action. But what does it actually refer to? In this short commentary, the author explains that 'Bismillah' points to the Essence of Allah, which is necessarily existent. He clarifies a subtle point: the attribute 'Wajib al-Wujud' (Necessary Existence) is not part of the name itself but serves to identify which Essence is meant.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the literal translation. Notice how the author begins by stating the meaning of the Basmala and then unpacks each phrase.
Pay attention to the grammatical notes about the definite article and the taa.
2
Focus on the two explanations
The passage explains two things: (1) 'upon the Essence' – the alif lam indicates a specific known Essence, the Creator, and the taa is for unity. (2) 'the Necessary Existence' – defines that Essence as eternal and necessary. Reflect on why these details matter.
The author emphasizes that the Essence is the Creator and cannot not exist.
3
Understand the distinction between name and attribute
The commentator explicitly states that mentioning 'Wajib al-Wujud' is only to clarify which Essence is named, not to make the attribute part of the name. The name refers solely to the Essence. This guards against the misconception that Allah's attributes are included in His name.
This is the key doctrinal point of the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
معنى البسملة على الذات الواجب الوجود الخالق للعالم. قوله (على الذات (أل) للعهد؛ أي: الذات المعهودة، وهي: الخالقة للعالم، وتاؤها ليست للتأنيث، بل للوحدة. قوله : (الواجب الوجود) أي: الذَّات التي لا يمكن عدمها في الماضي ولا في الحال ولا في الاستقبال والغرض من ذكر واجب الوجود بيان الذات المسمَّى لا بيان اعتباره في المسمى؛ لأنَّ المسمَّى الذات وحدها لا الذات مع الوصف.
The meaning of the Basmala: upon the Essence, the Necessary Existence, the Creator of the world. His statement: ('upon the Essence' – the 'alif lam' is for known reference, i.e., the known Essence, which is the Creator of the world, and its 'taa' is not for feminine gender but for unity. His statement: ('the Necessary Existence') i.e., the Essence that cannot not exist in the past, present, or future. The purpose of mentioning 'Necessary Existence' is to clarify the Essence named, not to clarify its consideration in the name; because the named is the Essence alone, not the Essence with the attribute.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the Basmala. It begins by stating the meaning: 'Bismillah' indicates the Essence that is necessarily existent, the Creator. The commentator then explains two points. First, the phrase 'upon the Essence' uses the definite article for a known reference—the specific Essence that is the Creator, not any other. The 'taa' at the end of 'dhat' is for unity, not femininity. Second, 'the Necessary Existence' defines that Essence as one that cannot be non-existent in any time. The purpose of mentioning this attribute is to identify which Essence is meant, not to make the attribute part of the name itself; the name refers only to the Essence, not the Essence together with the attribute.

Key Terms
البسملة (Basmala)
الذات (Essence)
الواجب الوجود (Necessary Existence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the attribute 'Necessary Existence' is part of the name 'Allah' and that the name includes both the Essence and the attribute.
Correction: The commentary explicitly states that mentioning 'Wajib al-Wujud' is only to clarify the Essence named, not to make the attribute part of the name. The name refers only to the Essence itself.

Checkpoint

B) It explains the attribute that is essential to the Essence.
C) It identifies which Essence is meant, not to include the attribute in the name.
D) It indicates that the Essence is contingent.

Key Takeaway
The Basmala refers to the Essence alone; 'Wajib al-Wujud' is an identifier, not part of the name.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Bismillah' before an action, do you truly intend the One who is necessary in existence, or do you sometimes let your mind wander to other thoughts? How would focusing on the Essence alone change your awareness of Allah in that moment?
?
The commentary insists that the attribute 'Wajib al-Wujud' is only to identify, not to be part of the name. Reflect: Have you ever attached other attributes to your understanding of Allah in a way that overshadowed the pure essence? What would it mean to know Allah as the One who simply is, without adding mental labels?
?
The Basmala is the most repeated phrase in your life. Today, would you say it with the conscious recognition that it names the sole Necessary Existence? What would it cost you to live each moment with that awareness?

The Basmala: Metaphorical Interpretation and Defense
Al-Sawi explains the basmala and defends a metaphorical interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the metaphorical interpretation of the Basmala according to Al-Sawi and how he defends it against objections while preserving divine transcendence.

Core Idea
Allah’s mercy cannot mean tenderness of heart; it must be understood metaphorically.

Al-Sawi tackles a critical point: how do we understand the names ar-Raḥmān and ar-Raḥīm without attributing human emotions to Allah? He presents a sophisticated metaphorical analysis, deals with objections, and settles on the safest interpretation. This lesson walks through his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the overall flow of the argument. Notice the structure: problem, proposed metaphor, objections, answers, conclusion.
The passage includes a biography of Ḥafīd al-Saʿd in the footnote.
2
Identify the core problem
The literal meaning of 'mercy' (tenderness of heart) is impossible for Allah. Look at how Al-Sawi states this and why it necessitates a metaphorical interpretation.
3
Understand the representational metaphor
Al-Sawi presents Ḥafīd al-Saʿd's view: the Basmala is a representational metaphor likening Allah's bestowal to a king's. Grasp the comparison and the borrowed configuration.
Remember: representational metaphors apply to compounds, not single words.
4
Follow the objection and answer
Three objections are raised against the metaphor. Read each and see how Al-Sawi answers them. Then note his final preference for an unrestricted metaphor (majāz mursal).
This shows the methodology of theological argumentation: proposal, critique, refinement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
معنى البسملة
لاستحالتها عليه تعالى؛ أي: الثابت له التفضل والإحسان كثيراً،
على المسبب، وذكر حفيد السَّعد (۱) أنَّ في الكلام استعارة تمثيليَّة؛ بأن يقال : شبَّه حال المولى مع خلقه في الإنعام بجلائل النعم ودقائقها بحال مَلِكٍ مع رعيَّته واستعيرت الهيئة الدالة على المشبه به للمشبَّه .
وأورد عليه : أنَّ الاستعارة التَّمثيليَّة لا تكون إلَّا في المركَّبات، وإطلاق الحال على الله لم يرد إذن به، وأنَّ الرَّحمن لم يستعمل في غيره تعالى، وأنَّ المشبه به أقوى؛ وهو إساءة أدب
وأجيب بأنَّه اقتصر على الجزء الأهم من المركَّب؛ إذ هو مركب : بحسب الأصل : فإنَّ الأصل : ملك رحمن رحيم، وإطلاق الحال جائز لضرورة التّعليم، والحقُّ ثبوت مجازات لا حقائق لها، وكون المشبه به أقوى أغلبي، وبعد هذا كله فالأحسن الاقتصار على كونه مجازاً مرسلاً .
تعالى .
قوله : (لاستحالتها) : أي : رقّة القلب.
قوله : ( أي : الثَّابت له التَّفضُّل ... إلخ) : بيان للمعنى المراد اللائق به
(۱) هو شيخ الإسلام أحمد بن يحيى بن محمد التفتازاني يعرف بـ(حفيد السعد)، من فقهاء الشافعية كان قاضي هراة ثلاثين سنة، له مصنفات عدة؛ منها: «الدر النضيد في مجموعة الحفيد»، و«حاشية على شرح التلخيص، وغيرها، توفي الله سنة
(٩١٦هـ) ينظر «الأعلام» (۲۷۰/۱).
The meaning of the Basmala: due to its impossibility for Him, may He be exalted; meaning, He is characterized by bestowing favor and bounty abundantly, upon the caused. And Ḥafīd al-Saʿd mentioned that in the statement there is a representational metaphor (istiʿāra tamthīliyya); by saying: the state of the Master with His creatures in bestowing great and subtle blessings is compared to the state of a king with his subjects, and the configuration indicating the compared-to is borrowed for the compared. And an objection was raised against him: that the representational metaphor is only in compounds, and applying the term 'state' (ḥāl) to God has not been permitted, and that ar-Raḥmān is not used for other than Him, and that the compared-to is stronger, and that is disrespect. And it was answered: that he confined himself to the most important part of the compound, for it is compound according to the origin; for the origin is: a king merciful and compassionate, and applying the term 'state' is permissible for the necessity of teaching, and the truth is that there are metaphors (majāzāt) that have no literal meanings, and that the compared-to being stronger is a general tendency, and after all this, the best is to restrict it to being an unrestricted metaphor (majāz mursal). Exalted is He. His saying: (due to its impossibility) means: tenderness of heart. His saying: (meaning, He is characterized by bestowal... etc.) is an explanation of the intended meaning befitting Him. (1) He is Shaykh al-Islām Aḥmad ibn Yaḥyā ibn Muḥammad al-Taftāzānī, known as Ḥafīd al-Saʿd, one of the Shāfiʿī jurists, he was judge of Herat for thirty years, he has several works; among them: 'al-Durr al-Naḍīd fī Majmūʿat al-Ḥafīd' and 'Ḥāshiya ʿalā Sharḥ al-Talkhīṣ', and others. He died in the year (916 AH). See 'al-Aʿlām' (1/270).
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the basmala (بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم). It begins by explaining that the literal meaning of الرحمة (tenderness of heart) is impossible for Allah, so the intended meaning is 'bestowing favor and bounty.' The commentator then presents the view of Ḥafīd al-Saʿd, who interprets the phrase as a representational metaphor (استعارة تمثيلية): the state of the Lord with His creatures is likened to that of a king with his subjects. Three objections are raised: (1) representational metaphors only apply to compound expressions, (2) applying the term 'state' (حال) to Allah is not permitted, (3) the name الرحمن is unique to Allah, and (4) the comparison is disrespectful as it suggests a stronger comparison. The answers are: (1) the metaphor focuses on the main element of the compound, whose origin is 'king merciful compassionate'; (2) applying 'state' is allowed for pedagogical necessity; (3) there are metaphors without literal basis; (4) the objection about strength is not absolute. The final conclusion is that the best view is to consider it an unrestricted metaphor (مجاز مرسل). The commentator then glosses the word 'impossibility' (لاستحالتها) to mean 'tenderness of heart' (رقة القلب), and explains that the expression 'the bestowal...' clarifies the befitting meaning. The footnote provides a brief biography of Ḥafīd al-Saʿd (Aḥmad al-Taftāzānī), noting his Shāfiʿī affiliation, his judgeship, and his works.

Key Terms
بسملة
استعارة تمثيلية
مجاز مرسل
حفيد السعد
رقة القلب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the names ar-Raḥmān and ar-Raḥīm can be understood literally for Allah, implying tenderness of heart.
Correction: Al-Sawi shows that the literal meaning (tenderness of heart) is impossible, so they must be understood metaphorically, ultimately as an unrestricted metaphor (majāz mursal).

Checkpoint
What is the final judgment of Al-Sawi regarding the attribution of الرحمة to Allah?
A) It is a literal attribute.
B) It is a representational metaphor (استعارة تمثيلية).
C) It is an unrestricted metaphor (مجاز مرسل).
D) It should be avoided altogether.

Key Takeaway
Al-Sawi ultimately favors interpreting الرحمة as a مجاز مرسل (unrestricted metaphor) to avoid anthropomorphism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you utter 'Bismillahir Rahmanir Rahim', do your heart and mind truly acknowledge that Allah's mercy is real and abundant, even if your imagination cannot grasp it? What concrete step will you take today to deepen your conviction in His mercy as taught in this lesson?
?
Are you prepared to accept that some of your preconceived notions about Allah's attributes, like a simplistic understanding of mercy, may need to be refined through study? Will you seek out the deeper meanings to worship Him with greater awareness and humility?
?
If a fellow believer questioned the authenticity of metaphorical interpretation, could you explain, using the reasoning from this passage, why it is necessary and not a denial of mercy? How would you defend the honor of Allah's names while maintaining respect for scholarly tradition?

Hamdalah as an Additional Opening: Reconciling Two Hadiths
Al-Sawi explains the dual opening and reconciles contradictory hadiths.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author of al-Kharidah opened with both basmalah and hamdalah, and how this reconciles two seemingly contradictory hadiths.

Core Idea
Hamdalah is an additional opening that follows the real opening of basmalah.

When you begin a book or speech, which phrase should you use: 'Bismillah' or 'Alhamdulillah'? Two hadiths give different instructions. The author of al-Kharidah uses both, and Al-Sawi explains that this is not a contradiction but a deliberate reconciliation. In this lesson, we explore how the basmalah is the real opening and the hamdalah an additional one.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and its translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to understand the main idea.
Pay attention to the terms 'iftitāḥ ḥaqīqī' and 'iftitāḥ iḍāfī'.
2
Identify the two types of openings
Notice that the author distinguishes between a 'real opening' (basmalah) and an 'additional opening' (hamdalah). Find these terms in the passage.
3
Understand the reconciliation
Ask yourself: Why does Al-Sawi consider the basmalah as the real beginning and the hamdalah as additional? The passage gives two reasons: agreement with the Qur'an and the strength of the hadith of basmalah.
The hadith of hamdalah is considered weaker, hence it is placed secondarily.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مطلب في بيان معنى الحمد
(الحمد الله) هو وما بعده إلى آخر الكتاب
قوله: (الحمد الله): لما افتتح بالبسملة افتتاحاً حقيقياً افتتح بالحمدلة افتتاحاً إضافياً، وهو ما تقدَّم على الشروع في المقصود بالذات؛ جمعاً بين حديثي البسملة والحمدلة.
وحمل البسملة على الابتداء الحقيقي، والحمدلة على الإضافي؛ الموافقة القرآن العزيز ولقوَّة حديث البسملة على حديث الحمدلة، وهو قوله صلى الله عليه وسلم : كلُّ كلام لا يُبدأُ فيه بالحمد الله؛ فهو أجذم(۱)، وهناك أوجه أُخر مشهورة لدفع التّعارض.
Section on explaining the meaning of praise (al-ḥamd). (Al-ḥamdu lillāh) is what follows and after it to the end of the book. His statement: (Al-ḥamdu lillāh): Since he began with the basmalah as a real opening, he began with the hamdalah as an additional opening, which is what precedes the commencement of the intended subject matter itself, reconciling between the two ḥadīths of the basmalah and the hamdalah. And he interpreted the basmalah as the real beginning, and the hamdalah as the additional one, in agreement with the Noble Qur’an and due to the strength of the ḥadīth of the basmalah over the ḥadīth of the hamdalah, which is his statement, peace be upon him: 'Every speech that does not begin with praise of God is maimed.' And there are other well-known ways to remove the contradiction.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory section on the meaning of 'al-ḥamd' (praise). The author notes that the phrase 'الحمد لله' (praise be to God) is the beginning of the main text. Al-Sawi comments that the author opened with the basmalah (in the name of God) as a real opening (iftitāḥ ḥaqīqī), and then opened with the hamdalah (the phrase 'al-ḥamdu lillāh') as an additional opening (iftitāḥ iḍāfī), which precedes the actual subject matter. This is done to reconcile the two ḥadīths: one commanding to begin with basmalah and another commanding to begin with hamdalah. The reconciliation is achieved by interpreting the basmalah as the real beginning and the hamdalah as an additional or secondary beginning. This interpretation is favored because it agrees with the Qur'an (which begins with basmalah) and because the ḥadīth of basmalah is considered stronger (due to its chains) than the ḥadīth of hamdalah. The passage also mentions that there are other well-known ways to resolve the apparent contradiction.

Key Terms
الحمد
البسملة
الحمدلة
افتتاح حقيقي
افتتاح إضافي
الحديث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author contradicts himself by starting with both basmalah and hamdalah.
Correction: The author intentionally uses both to reconcile two hadiths, with basmalah as the real opening and hamdalah as an additional one.

Checkpoint
Why does the author use both basmalah and hamdalah at the beginning?
A) He forgot to choose one
B) To reconcile two hadiths
C) To make the book longer
D) By mistake

Key Takeaway
The author uses basmalah as the real opening and hamdalah as an additional opening to reconcile two hadiths, prioritizing the stronger hadith of basmalah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you begin an important task, do you consciously prioritize what is truly primary (like basmalah) over what is merely customary? Reflect on one habit you can change to align with stronger evidence.
?
This lesson reconciles two hadiths by giving each its proper place. Is there a contradiction in your own practice between what you know is stronger (e.g., a clear command) and what you are used to doing? Will you adjust your practice today?
?
The author chooses the basmalah as the real opening because its hadith is stronger and it agrees with the Qur’an. Are you willing to let go of a cherished habit when you find it contradicts a stronger proof? What would that cost you—and is it worth it for the sake of truth?

Declarative or Performative? The Hamdalah Sentence
Al-Sawi discusses the grammatical and theological classification of 'al-hamdu lillah'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the phrase 'al-hamdu lillah' is considered declarative (khabariyya) and not performative, and how the objection regarding the pre-eternity of God's attributes is resolved.

Core Idea
The basmala is a report, not a new creation — it affirms what is eternal.

At first glance, saying 'Praise be to God' might seem like an act of praising. But Muslim theologians carefully distinguish between reporting praise and performing praise. This lesson unpacks the debate: is the sentence declarative (khabariyya) or performative (insha'iyya)? Al-Sawi presents the correct view, an objection, and a precise answer that safeguards God's pre-eternity while maintaining the sincerity of the praise.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice that it begins with a classification into two possibilities.
Pay attention to the technical terms: khabariyya and insha'iyya.
2
Identify the two views
The passage first says the sentence is either declarative in both wording and meaning (correct) or declarative in wording but performative in meaning.
The correct view is the first one.
3
Understand the objection
The objection argues that a servant cannot originate God's being worthy of praise because that is eternal.
This objection targets the performative interpretation.
4
Grasp the answer
The answer refines the meaning of 'performative': it refers to initiating the praise (the act of praising), not initiating the content (God's worthiness).
This resolves the conflict with pre-eternity.
5
Note the reason for nominal form
The passage concludes by explaining why the nominal form is preferred: it indicates permanence and follows the Quran.
This reinforces the declarative nature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجملة الحمدلة إمَّا خبريَّة لفظاً ومعنى؛ بناء على أنَّ المخبر بالحمد حامد؛ وهو الصحيح، أو خبريَّة لفظاً، إنشائية معنى.
واستشكل : بأنَّه لا يمكن العبد أن ينشئ اختصاصه تعالى بالمحامد أو استحقاقه إيَّاها لقدم ذلك.
وأجيب : بأنَّ المراد بكونها إنشائيَّة أنّها لإنشاء الثَّناء بمضمونها لا أنَّها لإنشاء مضمونها؛ إذ هو ثابت أزلاً لا يمكن إنشاؤه من العبيد، وآثر الاسميَّة لدلالتها على الثبوت والدوام، واقتداء بالكتاب العزيز.
And the sentence of the hamdalah is either declarative (khabariyya) in wording and meaning, based on the fact that the one who reports praise is a praiser; and this is the correct view, or declarative in wording, performative (inshāʾiyya) in meaning. An objection is raised: that the servant cannot originate the attribution of praises to God, may He be exalted, or His deserving them, due to the pre-eternity of that. And it is answered: that the intended meaning of it being performative is that it is for initiating praise with its content, not that it is for initiating the content itself; for that is eternally established and cannot be initiated by servants, and the nominal form was preferred for its indication of permanence and continuity, and in imitation of the Noble Book.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage is a theological-grammatical analysis of the sentence 'الْحَمْدُ لِلَّهِ' (praise be to God). It begins by classifying the sentence as either declarative (khabariyya) in both wording and meaning—which is the correct view because the one who utters it is reporting that praise belongs to God—or declarative in wording but performative in meaning (i.e., it actually performs the act of praising). The latter view is then problematized with an objection: a servant cannot newly originate (inshā') the attribution of praises to God or God's deserving them, since those are eternal and pre-eternal (qadīm). The objection is answered by clarifying that if it is considered performative, it means the servant is initiating praise by expressing that content, not initiating the content itself (i.e., not making God deserving of praise anew). The passage concludes by noting that the nominal form (al-ḥamdu lillāh) is preferred over the verbal form because it indicates permanence and continuity (thubūt wa dawām), and also to follow the Qur'anic style.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that calling the sentence 'performative' means the servant creates God's worthiness of praise, which is impossible.
Correction: The performative interpretation only refers to initiating the praise (the act of praising), not initiating the content (God's worthiness), which is eternal and cannot be created.

Checkpoint
According to the correct view mentioned in this passage, how is the sentence 'al-hamdu lillah' classified?
A) Performative in wording and meaning
B) Declarative in wording and meaning
C) Declarative in wording, performative in meaning
D) Performative in wording, declarative in meaning

Key Takeaway
The hamdalah is a declarative sentence that reports an eternal truth, and any performative interpretation is carefully qualified.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'al-hamdu lillah', are you merely repeating a formula, or does your heart actively engage in the praise? How can you ensure your utterance is both a report and a genuine act of praise?
?
The passage teaches that God's praiseworthiness is eternal and unchangeable. How does this truth comfort you in times of hardship, when you may not feel like praising?
?
The nominal form indicates continuity. Is your praise of God a continuous state in your life, or is it limited to specific moments? What practical step can you take today to make 'al-hamdu lillah' a constant reality in your heart?

Linguistic Definition of Al-Ḥamd
Al-Sawi defines ḥamd linguistically with its four conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise linguistic definition of al-ḥamd (praise) and its key components: praise with beauty for a voluntary good in a manner of glorification, covering both virtues and favors.

Core Idea
Al-Ḥamd is praise of a voluntary good with glorification of the heart.

This lesson unpacks the linguistic definition of al-ḥamd, a central term in Islamic theology and worship. The definition distinguishes ḥamd from mere commendation (madḥ) by specifying its object and manner. Let's explore each component carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the Definition and Translation
Begin by reading the Arabic definition of al-ḥamd along with its literal English translation. Notice the structure: the definition lists several conditions.
Pay attention to each term: thanāʾ, jamīl, ikhtiyārī, taʿẓīm.
2
Identify the Four Components
Break down the definition into four parts: (1) praise (al-thanāʾ), (2) with beauty (bi-l-jamīl), (3) for a voluntary beautiful thing (ʿalā jamīl ikhtiyārī), and (4) in a manner of glorification (ʿalā jihati l-taʿẓīm). Understand each component.
The phrase 'with beauty' is descriptive, like 'I saw with my eye' – it specifies the means of praise.
3
Understand the Exclusion of Non-Voluntary Beauty
The condition 'voluntary' (ikhtiyārī) excludes things that are beautiful but not voluntary. Praise for such things is called madḥ (commendation), not ḥamd. This is a key distinction.
Voluntary can be actual (actions) or legal (essence and attributes as sources of actions).
4
Reflect on the Final Phrase: Virtues and Favors
The definition ends with 'whether it relates to virtues (al-faḍāʾil) or favors (al-fawāḍil).' This means ḥamd applies equally to inherent qualities and external bounties. No distinction is needed.
This broadens the scope of praise in worship and theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
معنى الحمد والحمد لغة : هو الثَّناء بالجميل على جميل اختياري على جهة التّعظيم، سواء تعلَّق بالفضائل أم بالفواضل. اقتصر الشارح عليها قوله : (لغة) : منصوبٌ على التّمييز . قوله : (هو الثَّناء): بتقديم المثلثة على النُّون والمد : الذِّكر بخير، النُّون على المثلثة والقصر ضدُّه، وحينئذ فقوله : (بالجميل) وصف وبتقديم كاشف على حدّ نظرت بعيني، وسمعت بأذني، والمراد به الصادر بالكلام قديماً كان أو حادثاً، فيشمل أقسام الحمد الأربعة. قوله : (بالجميل) بيان للمحمود ،به وللصيغة الصَّادرين من الحامد للمحمود . قوله : على جميل اختياري) بيان للمحمود عليه، والمراد بالاختياري حقيقة كالحمد على صفات الأفعال، أو حكماً : كالحمد على الذات وصفاتها ؛ لأنَّها منشأ أفعال اختياريَّة، وخرج بذلك ما كان جميلاً غير اختياري، فالثناء عليه مدح. قوله : (على جهة التَّعظيم) : أقحم لفظة (جهة) إشعاراً بأنَّه لا يكفي في الحمد التَّعظيمُ الظَّاهري، بل لا بدَّ أن يوافق الكلام الجنان، كذا قيل؛ لكن قال الأشياخ : الرَّاجح عدم اشتراطه . قوله : (سواء تعلق بالفضائل) : سواء خبر مقدم، وما بعده في تأويل مصدر مبتدأ مؤخَّر، والمعنى : تعلّقه بالفضائل أم بالفواضل مستو
The meaning of al-ḥamd: And al-ḥamd linguistically is: praise (al-thanā') with beauty (al-jamīl) for a voluntary beautiful thing (jamīl ikhtiyārī) in a manner of glorification (taʿẓīm), whether it relates to virtues (al-faḍā'il) or favors (al-fawāḍil). The commentator limited himself to it. His statement: '(linguistically)' is in the accusative as a specification. His statement: '(is praise)' with the thā' before the nūn and with lengthening: remembrance with goodness, with the nūn before the thā' and with shortening is its opposite. So his statement: '(with beauty)' is a description and is like what the grammarian calls a 'disambiguating' structure like 'I saw with my eye' and 'I heard with my ear'. What is intended by it is the speech act, whether pre-eternal or temporal, so it includes the four divisions of praise. His statement: '(with beauty)' is an explanation of what is praised and the form issuing from the praiser to the praised. His statement: 'for a voluntary beautiful thing' is an explanation of the reason for praise, and what is meant by 'voluntary' is either real like praise for attributes of actions, or legal like praise for the Essence and Its attributes because they are the source of voluntary actions. And by this is excluded what is beautiful but not voluntary, for praising that is madḥ (commendation). His statement: '(in a manner of glorification)': he inserted the word 'manner' to indicate that apparent glorification is not sufficient in praise, but rather the speech must correspond to the innermost heart (al-janān). Thus it is said; but the masters said: the preponderant view is that this condition is not required. His statement: '(whether it relates to virtues)': 'whether' is a preposed predicate, and what follows is interpreted as a substantivized clause as a postponed subject, and the meaning is: its relation to virtues or to favors is equal.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed linguistic definition of al-ḥamd (praise). The commentator explains each component of the definition given by the author. The definition states: 'Ḥamd linguistically is praise with beauty for a voluntary beautiful thing in a manner of glorification, whether it pertains to virtues or favors.' The commentator clarifies that 'praise' (al-thanā') means remembrance with goodness. He notes that the phrase 'with beauty' (bi-l-jamīl) is an descriptive structure similar to 'I saw with my eye' – it specifies the means. The praise can be pre-eternal (like God's praise of Himself) or temporal (like the praise of creatures), covering all four types of praise. The phrase 'for a voluntary beautiful thing' (ʿalā jamīl ikhtiyārī) specifies the object of praise: it must be a voluntary good, either actually voluntary (like actions) or legally voluntary (like the Essence and attributes, which are the source of voluntary acts). This excludes involuntary beauty, which would be madḥ (commendation) rather than ḥamd. The phrase 'in a manner of glorification' (ʿalā jihati l-taʿẓīm) implies sincerity – the praise must come from the heart, but the preponderant view is that this condition is not necessary. Finally, 'whether it relates to virtues or favors' means that ḥamd applies to both inherent virtues like knowledge and external favors like bounties – no distinction is needed.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that ḥamd (praise) is only for favors or benefits received, not for inherent virtues.
Correction: The definition explicitly states that ḥamd applies to both virtues (al-faḍāʾil) and favors (al-fawāḍil). It is not restricted to voluntary benefits.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The linguistic definition of ḥamd includes praise with beauty for a voluntary good in a manner of glorification, applying equally to virtues and favors.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Think of a blessing you received recently. Did you only thank Allah for that specific favor, or did you also praise Him for His virtues—His knowledge, power, mercy? Which do you neglect, and how can you change that starting now?
?
There are beautiful things around you that are not voluntary—like a sunset or a baby's smile. The lesson says praising that is madḥ, not ḥamd. How often do you mistakenly elevate creation to the status of the Creator? How will you redirect your praise to Allah alone for His voluntary acts?

The Five Pillars of Praise: Fada'il and Fawadil
Sanusi defines fada'il and fawadil and enumerates the five pillars of hamd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between fada'il and fawadil, and the five essential components of the act of praise (hamd) as outlined by Sanusi.

Core Idea
Praise has five pillars: the praiser, the praised, the reason, the quality, and the words.

Sanusi begins by clarifying two categories of praiseworthy qualities: fada'il (virtues that are inherently good) and fawadil (benefactions that depend on their effect on others). He then derives the five pillars of any act of praise, using the example of praising someone for their knowledge. This passage lays the groundwork for understanding how praise is structured in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two categories
Read the definitions of fada'il and fawadil carefully. Notice the condition about whether the effect reaches others. Fada'il are like knowledge; fawadil like generosity.
2
Identify the five pillars
The passage lists five components: hamid, mahmud, mahmud bihi, mahmud 'alayhi, sigha. Think about how each plays a role in praise.
3
Apply the example
The author gives the example of saying 'Zayd is knowledgeable' when praising him for honoring you. Map each component to this example.
This helps solidify the abstract concepts.
4
Reflect on the distinction
Consider why the author distinguishes between fada'il and fawadil before giving the pillars. How does this classification prepare the ground?
This shows the systematic nature of kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمراد بالفضائل : المزايا القاصرة وهي التي لا يتوقف تعلُّقها على تعدّي أثرها للغير، وإن كانت هي متعديَّة؛ كالعلم والقدرة والحسن، وبالفواضل المزايا المتعديَّة، وهي التي يتوقف تعلُّقها على تعدّي أثرها للغير؛ كالكرم والتَّعليم، وهذه العبارة معنى قول غيره، سواء كان في مقابلة نعمة أم لا، فتحصل أنَّ أركان الحمد خمسة: حامد، ومحمود، ومحمود به، ومحمود عليه وصيغة؛ فإذا حمدت زيداً لكونه أكرمك بقولك : زيد عالم؛ فأنت حامد وزيد محمود، والإكرام محمود عليه؛ أي: محمود به لأجله، وثبوت العلم الذي هو مدلول قولك: زيد عالم؛ محمود به، وقولك : زيد عالم هو الصيغة.
By 'al-fada'il' are meant the restricted merits, which are those whose attribution does not depend on the effect reaching others, even if they are transitive in themselves—like knowledge, power, and beauty. By 'al-fawadil' are meant the transitive merits, which are those whose attribution depends on the effect reaching others—like generosity and teaching. This expression is the meaning of the saying of another: whether it is in return for a blessing or not. Thus it becomes clear that the pillars of praise (ḥamd) are five: a praiser (ḥāmid), a praised one (maḥmūd), that by which praised (maḥmūd bihi), that for which praised (maḥmūd ʿalayhi), and the formula (ṣīgha). So if you praise Zayd for having honored you by saying 'Zayd is knowledgeable,' then you are the praiser, Zayd is the praised one, the honor is that for which praised—i.e., for the sake of which he is praised—and the affirmation of knowledge, which is the meaning of your statement 'Zayd is knowledgeable,' is that by which praised, and your statement 'Zayd is knowledgeable' is the formula.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage defines two types of praiseworthy qualities and then enumerates the five essential components (arkan) of the act of praise (hamd). First, it explains that 'al-fada'il' (virtues) are qualities whose attribution to a person does not depend on their effect extending to others, even if the quality itself is transitive (e.g., knowledge, power, beauty). In contrast, 'al-fawadil' (benefactions) are qualities whose attribution does depend on their effect reaching others (e.g., generosity, teaching). The author then states that these correspond to a distinction made by another scholar, and notes that this applies whether or not the praise is in response to a blessing. From this, he derives the five pillars of hamd: (1) the praiser (hamid), (2) the praised one (mahmud), (3) that by which praise is expressed (mahmud bihi), (4) that for which praise is given (mahmud 'alayhi), and (5) the verbal formula (sigha). He provides a concrete example: if you praise Zayd for having honored you by saying 'Zayd is knowledgeable,' then you are the praiser, Zayd is the praised one, the honoring is the occasion (mahmud 'alayhi), the knowledge affirmed is the attribute (mahmud bihi), and your statement is the formula.

Correction: Sanusi expands to five by including 'that by which' (the attribute) and 'that for which' (the reason).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Alhamdulillah', do you consciously identify what specific attribute of Allah you are praising (fada'il) and for which specific blessing you are grateful (fawadil)? This lesson reveals that every act of hamd has five pillars. Without this awareness, is your praise truly directed, or is it a mere habit? Commit today to utter one hamd with deliberate attention to all five components.
?

Now break down that praise using the five pillars: who is the hāmid, who is the maḥmūd, what is the maḥmūd bihi, what is the maḥmūd ʿalayhi, and what is the ṣīgha? Did your praise honor the person as Allah deserves to be honored? Or have you ever praised someone for a virtue (fadīla) that you did not truly believe they possessed? This lesson demands integrity in every word of praise.
?
Allah’s attributes are divided into fada'il (essential) and fawadil (relational). When you praise Allah, do you only think of His blessings (fawadil) and forget to praise Him for His very essence (fada'il)? Today, set aside a moment to praise Allah solely for His knowledge, power, and beauty—without any request or mention of His gifts. Can you do it without feeling that it is incomplete? Let this lesson transform your hamd.

The Object of Praise Must Be Voluntary
Sanusi lays down the condition for the object of praise with detailed examples from divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the object of praise (maḥmūd ʿalayhi) must be voluntary either actually or judicially, and how this applies to God's attributes and essence.

Core Idea
Praise is only for that which is voluntary, but God's attributes are voluntary by legal judgment.

Sanusi begins by detailing the first component of praise: the object or occasion for which one is praised. He establishes a crucial condition: it must be voluntary. But what about praising God for His attributes like power and knowledge, which are not voluntary in the usual sense? Sanusi explains that they are considered 'voluntary by judgment' because they give rise to voluntary acts or are necessary concomitants of an essence that does.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition for the object of praise
Read the first sentence carefully. Sanusi states that the object of praise must be voluntary either actually or judicially. Note the two ways: being the source of voluntary acts or being a necessary concomitant of that source.
2
Understand 'voluntary by judgment'
Look at the examples: God's power, will, and knowledge. They are not voluntary in themselves, but because they produce voluntary acts, they are considered voluntary. This is 'by judgment' (ḥukman).
Similarly, God's essence is treated as voluntary for the same reason.
3
Examine attributes that do not directly cause voluntary acts
Sanusi then discusses hearing, sight, and speech. These do not directly cause voluntary acts, but they are necessary concomitants of the essence that does. Therefore, they too are voluntary by judgment.
This shows the breadth of the condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأنَّ المحمود عليه يُشترط فيه أن يكون اختيارياً حقيقة أو حكماً؛ بأن يكون منشأ لأفعال اختياريَّة أو ملازماً لمنشئها فيصدق بقدرة الله وإرادته وعلمه إذا حُمد لأجلها؛ فإنَّها وإن كانت غير اختياريَّة حقيقةً لكنَّها اختياريَّة حكماً؛ لأنَّها ينشأ عنها فعل اختياري، وكذا يصدق بذات الله إذا حمد لأجلها فهي اختيارية حكماً لما ذكر. وكذا يصدَّق بالسَّمع والبصر والكلام ونحوها مما لا ينشأ عنه فعل اختياري إذا حمد لأجلها، فهي اختياريَّة حكماً باعتبار أنَّها ملازمة للذات التي ينشأ عنها فعل اختياري. وأنَّ المحمود به لا يشترط فيه أن يكون اختيارياً؛ بل تارة يكون اختيارياً كالكرم وتارة لا يكون اختيارياً؛ كحسن الوجه. وأنَّ المحمود به والمحمود عليه تارة يختلفان ذاتاً واعتباراً، كأن يكون المحمود عليه الكرم، والمحمود به العلم وتارةً يتحدان ذاتاً ويختلفان اعتباراً، كأن يكون كل منهما نفس الكرم، لكنَّه من حيث كونه باعثاً على الحمد يقال له: محمود عليه ومن حيث كونه مدلول الصيغة يقال له: محمود به. قوله: (وفي عرف أهل الشَّرع): المراد بهم بعض المتكلمين، وإلا فأهل اللُّغة والشَّرع اتَّفقوا على أنَّ حقيقة الحمد الوصف بالجميل، فليس الحمد لغة أعم منه شرعاً.
And that the object for which praised (maḥmūd ʿalayhi) is required to be voluntary (ikhtiyārī) either actually (ḥaqīqatan) or judiciously (ḥukman); such that it is the source of voluntary acts or necessarily concomitant to that source. Thus it applies to the power, will, and knowledge of God when He is praised on their account; for though they are not voluntary in reality, they are voluntary judiciously because they give rise to a voluntary act. Likewise, it applies to God's essence when praised on its account, for it is voluntary judiciously due to what has been mentioned. Likewise, it applies to hearing, sight, speech and similar attributes that do not give rise to a voluntary act when praised on their account, for they are voluntary judiciously in consideration of their being necessary concomitants of the essence that gives rise to a voluntary act. And that that by which praised (maḥmūd bihi) is not required to be voluntary; rather it may be voluntary like generosity or non-voluntary like beauty of face. And that the object for which praised (maḥmūd ʿalayhi) and that by which praised (maḥmūd bihi) may differ in essence and consideration, such as when the object is generosity and that by which is knowledge; and they may be identical in essence but differ in consideration, such as when both are the same generosity, but insofar as it is the motive for praise it is called 'object' and insofar as it is the meaning of the formula it is called 'that by which.' His statement 'In the custom of the people of the revealed law (sharʿ)' refers to some theologians (mutakallimīn); otherwise the linguists and the jurists agree that the reality of praise (ḥamd) is describing with the beautiful, so praise in language is not more general than it is in law.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage clarifies two important conditions regarding the components of praise. First, it states that the occasion of praise (maḥmūd ʿalayhi) must be voluntary (ikhtiyārī), either truly or by legal judgment (ḥukman). The author explains that even attributes of God like power, will, and knowledge, though not voluntary in themselves, are considered voluntary because they produce voluntary acts. Likewise, God's essence is considered voluntary for the same reason. Even attributes like hearing and sight, which do not directly produce voluntary acts, are considered voluntary because they are necessary concomitants of an essence that does produce voluntary acts. Second, the attribute praised (maḥmūd bihi) does not need to be voluntary; it can be either voluntary (e.g., generosity) or non-voluntary (e.g., beauty). The passage then discusses the relationship between the object and the attribute: they may be different in essence (e.g., generosity as object, knowledge as attribute) or they may be the same essence but different in consideration (e.g., generosity considered as motive vs. as content of the formula). Finally, the gloss notes that the phrase 'in the custom of the people of the sharʿ' refers only to some mutakallimūn; otherwise, the linguistic and legal definitions of ḥamd agree—it is simply describing with beauty, so the legal definition is not narrower.

اختياري حكما
ذات الله
قدرة
إرادة
علم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that only human voluntary actions can be objects of praise, and divine attributes cannot fit.
Correction: Sanusi shows that divine attributes are considered 'voluntary by judgment' because they are either sources of voluntary acts or necessary concomitants of such sources.

Checkpoint
Why are God's hearing and sight considered voluntary by judgment according to the passage?
A) Because they are intrinsically voluntary
B) Because they give rise to voluntary acts
C) Because they are necessary concomitants of the essence that gives rise to voluntary acts
D) Because they are attributes of perfection

Key Takeaway
The object of praise must be voluntary, and for God's attributes, this is fulfilled through their connection to voluntary acts—either as their source or as necessary concomitants.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you praise God for His power, will, or knowledge, do you ever wonder why those attributes deserve praise? How does the fact that they are 'voluntary by judgment' deepen your understanding of your own praise? Will you now be more deliberate in thanking God for attributes like hearing and sight, which you might have overlooked?
?
If God's essence itself is praiseworthy only because it gives rise to voluntary acts, what does that say about your own intentions and actions? Are your voluntary deeds truly worthy of praise? What would it take for your life to become a source of praise to God?
?
This lesson teaches that even attributes like hearing and sight are praiseworthy because they are inseparable from God. Have you ever praised God specifically for His hearing, that He hears your every whisper? If not, what stops you? Today, will you make a deliberate act of praise for one of His attributes that you normally take for granted?

beginner

The Attribute of Praise Need Not Be Voluntary
Sanusi states the condition for the attribute of praise and gives examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the attribute or quality by which one is praised (maḥmūd bihi) does not have to be voluntary; it can be either voluntary like generosity or non-voluntary like beauty.

Core Idea
Praise can be for non-voluntary attributes like beauty; only the occasion of praise must be voluntary.

Having established that the object of praise must be voluntary, Sanusi now turns to the other component: the attribute or quality that is the content of the praise. He surprises by stating that this attribute does not need to be voluntary at all. A person can be praised for innate qualities like beauty or intelligence, which are not under one's control.

Guided Path
1
Read the condition for the attribute of praise
Sanusi says that the attribute (maḥmūd bihi) is not required to be voluntary. It can be either voluntary or non-voluntary.
2
Examine the examples
The voluntary example is generosity (karm). The non-voluntary example is beauty of face (ḥusn al-wajh). Notice that both can be valid attributes for praise.
This contrasts with the object of praise, which must be voluntary.
3
Reflect on the implication
Think about why this distinction matters. Praise formulas (like 'You are generous' or 'You are beautiful') can refer to qualities that are not chosen, but the reason for praising (the object) must be something the person did voluntarily.

Source Text

How the Object and Attribute of Praise Relate
Sanusi classifies the possible relationships between the object and attribute of praise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the object of praise (maḥmūd ʿalayhi) and the attribute of praise (maḥmūd bihi) can be either identical in essence but different in consideration, or different in both essence and consideration.

Core Idea
The same thing can be both the object and attribute of praise, viewed from different angles.

Sanusi now explores the relationship between the two components of praise. He presents two possibilities: they can be entirely different in essence, such as when the object is generosity and the attribute is knowledge; or they can be the same essence like generosity, but considered from two perspectives—as the motive for praise (object) and as the content of the formula (attribute).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two possible relations
Sanusi says the object (maḥmūd ʿalayhi) and attribute (maḥmūd bihi) may either differ in essence and consideration, or be identical in essence but differ in consideration.
2
Analyze the first case: different essence
Example: object is generosity, attribute is knowledge. Here the reason for praise (generosity) is one thing, but the formula mentions knowledge. This is possible because one can be praised for generosity while being described as knowledgeable.
3
Analyze the second case: same essence, different consideration
Example: both are generosity. As a motive for praise, it is called 'object'; as the content of the praise formula, it is called 'attribute'. The same quality serves both roles.
This highlights the conceptual distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

The True Definition of Praise (Ḥamd) in Language and Law
Sanusi clarifies the definition of ḥamd and the reference of 'custom of the people of the sharʿ'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the definition of praise (ḥamd) as 'describing with the beautiful' is agreed upon by linguists and jurists, and that the phrase 'in the custom of the people of the sharʿ' actually refers only to some theologians, not a different definition.

Core Idea
Praise means the same in language and law: describing something with beauty.

Sanusi concludes his discussion on the components of praise with a clarifying remark about the phrase 'in the custom of the people of the sharʿ'. He points out that this phrase is used by some theologians, but in reality, both linguists and jurists agree that the essence of praise (ḥamd) is simply 'describing with beauty'. Therefore, the legal definition is not more specific than the linguistic one.

Guided Path
1
Read the final gloss
Sanusi comments on an earlier phrase 'in the custom of the people of the sharʿ'. He says this refers only to some theologians (mutakallimīn).
2
Understand the agreement between language and law
He asserts that linguists and jurists (ahl al-lugha wa al-sharʿ) agree that praise is 'describing with the beautiful'. So the definition is the same.
3
Note the implication
This means that praise (ḥamd) is not more general in language than in law; they share the same meaning.
This counters a potential misconception that the term might have a broader linguistic range.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'al-ḥamdu lillāh', do you consciously reflect on the beautiful attributes of Allah you are describing, or has it become a mere habit? What would it take for your praise to be a true description of His perfection?
?
Sanusi teaches that praise is 'describing with the beautiful'. Is your praise of others, when given, truthful and accurate? Do you ever praise insincerely or exaggerate? How can you align your everyday praise with this definition?
?
If the reality of praise is consistent across language and law, what does that demand from you in terms of your intention when you praise Allah? Are you merely uttering words, or are you committing to a truthful description that should transform your heart and actions?
