Al-Hukm: Legal and Non-Legal Judgment
Defines al-hukm and divides it into shar'i and ghayr shar'i.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of al-hukm and the distinction between legal judgment (shar'i) and non-legal judgment (ghayr shar'i).

Core Idea
Legal judgment is Allah's speech about our actions; non-legal judgment is any attribution.

Before we dive into theological arguments, we need to clarify what we mean by 'judgment' (hukm). The author presents two fundamental types: legal judgment, which is the address of Allah concerning human actions, and non-legal judgment, which is simply affirming one thing of another. Understanding this distinction is crucial for following later discussions on divine command and moral obligation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text carefully. Notice how the author begins by defining 'hukm' as either shar'i or other, then elaborates on each.
Pay attention to the parenthetical examples from the Qur'an.
2
Identify the definition of legal judgment
Find where the author defines legal judgment as 'khitab Allah' (the address of Allah). Understand that it means Allah's eternal speech, not the act of speaking.
The commentary clarifies that 'khitab' here is a verbal noun used to mean the passive participle (i.e., what is addressed).
3
Understand the scope of legal judgment
Note that legal judgment includes Allah's speech about non-action matters (like essences and attributes) but is narrowed to acts of the morally responsible when it comes to request, permission, or positing.
4
Define non-legal judgment
Find the definition of non-legal judgment as 'ithbat amr li amr' (affirmation of a thing for a thing). This covers all attributive statements like 'Zayd is standing'.
5
Reflect on the broader usage of 'hukm'
The passage notes that 'hukm' has multiple usages. Distinguish between the legal usage (Allah's address) and the logical/grammatical usage (relative judgment).
This prevents confusion in later readings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( والحكم إما شرعيٌّ :أي من حيث هو قوله: (خطاب الله): :أي: كلامه تعالى المخاطب به من إطلاق المصدر وإرادة اسم المفعول، وليس باقياً على مصدريَّته من أنَّه توجيه الكلام إلى مخاطب لعدم صحته هنا ؛ لأنَّه تعريف للأزليّ، هذا كالجنس فيدخل فيه كلامه تعالى المتعلّق بغير أفعال المكلَّفين؛ كالمتعلّق بذواتهم (۱) والمتعلّق بذاته تعالى وصفاته (۲) وأفعاله (۳). (۱) كقوله تعالى: كُنتُمْ خَيْرَ أُمَّةٍ أُخْرِجَتْ لِلنَّاسِ) [آل عمران: ١١٠]. (٢) كقوله سبحانه : الله خَلِقُ كُلِّ شَيْءٍ) [الرعد: ١٦]. (۳) كقوله جل جلاله : وَنَزَّلْنَا مِنَ السَّمَاءِ مَاءً مُبَرَكَا) [ ق : ۹] . أقسام الحكم المتعلّق بأفعال المكلَّفين بالطَّلب أو الإباحة أو الوضع لهما، وإمَّا غيره، وهو : إثبات أمر لأمر قوله : (المتعلّق بأفعال المكلفين): كالفصل، خرج به المتعلّق بغير أفعالهم، فلا يسمى حكماً شرعياً، والمراد تعلُّق دلالة لا تعلُّق تأثير ولا انکشاف . قوله : (وإمَّا غيره) : مقابل قوله : (إما شرعي) قوله : (وهو : إثبات ... إلخ) اعلم أنَّ الحكم له إطلاقات؛ منها : خطاب الله ، ومنها النّسبة الحكميَّة؛ كثبوت القيام لزيد في زيد قائم
His saying: (And al-hukm is either shar'i) i.e. from the perspective that it is the saying of Allah. His saying: (the address of Allah) i.e. His exalted speech addressed, by using the verbal noun and intending the passive participle, and it is not remaining on its verbal noun meaning of directing speech to an addressee because that is not correct here, since it is a definition of the eternal. This is like a genus, so it includes His exalted speech related to other than the acts of the morally responsible, like related to their essences (1) and related to His exalted essence and attributes (2) and His acts (3). (1) Like His saying: 'You are the best nation brought forth for mankind' [3:110]. (2) Like His saying: 'Allah is the Creator of everything' [13:16]. (3) Like His saying: 'And We sent down from the sky blessed water' [50:9]. Sections of al-hukm: Related to the acts of the morally responsible by request or permission or positing for them both, or other than it, and it is: affirmation of a thing for a thing. His saying: (Related to the acts of the morally responsible) is like a differentia; it excludes what is related to other than their acts, so it is not called a legal judgment, and what is intended is a relation of indication, not a relation of effect nor disclosure. His saying: (or other than it) is the counterpart of his saying: (either shar'i). His saying: (and it is: affirmation...) Know that al-hukm has usages; among them: the address of Allah, and among them: the relative judgment, like the establishment of standing for Zayd in 'Zayd is standing'.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'al-hukm' (judgment) and divides it into two types: shar'i (legal) and ghayr shar'i (non-legal). The author explains that the legal judgment is defined as 'the address of Allah' (khitab Allah). The comment clarifies that 'address' here means Allah's speech as an eternal attribute, not the act of addressing, because that would imply temporality. This definition includes Allah's speech about things other than the acts of the morally responsible (mukallafun), such as statements about their essences, His attributes, or His acts. Examples are given from the Qur'an. Then the text narrows the definition by adding 'related to the acts of the morally responsible by request, permission, or positing for them both'. This qualification excludes Allah's speech about other topics from being called legal judgment. The second type of judgment is non-legal judgment, defined as 'the affirmation of a thing for a thing', i.e., an attributive judgment like 'Zayd is standing'. Finally, a note mentions that the word 'hukm' has multiple usages: it can mean Allah's address (the legal usage) or the relative judgment in logic/grammar.

الحكم الشرعي
خطاب الله
الحكم غير الشرعي
إثبات أمر لأمر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think 'khitab Allah' (address of Allah) means the act of speaking or a temporal event.
Correction: In this context, it refers to Allah's eternal speech, not the act of directing speech to someone.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the definition of legal judgment (al-hukm al-shar'i)?
A) The affirmation of a thing for a thing
B) Allah's speech related to the acts of the morally responsible by request, permission, or positing
C) Allah's speech about any topic
D) The rational judgment that something is necessary

Key Takeaway
Legal judgment is Allah's eternal speech concerning human actions, while non-legal judgment is any affirmation of one thing of another.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The lesson explains that legal judgment is only about your actions. How many of your daily decisions do you consciously submit to Allah's judgment? Name one area of your life you have not yet made subject to His command, and resolve to seek His ruling on it.

The Three Components of Legal Judgment
Identifies three components of legal judgment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three components of legal judgment (talab, ibaha, wad') and the four types of request.

Core Idea
Legal judgment is built on request, permission, and positing.

Sanusi begins by detailing the three elements that make up the legal judgment: request (talab), permission (ibaha), and positing (wad'). Request alone has four subdivisions based on act/abandonment and decisiveness, which together with permission form the five taklifi categories. This lesson lays the foundation for understanding the full spectrum of Islamic legal rulings.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the passage once to get the overall structure. Notice how Sanusi introduces each component with 'qawlahu'.
The passage is from a commentary on Kharidat, so it explains an earlier text.
2
Identify the three components
Find the three components: talab (request), ibaha (permission), and wad' (positing). See how they are connected grammatically.
3
Understand the four types of talab
Focus on the explanation of talab: it includes four divisions based on act/abandonment and decisiveness. Map these to the well-known taklifi categories.
This leads to the five taklifi when combined with ibaha.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (بالطلب) : الباء للملابسة متعلقة بـ(خطاب) من ملابسة ما هو ؛ كالكلِّي لما هو كجزأيه، والطلب شامل لأقسامه الأربعة، إذ هو إما طلب فعل أو ترك، وفي كلِّ إمَّا جازم أو غير جازم. قوله : (أو الإباحة) معطوف على (الطَّلب ) . قوله : (أو الوضع لهما ) : معطوف على (الإباحة)، والضمير في (لهما ) عائد على الطّلب والإباحة . والوضع : جعل الشَّيء شرطاً أو سبباً، أو مانعاً أو صحيحاً أو فاسداً، وحدودها مشهورة فمثال السَّبب بالنسبة للصَّلاة دخول الوقت، والشرط؛ كالطَّهارة والمانع ؛ كالحيض، والصحة موافقتها الشرع باستيفاء الشروط وانتفاء الموانع، والفساد ضدُّه، فتحصل أنَّ الشَّرعيّ أقسامه عشرة؛ خمسة منها تكليفية، وخمسة وضعيّة
His saying: (by request) – the preposition 'ba' indicates connection, connected to 'address' from the connection of what is, like the universal to what is like its two parts. And request includes its four divisions, for it is either a request for an act or abandonment, and in each either decisive or non-decisive. His saying: (or permission) is coordinated with 'request'. His saying: (or positing for them both) is coordinated with 'permission', and the pronoun in 'for them both' refers to request and permission. And positing: making a thing a condition or a cause, or an obstacle, or valid, or invalid; their definitions are well-known. For example, the cause (sabab) for prayer is the entry of time, the condition (shart) is purification, the obstacle (mani') is menstruation, validity (sihha) is its conformity to the law by fulfilling conditions and absence of obstacles, and invalidity (fasad) is its opposite. So it results that the legal judgment has ten divisions: five of them taklifiyyah (obligatory) and five wad'iyyah (positing).
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage details the components of legal judgment (al-hukm al-shar'i) as introduced in the previous lesson. It explains the three elements: request (talab), permission (ibaha), and positing (wad'). Request is further subdivided into four types, based on whether it demands an act or abandonment, and whether it is decisive (jāzim) or non-decisive (ghayr jāzim). This gives the well-known five taklifi (obligatory) categories: obligatory (wajib), recommended (mandub), prohibited (haram), disliked (makruh), and permissible (mubah). Permission (ibaha) is the same as the permissible. Positing (wad') includes five categories: cause (sabab), condition (shart), obstacle (mani'), validity (sihha), and invalidity (fasad). Examples are given: time as a cause for prayer, purification as a condition, menstruation as an obstacle, validity as conformity to law, and invalidity as its opposite. Altogether, legal judgment has ten divisions: five taklifiyyah (related to obligation) and five wad'iyyah (related to positing).

إباحة
وضع
جازم
غير جازم

Correction: Wad' is explicitly mentioned as a third component, covering cause, condition, obstacle, validity, and invalidity.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How does your heart respond to Allah's permission compared to His demand? This lesson shows that permission stands separate from request; are you confusing ibaha with talab to excuse yourself? Ask Allah to give you clarity and strength to act on what He truly asks, not what you wish He asked.
?
The concept of wad' (positing) is often overlooked, yet it shapes your daily worship—like conditions for prayer or causes for rulings. Do you ever ignore a condition (shart) because you think it's 'just a technicality'? This lesson places wad' as a full component of divine judgment; will you treat it with the same weight as a direct command? Resolve to study the ahkam al-wad'iyya and apply them consciously.

The Ten Divisions of Legal Judgment

He then concludes that the entire legal judgment has ten divisions—five taklifiyyah (derived from talab and ibaha) and five wad'iyyah (from wad'). Definition and Scope of Hukm Shar'i
Sanusi defines hukm shar'i and explains its scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of hukm shar'i and what it includes and excludes.

Core Idea
Hukm shar'i is the divine address relating to actions of the legally responsible.

Sanusi begins by laying the foundational definition of religious ruling (hukm shar'i). This definition is the key to understanding all subsequent divisions. The author carefully specifies that it relates to human actions through request, permission, or stipulation, and that the relation is one of indication, not causation.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Read the Arabic definition carefully: 'al-muta'alliq bi-af'āl al-mukallafīn bi-ṭ-ṭalab aw al-ibāḥa aw al-waḍ' lahumā'. Notice the three categories mentioned.
2
Understand the exclusion
The phrase 'kharaja bihi al-muta'alliq bi-ghayr af'ālihim' excludes rulings about things other than human actions, such as the attributes of Allah.
3
Grasp the nature of relation
The author clarifies that the relation here is of indication (dalāla), not effect (ta'thīr) or disclosure (inkishāf). This is important for later discussions.
This prevents misunderstanding that rulings cause actions directly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقسام الحكم
المتعلّق بأفعال المكلَّفين بالطَّلب أو الإباحة أو الوضع لهما، وإمَّا غيره، وهو : إثبات أمر لأمر
وقوله : (المتعلّق بأفعال المكلفين): كالفصل، خرج به المتعلّق بغير أفعالهم، فلا يسمى حكماً شرعياً، والمراد تعلُّق دلالة لا تعلُّق تأثير ولا انکشاف .
وقوله : (بالطلب) : الباء للملابسة متعلقة بـ(خطاب) من ملابسة ما هو ؛ كالكلّيّ لما هو كجزأيه، والطلب شامل لأقسامه الأربعة، إذ هو إما طلب فعل أو ترك، وفي كلِّ إمَّا جازم أو غير جازم.
وقوله : (أو الإباحة) معطوف على (الطَّلب ) .
وقوله : (أو الوضع لهما ) : معطوف على (الإباحة)، والضمير في (لهما ) عائد على الطّلب والإباحة .
والوضع : جعل الشَّيء شرطاً أو سبباً، أو مانعاً أو صحيحاً أو فاسداً، وحدودها مشهورة فمثال السَّبب بالنسبة للصَّلاة دخول الوقت، والشرط؛ كالطَّهارة والمانع ؛ كالحيض، والصحة موافقتها الشرع باستيفاء الشروط وانتفاء الموانع، والفساد ضدُّه، فتحصل أنَّ الشَّرعيّ أقسامه عشرة؛
خمسة منها تكليفية، وخمسة وضعيّة
Sections of ruling (hukm): relating to actions of the legally responsible (mukallafin) by request (talab) or permission (ibaha) or stipulation (wad') for both, or other than that, and it is: affirming a thing for a thing. His statement: (relating to actions of the legally responsible) is like a separator, by it is excluded what relates to other than their actions, so it is not called a religious ruling (hukm shar'i), and what is meant is relation of indication, not relation of effect nor disclosure. His statement: (by request): the ba is for association, attached to 'address' (khitab), of the association of part to whole; request includes its four divisions, for it is either request for action or abandonment, and in each either decisive (jazim) or non-decisive. His statement: (or permission) is conjunct to (request). His statement: (or stipulation for both) is conjunct to (permission), and the pronoun in (for both) refers to request and permission. Stipulation (wad') is making a thing a condition (shart) or cause (sabab), or preventive (mani') or valid (sahih) or invalid (fasid); their definitions are well known; e.g., cause for prayer is the entry of time, condition like purity, preventive like menstruation, validity is its agreement with the law by fulfilling conditions and absence of preventives, and invalidity is its opposite. So it results that the religious ruling has ten divisions: five of them are constituting obligation (taklifiyya) and five stipulatory (wadi'yya).
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage defines religious ruling (hukm shar'i) and enumerates its ten subdivisions. The author begins by stating the definition: 'relating to actions of the legally responsible by request, permission, or stipulation for both, or other than that, and it is affirming a thing for a thing.' The commentary then explains each component: 'relating to actions of the legally responsible' excludes actions of other beings; the relation is of indication, not causation. 'By request' – the preposition indicates association with the divine address; request includes four types: commanding action, prohibiting action, and each can be decisive (obligatory or forbidden) or non-decisive (recommended or disliked). 'Permission' is the fifth type (permissible). 'Stipulation for both' refers to the five wad'i categories: cause, condition, preventive, validity, and invalidity. Examples are given for prayer: time as cause, purity as condition, menstruation as preventive, validity as fulfilling conditions and lacking preventives, invalidity as its opposite. Thus the total is ten: five taklifi (obligation, prohibition, recommendation, dislike, permission) and five wad'i (cause, condition, preventive, validity, invalidity).

مكلَّف

إباحة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Hukm shar'i only includes obligations and prohibitions.
Correction: The definition includes permission (ibaha) and stipulation (wad'), showing it is broader.

Checkpoint
What does the definition of hukm shar'i exclude?
A) Actions of the legally responsible
B) Rulings about non-human actions
C) Permission (ibaha)
D) Stipulation (wad')

Key Takeaway
Hukm shar'i is the indicative divine address concerning the actions of responsible persons through request, permission, or stipulation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The definition of hukm shar'i excludes rulings about Allah's essence. When you read the Quran, do you distinguish between verses that command your action and verses that describe Allah's attributes? How might neglecting this distinction affect your worship? What will you do this week to focus on the commands that demand a response from you?
?
The relation in hukm shar'i is one of indication, not effect. Do you sometimes wait for a divine command to 'compel' you before obeying, rather than taking the indication as enough? In what specific area of your life have you used this as an excuse for delay? Commit today to act on one clear command without waiting for a stronger feeling.
?
Since hukm shar'i includes permission and stipulative rulings, not just obligation and prohibition, have you been ignoring the aspects of divine address that are not explicitly commanding? Consider one area where you have freedom (ibaha) – are you using it to please Allah or to indulge yourself? What change will you make this week to turn your permissions into acts of worship through intention?

The Five Taklifi Rulings
Sanusi explains the subdivisions of talab and includes ibaha as the fifth taklifi ruling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five taklifi rulings and how they derive from request (talab) and permission (ibaha).

Core Idea
The five taklifi rulings are obligation, prohibition, recommendation, dislike, and permission.

After defining hukm shar'i, Sanusi unpacks the category of 'request' (talab) and shows how it yields four rulings: obligation, prohibition, recommendation, and dislike. Permission (ibaha) is the fifth. This lesson clarifies this subdivision.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of request
Notice that talab is divided into request for action and request for abandonment. Each can be decisive (jāzim) or non-decisive (ghayr jāzim).
2
Map to the five rulings
Request for action + decisive = obligation (wājib); request for action + non-decisive = recommendation (mandūb); request for abandonment + decisive = prohibition (ḥarām); request for abandonment + non-decisive = dislike (makrūh). Permission (ibāḥa) is the fifth, neither requesting action nor abandonment.
3
Understand the inclusion of permission
The source explicitly says 'aw al-ibāḥa' as a separate category, so it is not a request but a distinct type of ruling.
This shows the five taklifi rulings.

Source Text

The Five Wad'i Rulings
Sanusi explains the five wad'i rulings and gives examples, concluding the tenfold classification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five wad'i rulings: cause, condition, preventive, validity, and invalidity, and their examples.

Core Idea
Wad'i rulings are stipulative: they make something a cause, condition, preventive, valid, or invalid.

Sanusi concludes the classification by explaining the stipulative (wad'i) rulings. These are not directly about obligation but about establishing relationships. He lists five types and gives examples from prayer to illustrate.

Guided Path
1
Learn the definition of wad'
The text defines wad' as 'making a thing a condition or cause, or preventive or valid or invalid.'
2
Study the examples
Notice the examples: time as cause for prayer, purity as condition, menstruation as preventive, validity as fulfilling conditions and lacking preventives, invalidity as the opposite.
3
Count the total
The author concludes that hukm shar'i has ten divisions: five taklifi and five wad'i.

The Multiple Usages of Hukm and the Intended Meaning
Enumeration of the usages of the term 'hukm'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the various technical usages of the term 'hukm' in Islamic theology, and identify which specific meaning is intended in the definition of non-religious rulings.

Core Idea
The word 'hukm' has multiple meanings; in this definition, it means 'affirming a thing for a thing'.

Before analyzing the definition of rulings as 'affirming a thing for a thing or its negation', it is crucial to recognize that the term 'hukm' is used in several distinct senses. The author lists five usages, from the address of Allah to the relational proportion, subject, predicate, and finally the intended meaning: affirming a thing. This lesson clarifies those usages.

Guided Path
1
Read the enumeration of usages
Read the passage where the author lists the different meanings of hukm. The passage begins with 'اعلم أنَّ الحكم له إطلاقات'.
The five usages are listed in sequence.
2
Identify the five usages
Note each usage: address of Allah, relational proportion, subject, predicate, and affirming a thing. Understand which one is the focus.
Affirming a thing is the intended meaning here.
3
Connect to the definition
See how the definition 'affirming a thing for a thing' corresponds to the fifth usage.
The definition is exemplified with 'Zayd is standing'.

منه،
قوله: (والحاكم به أي بالحكم لا بالمعنى المذكور، كما هو ظاهر، بل بمعنى المحكوم به ففيه استخدام ويصح أن يكون الضَّمير على الأمر؛ أي: والحاكم بالأمر المثبت لغيره وهو المحكوم به .
عائداً
His statement: (and as for other than it) corresponds to his statement: (either religious). His statement: (and it is: affirming... etc.) Know that 'ruling' (hukm) has usages; among them: the address of Allah, and among them: the relational proportion (al-nisba al-hukmiyya), like the affirmation of standing for Zayd in 'Zayd is standing' or its negation from him, and the one who judges (al-hakim) by it. And among them: the subject of judgment (al-mahkum 'alayh) like Zayd in the example, and among them: the predicate of judgment (al-mahkum bih) like standing in the example, and among them: affirming a thing (ithbat amr) and that is what is meant here. So his statement: (affirming a thing for a thing) is like affirming standing for Zayd in 'Zayd is standing'. 'Thing'. His statement: (or its negation from him) i.e., from a thing, and the apparent meaning is that the pronoun in 'its negation' refers to the thing restricted by affirmation, and then the definition does not include when negation is made from the outset without prior affirmation, like when you say initially: 'Zayd is not standing.' The answer is that the pronoun refers to the thing without the prior restriction, and it is not of the type of 'I have a dirham and its half', because his statement 'and its half' cannot refer to the previous dirham nor to the absolute dirham that applies to the first as here, but rather the pronoun necessarily refers to another dirham other than the previous one. And 'aw' (or) in the definition is not for doubt, because it does not enter into the definition whether it is a delimitation (rasm) or a definition (hadd), because doubt does not accompany definite conceptualization which is the purpose of definition; rather it is for diversification (tanwi'), and 'aw' for diversification enters into delimitation but not into definition, because its entry into definition would necessitate that the differentia (fasl) is equal to its essence and more specific than it, because the differentia occurring in the definition is necessarily equal to the essence; so if another differentia is mentioned that takes its place, the essence would exist with it, necessitating that the essence be more general, while the assumption is that it is equal to it. From it. His statement: (and the one who judges by it) i.e., by the ruling, not in the mentioned sense, as is apparent, but in the sense of the predicate of judgment (al-mahkum bih), so there is an inclusion (istikhdam), and it is possible that the pronoun refers to the thing; i.e., the one who judges by the thing affirmed for another, and it is the predicate of judgment. Returning.

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on the definition of non-religious ruling (ghayr shar'i) as 'affirming a thing for a thing'. The author first distinguishes various usages of 'hukm': (1) Allah's address, (2) the relational proportion (e.g., 'Zayd is standing' affirms or negates a predicate), (3) the subject of judgment (mahkum 'alayh), (4) the predicate (mahkum bih), and (5) affirming a thing (ithbat amr) – the intended meaning here. The definition is like affirming standing for Zayd. Then a potential problem arises: the phrase 'or its negation' might restrict the definition to negation that follows prior affirmation, excluding initial negation (e.g., 'Zayd is not standing' said without prior affirmation). The answer is that the pronoun in 'its negation' refers to 'thing' without the prior restriction; the analogy of 'I have a dirham and its half' is rejected because that involves a specific reference. Next, the particle 'aw' in the definition is explained: it does not indicate doubt (shakk) because definitions require definite conceptualization, but rather indicates diversification (tanwi'). The author notes that 'aw' for diversification is permissible in delimitations (rusum) but not in strict definitions (hudud), because if a definition had two differentiae, the essence would be both equal and more general – a contradiction. Finally, the phrase 'al-hakim bihi' (the one who judges by it) is clarified: it refers to the predicate (mahkum bih), not the ruling sense previously mentioned, and the pronoun can refer back to 'thing'.

Key Terms
الحكم
إثبات أمر لأمر
نسبة حكمية
محمول
محكوم عليه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The term 'hukm' is often taken to mean only divine ruling (Allah's command).
Correction: In fact, 'hukm' has multiple technical meanings, and here it specifically means affirming a thing for a thing.

Checkpoint
Which usage of 'hukm' is specifically intended in the definition of non-religious rulings?
A) Address of Allah
B) Relational proportion
C) Subject of judgment
D) Affirming a thing

Key Takeaway
The term 'hukm' is polysemous; in the definition of non-religious rulings, it specifically means affirming a thing for a thing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is just' or 'Allah is merciful', are you aware that you are affirming a thing (mercy) for a thing (Allah)? Does this awareness change how you relate to these attributes?
?
The author distinguishes between the subject of judgment (Zayd) and the predicate (standing). In your own judgments about people, do you often confuse who or what you are judging? How can this lesson help you be more fair?
?
If someone asked you what 'hukm' means in the study of kalam, would you be able to give a precise answer like the one in this passage? What does your answer reveal about your commitment to learning technical terms accurately?

The Definition: Affirming a Thing or Its Negation, and the Pronoun Problem
Problem-solution regarding the pronoun in the definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the definition 'affirming a thing for a thing or its negation' applies to both affirmation and negation, and how a potential objection regarding initial negation is resolved.

Core Idea
The definition covers both affirmation and negation, even when negation is stated first.

The previous lesson established that 'hukm' means affirming a thing for a thing. The full definition includes 'or its negation', but this raises a question: does it only cover negation that follows prior affirmation? The author addresses this by clarifying the reference of the pronoun and providing a counterexample from analogical reasoning. This lesson unpacks that subtle issue.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and the objection
Read the passage where the author explains the definition and raises the problem: if the pronoun in 'its negation' refers to the affirmed thing, then initial negation (e.g., 'Zayd is not standing' said first) would be excluded.
The objection is based on the apparent meaning of the pronoun.
2
Examine the answer
The author resolves that the pronoun refers to 'thing' without the restriction of prior affirmation. Understand the reasoning via the dirham analogy: 'I have a dirham and its half'—the pronoun refers to a different dirham, not the same.
This shows that the pronoun is not restricted to the previous affirmation.
3
Appreciate the logical precision
Recognize how this clarification ensures the definition remains comprehensive, covering both affirmation and negation in all cases.

منه،
قوله: (والحاكم به أي بالحكم لا بالمعنى المذكور، كما هو ظاهر، بل بمعنى المحكوم به ففيه استخدام ويصح أن يكون الضَّمير على الأمر؛ أي: والحاكم بالأمر المثبت لغيره وهو المحكوم به .
عائداً
His statement: (and as for other than it) corresponds to his statement: (either religious). His statement: (and it is: affirming... etc.) Know that 'ruling' (hukm) has usages; among them: the address of Allah, and among them: the relational proportion (al-nisba al-hukmiyya), like the affirmation of standing for Zayd in 'Zayd is standing' or its negation from him, and the one who judges (al-hakim) by it. And among them: the subject of judgment (al-mahkum 'alayh) like Zayd in the example, and among them: the predicate of judgment (al-mahkum bih) like standing in the example, and among them: affirming a thing (ithbat amr) and that is what is meant here. So his statement: (affirming a thing for a thing) is like affirming standing for Zayd in 'Zayd is standing'. 'Thing'. His statement: (or its negation from him) i.e., from a thing, and the apparent meaning is that the pronoun in 'its negation' refers to the thing restricted by affirmation, and then the definition does not include when negation is made from the outset without prior affirmation, like when you say initially: 'Zayd is not standing.' The answer is that the pronoun refers to the thing without the prior restriction, and it is not of the type of 'I have a dirham and its half', because his statement 'and its half' cannot refer to the previous dirham nor to the absolute dirham that applies to the first as here, but rather the pronoun necessarily refers to another dirham other than the previous one. And 'aw' (or) in the definition is not for doubt, because it does not enter into the definition whether it is a delimitation (rasm) or a definition (hadd), because doubt does not accompany definite conceptualization which is the purpose of definition; rather it is for diversification (tanwi'), and 'aw' for diversification enters into delimitation but not into definition, because its entry into definition would necessitate that the differentia (fasl) is equal to its essence and more specific than it, because the differentia occurring in the definition is necessarily equal to the essence; so if another differentia is mentioned that takes its place, the essence would exist with it, necessitating that the essence be more general, while the assumption is that it is equal to it. From it. His statement: (and the one who judges by it) i.e., by the ruling, not in the mentioned sense, as is apparent, but in the sense of the predicate of judgment (al-mahkum bih), so there is an inclusion (istikhdam), and it is possible that the pronoun refers to the thing; i.e., the one who judges by the thing affirmed for another, and it is the predicate of judgment. Returning.

The Role of 'Aw' in Definitions and the Meaning of 'Al-Hakim Bihi'
Explanation of the particle 'aw' and clarification of a term.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why 'aw' (or) in the definition indicates diversification, not doubt, and how it is permissible in delimitations (rusum) but not in strict definitions (hudud). You will also grasp the clarification of 'al-hakim bihi' as the predicate.

Core Idea
The 'aw' in the definition is for diversification, not doubt.

Having established the definition's coverage, the author turns to a linguistic nuance: the particle 'aw' (or) is often misunderstood. In this context, it does not indicate uncertainty but rather presents two alternatives. Furthermore, the author explains that such 'aw' is acceptable in delimitations (rasm) but not in strict definitions (hadd) due to a logical argument. Finally, the phrase 'al-hakim bihi' is clarified.

Guided Path
1
Read the explanation of 'aw'
Read the passage where the author states that 'aw' is not for doubt but for diversification, and the reason why doubt is incompatible with definition.
Definitions aim for definite conceptualization.
2
Distinguish between rasm and hadd
Understand the argument why 'aw' for diversification enters into rasm (delimitation) but not into hadd (definition proper), due to the issue of the differentia (fasl).
If hadd had two differentiae, the essence would be both equal and more general – a contradiction.
3
Understand 'al-hakim bihi'
Read the final clarification that 'al-hakim bihi' refers to the predicate (mahkum bih) and the two possible interpretations of the pronoun.
The pronoun can refer to the ruling or to the thing.

Definition of Intellect and Moral Responsibility
Definition and attributes of the intellect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Kalam defines the intellect as a spiritual secret and how its development from the breathing of the spirit to puberty establishes moral responsibility.

Core Idea
The intellect is a spiritual secret that grows from breath of spirit to perfection at puberty.

This lesson introduces the definition of the intellect according to the Sunni tradition. You will learn its essential attributes, including its locus in the heart, its beginning at the breathing of the spirit, and its completion at puberty, which triggers moral responsibility. The passage also presents the correct view held by Imam Malik and Imam al-Shafi'i, and mentions an alternative opinion.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and attributes
Read the Arabic passage focusing on the definition of intellect as a 'spiritual secret' and the subsequent attributes: its locus, light, beginning, and first perfection. Note the logical flow from these attributes to moral responsibility.
The definition is comprehensive, covering both necessary and theoretical sciences.
2
Identify the correct doctrine
Recognize that the passage asserts this as the correct view held by Malik and al-Shafi'i. Understand that this view is the basis for the statement that the intellect is a 'divine subtlety'.
3
Reflect on the alternative view
Notice that the passage briefly mentions an alternative: 'it is a faculty of the soul'. Compare this with the primary definition.
This alternative relates to the rational soul, which will be clarified in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والعقلُ : سرّ روحانيّ تُدرِك به النَّفْسُ العلوم الضَّروريَّة والنَّظريَّة، ومحله القلب، ونوره في الدماغ، وابتداؤه من حين نفخ الروح في الجنين، وأوَّلُ كماله البلوغ، ولذا كان التكليف بالبلوغ، هذا هو الصَّحيح الذي عليه مالك والشافعي لها، وهو مراد من قال: هو لطيفة ربانية تدرك به النفس ... إلخ . وقيل : هو قوَّة للنفس .... قوله : (سرِّ (روحاني) : أي : من قبيل الأرواح التي هي أجسام لطيفة جوهرية لا عرضيَّة، كما هو الحقُّ الذي تدلُّ عليه الأخبار الصّحيحة؛ من أنَّ الأرواح أجسام لطيفة تبقى بعد فناء جسدها، وتذهب وتجيء؛ فإمَّا في عليين، وإما في سجين، ومعنى كون العقل من قبيل الأرواح أنَّه من الأمور الملكوتية. قوله : ( ومحله (القلب) : أي : ولا استحالة في حلول جوهر في جوهر، إذا كانا لطيفين أو أحدهما ، والمراد بالقلب هنا اللّحمة الصَّنوبريَّة الشكل، ويطلق أيضاً على نفس العقل، كما في قوله تعالى : لِمَن كَانَ لَهُ قَلْبُ ) (١). قوله : (هذا هو الصَّحيح اسم الإشارة عائد على جميع ما قبله؛ من جوهر، وأنَّ محلَّه القلب، ومن أنَّ ابتداءه من نفخ الرُّوح فيه، ومن أنَّ أنَّه أوّل كماله البلوغ. قوله: (وقيل: هو قوَّة للنَّفس ) : هو معنى قولهم : النَّفس الناطقة ؛ أي: المتفكّرة بالقوة.
And the intellect is: a spiritual secret by which the soul perceives necessary and theoretical sciences. Its locus is the heart, its light is in the brain, its beginning is from the time the spirit is breathed into the fetus, and its first perfection is puberty. Therefore, moral responsibility is contingent upon puberty. This is the correct view held by Malik and al-Shafi'i, and it is what is meant by one who said: 'It is a divine subtlety by which the soul perceives... etc.' And it is said: 'It is a faculty of the soul...' His saying: 'a spiritual secret' means: it belongs to the category of spirits, which are subtle, substantial bodies, not accidents, as is the truth indicated by authentic reports: that spirits are subtle bodies that remain after the destruction of their bodies, and go and come; either in 'Illiyin or in Sijjin. The meaning of the intellect belonging to the category of spirits is that it is among the angelic matters. His saying: 'its locus is the heart' means: there is no impossibility in the indwelling of a substance in a substance if both are subtle or one of them is. What is meant by 'heart' here is the pineal flesh, and it is also used to refer to the intellect itself, as in the Almighty's saying: 'for indeed he has a heart' (Qaf 37). His saying: 'This is the correct view' – the demonstrative pronoun refers to all that preceded: that it is a substance, that its locus is the heart, that its beginning is from the breathing of the spirit into it, and that its first perfection is puberty. His saying: 'and it is said: it is a faculty of the soul' means the meaning of their saying: 'the rational soul', i.e., the potentially thinking one.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage defines the intellect (al-'aql) and clarifies its nature according to the correct Sunni doctrine. The definition states that the intellect is a 'spiritual secret' (sirr ruhani) by which the soul perceives both necessary and theoretical sciences. Its locus is the heart (al-qalb), its light is in the brain, it begins when the spirit is breathed into the fetus, and its first perfection is attained at puberty, when moral responsibility (taklif) begins. The commentator affirms that this is the correct view held by Imams Malik and al-Shafi'i, and it is what is meant by those who describe it as a 'divine subtlety' (latifa rabbaniyya). An alternative view is mentioned, that the intellect is a 'faculty of the soul' (quwwa li-l-nafs). The commentary then explains each part: 'spiritual secret' means it belongs to the category of spirits (arwah), which are subtle, substantial bodies (ajsam latifa jawhariyya), not accidents, as proven by authentic hadith about the afterlife. The intellect's being from this category makes it part of the angelic realm. Regarding its locus, the heart, the commentator explains that there is no impossibility for one substance to dwell in another when both are subtle, and the 'heart' here refers to the pineal flesh (al-lahma al-sanubariyya), though the term can also denote the intellect itself, as in the Quranic verse. The demonstrative 'this' in 'this is the correct view' refers back to all the attributes mentioned: its substantial nature, its locus in the heart, its beginning at the breathing of the spirit, and its perfection at puberty. Finally, the alternative view that it is a 'faculty' is interpreted as the meaning of the 'rational soul' (al-nafs al-natiqa), i.e., the soul in potentiality for thinking.

سر روحاني
قلب
تكليف
بلوغ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the correct view in this passage, when does the intellect attain its first perfection?
A) At birth
B) At the breathing of the spirit
C) At puberty
D) At adulthood

Key Takeaway
The intellect is a spiritual secret that begins at the breathing of the spirit and reaches its first perfection at puberty, making the individual morally responsible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your intellect is a divine subtlety given by Allah. Have you used it today to seek knowledge of Him, or have you wasted it on distractions? Commit to one action where you will employ your intellect to know your Lord before the day ends.
?
Moral responsibility began at your puberty. When you stand before Allah, will you be able to say you fulfilled the trust of your intellect? What is one neglected obligation you will resume starting today?
?
The intellect's light is in the brain, but its locus is the heart. Is your heart attached to this world, blocking that light? What worldly attachment will you cut off so the light of your intellect can guide you to truth?

The Nature of Intellect: A Spiritual Substance
Explanation of the intellect as a spiritual substance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that in Sunni doctrine the intellect is a subtle, substantial body from the angelic realm, located in the heart, and distinct from the rational soul.

Core Idea
The intellect is a spiritual substance, not an accident, and its locus is the heart.

This lesson dives into the theological explanation of the intellect's nature. It clarifies that the 'spiritual secret' refers to a category of spirits that are subtle, substantial bodies, not accidents. It also explains the meaning of the intellect's locus being the heart, and reconciles the alternative view of the intellect as a faculty with the concept of the rational soul.

Guided Path
1
Understand 'spiritual secret' as substantial body
Read the explanation of 'spiritual secret' carefully. Notice that it asserts the intellect is from the category of spirits (arwah), which are subtle, substantial bodies (ajsam latifa jawhariyya), not accidents. This is supported by authentic hadith about the afterlife.
This is a key point against those who consider the intellect an accident.
2
Grasp the locus in the heart
Study the commentary on the locus: the heart (al-qalb) refers to the pineal flesh (al-lahma al-sanubariyya). The passage also explains that one substance can dwell in another if both are subtle, and the term 'heart' can also denote the intellect itself.
3
Integrate the alternative view
Examine the last part: the alternative view that the intellect is a 'faculty of the soul' is interpreted as the 'rational soul' (al-nafs al-natiqa) in potentiality. This harmonizes with the main definition.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the intellect is an accident (a property) rather than a substance.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the intellect is a subtle, substantial body (jawhar latif), not an accident. It belongs to the category of spirits, which are bodies that persist after death.

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, what does 'spiritual secret' (sirr ruhani) mean in terms of the intellect's nature?
A) It is an accident that resides in the soul
B) It is a subtle, substantial body from the category of spirits
C) It is the rational soul itself
D) It is the light in the brain

Key Takeaway
The intellect is a spiritual substance, a subtle body from the angelic realm, subsisting in the heart, and distinct from the rational soul in potentiality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that your intellect is a spiritual substance that will survive your body. Does this change how you value your own mind? What would it mean for you to treat your intellect as something eternal, not just a tool for daily tasks? Will you nurture it with knowledge that lasts beyond this life?
?
If your intellect subsists in your heart, how should you guard what enters your heart? What thoughts, images, and beliefs are you allowing to settle there that might corrupt this spiritual gift? What will you do this week to purify the place where your intellect dwells?
?
The passage says the intellect is from the angelic realm. Do you live as if your intellect connects you to the unseen world? Or do you reduce it to calculations and opinions? What would change if you began each day by acknowledging that your intellect is a loan from the angelic realm, to be used for divine purpose?

What Is Kalam?
intermediate
8 min
The Intellect: Definition and Interpretations
Al-Sawi defines 'aql, presents the correct view, and lists alternative definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct definition of 'aql according to Malik and al-Shafi'i, its nature as a spiritual substance, and the alternative scholarly opinions.

Core Idea
The intellect is a spiritual substance in the heart, not merely a faculty or knowledge.

Before we can discuss theological arguments, we need a clear understanding of the instrument of reasoning—the intellect ('aql). Al-Sawi presents the definition agreed upon by Malik and al-Shafi'i, explaining its spiritual nature, location in the heart, and connection to legal obligation. He then contrasts this with other definitions, showing how different scholars conceptualized the intellect.

Guided Path
1
Read the main definition
Start by reading the first few lines of the Arabic passage where Al-Sawi defines 'aql as a spiritual secret (sirr rūḥānī). Notice the key elements: what it perceives, its locus, its light, its beginning, and its perfection.
This is the correct view according to the text.
2
Study the commentary on locus and substance
Al-Sawi elaborates that the intellect is a subtle substance (jawhar), not an accident, and its locus is the heart (the pineal-shaped flesh). He defends the possibility of one substance indwelling another.
This addresses a potential philosophical objection.
3
Examine the alternative definitions
After the main definition, Al-Sawi presents two other opinions: that 'aql is a power of the soul, and that it is some of the necessary sciences (knowledge of wājib, mustaḥīl, jā'iz). Read these and note how they differ from the first.
4
Understand the critique of the 'sciences' view
Al-Sawi explains why 'aql cannot be all necessary sciences, because that would exclude some rational beings. He clarifies that the intended knowledge is a basic recognition of necessity, impossibility, and possibility.
This shows the precision required in defining key terms.

تعريف العقل:
وَالْعَقْلُ : سِرٌّ رُوحَانِيٌّ تُدْرِكُ بِهِ النَّفْسُ الْعُلُومَ الضَّرُورِيَّةَ وَالنَّظَرِيَّةَ، وَمَحَلُّهُ الْقَلْبُ، وَنُورُهُ فِي الدِّمَاغِ، وَابْتِدَاؤُهُ مِنْ حِينَ نَفْخِ الرُّوحِ فِي الْجَنِينِ، وَأَوَّلُ كَمَالِهِ الْبُلُوغُ وَلِذَا كَانَ التَّكْلِيفُ بِالْبُلُوغِ، هَذَا هُوَ الصَّحِيحُ الَّذِي عَلَيْهِ مَالِكٌ وَالشَّافِعِيُّ لَهَا، وَهُوَ مُرَادُ مَنْ قَالَ: هُوَ لَطِيفَةٌ رَبَّانِيَّةٌ تُدْرِكُ بِهَا النَّفْسُ ... إِلَخْ. وَقِيلَ: هُوَ قُوَّةٌ لِلنَّفْسِ. ...
قَوْلُهُ: (سِرِّ رُوحَانِيٍّ) أَيْ: مِنْ قَبِيلِ الْأَرْوَاحِ الَّتِي هِيَ أَجْسَامٌ لَطِيفَةٌ جَوْهَرِيَّةٌ لَا عَرَضِيَّةٌ، كَمَا هُوَ الْحَقُّ الَّذِي تَدُلُّ عَلَيْهِ الْأَخْبَارُ الصَّحِيحَةُ؛ مِنْ أَنَّ الْأَرْوَاحَ أَجْسَامٌ لَطِيفَةٌ تَبْقَى بَعْدَ فَنَاءِ جَسَدِهَا، وَتَذْهَبُ وَتَجِيءُ؛ فَإِمَّا فِي عِلِّيِّينَ، وَإِمَّا فِي سِجِّينٍ، وَمَعْنَى كَوْنِ الْعَقْلِ مِنْ قَبِيلِ الْأَرْوَاحِ أَنَّهُ مِنَ الْأُمُورِ الْمَلَكُوتِيَّةِ. قَوْلُهُ: (وَمَحَلُّهُ الْقَلْبُ) أَيْ: وَلَا اسْتِحَالَةَ فِي حُلُولِ جَوْهَرٍ فِي جَوْهَرٍ، إِذَا كَانَا لَطِيفَيْنِ أَوْ أَحَدُهُمَا، وَالْمُرَادُ بِالْقَلْبِ هُنَا اللَّحْمَةُ الصَّنَوْبَرِيَّةُ الشَّكْلِ، وَيُطْلَقُ أَيْضًا عَلَى نَفْسِ الْعَقْلِ، كَمَا فِي قَوْلِهِ تَعَالَى: لِمَنْ كَانَ لَهُ قَلْبُ (سورة ق: ٣٧). قَوْلُهُ: (هَذَا هُوَ الصَّحِيحُ) اسْمُ الْإِشَارَةِ عَائِدٌ عَلَى جَمِيعِ مَا قَبْلَهُ؛ مِنْ جَوْهَرٍ، وَأَنَّ مَحَلَّهُ الْقَلْبُ، وَمِنْ أَنَّ ابْتِدَاءَهُ مِنْ نَفْخِ الرُّوحِ فِيهِ، وَمِنْ أَنَّ أَوَّلَ كَمَالِهِ الْبُلُوغُ. قَوْلُهُ: (وَقِيلَ: هُوَ قُوَّةٌ لِلنَّفْسِ) هُوَ مَعْنَى قَوْلِهِمُ: النَّفْسُ النَّاطِقَةُ؛ أَيِ الْمُفَكِّرَةُ بِالْقُوَّةِ، مُعَدَّةٌ لِاكْتِسَابِ الْآرَاءِ؛ أَيِ الِاعْتِقَادَاتِ. وَقِيلَ: هُوَ مِنْ قَبِيلِ الْعُلُومِ، قَالَ الْقَاضِي: هُوَ بَعْضُ الْعُلُومِ الضَّرُورِيَّةِ، وَهُوَ الْعِلْمُ بِوُجُوبِ الْوَاجِبَاتِ، وَاسْتِحَالَةِ الْمُسْتَحِيلَاتِ، وَجَوَازِ الْجَائِزَاتِ...
قَوْلُهُ: (مُعَدَّةٌ) اسْمُ مَفْعُولٍ؛ أَيْ مُهَيَّأَةٌ. قَوْلُهُ: (أَيِ الِاعْتِقَادَاتِ) أَيِ الْمَسَائِلُ الَّتِي شَأْنُهَا أَنْ تُعْتَقَدَ. قَوْلُهُ: (وَقِيلَ: هُوَ مِنْ قَبِيلِ الْعُلُومِ) أَيْ: بِدَلِيلِ أَنَّ الْحَيَوَانَ الَّذِي لَا عِلْمَ عِنْدَهُ؛ كَالْفَرَسِ وَالْحِمَارِ لَا عَقْلَ عِنْدَهُ. قَوْلُهُ: (هُوَ بَعْضُ الْعُلُومِ الضَّرُورِيَّةِ) أَيْ: لَا كُلُّهَا؛ لِأَنَّ الْعُلُومَ الضَّرُورِيَّةَ كَثِيرَةٌ مُنْتَشِرَةٌ فِي سَائِرِ الْعُقَلَاءِ فِي جَمِيعِ الْأَمْكِنَةِ، وَمِنَ الْمَعْلُومِ أَنَّ هُنَاكَ عُلُومًا ضَرُورِيَّةً عِنْدَ بَعْضِ الْعُقَلَاءِ دُونَ بَعْضٍ، فَلَوْ أُرِيدَ جَمِيعُ الضَّرُورِيَّاتِ؛ لَلَزِمَ أَنَّ بَعْضَ الْعُقَلَاءِ الَّذِي لَمْ يَعْرِفْ بَعْضَهَا لَيْسَ بِعَاقِلٍ، وَلَيْسَ كَذَلِكَ. قَوْلُهُ: (وَهُوَ الْعِلْمُ بِوُجُوبِ الْوَاجِبَاتِ... إِلَخْ): وَالْمُرَادُ الْعِلْمُ بِأَنَّ هُنَاكَ أُمُورًا لَا بُدَّ مِنْهَا وَلَا انْفِكَاكَ عَنْهَا، وَبِأَنَّ هُنَاكَ أُمُورًا أُخَرَ لَا تَتَأَتَّى وَلَا تَقَعُ، وَأَنَّ هُنَاكَ أُمُورًا يَصِحُّ وُقُوعُهَا وَعَدَمُ وُقُوعِهَا، وَإِلَّا خَرَجَ كَثِيرٌ مِنَ النَّاسِ الَّذِينَ لَا يَعْرِفُونَ حَقِيقَةَ الْوَاجِبِ وَالْمُسْتَحِيلِ وَالْجَائِزِ عَنْ كَوْنِهِمْ عُقَلَاءَ، وَلَا قَائِلَ بِهِ. (وَهْوَ الْعَلَّامَةُ الْمُتَكَلِّمُ الْأُصُولِيُّ أَبُو بَكْرٍ مُحَمَّدُ بْنُ الطَّيِّبِ الْبَاقِلَّانِيُّ ت ٤٠٣)
Definition of the intellect: The intellect is a spiritual secret by which the soul perceives necessary and theoretical sciences. Its locus is the heart, its light is in the brain, its beginning is from the time the spirit is blown into the fetus, and its first perfection is puberty, and for this reason obligation (taklīf) is based on puberty. This is the correct view according to Malik and al-Shafi‘i, and it is what is meant by whoever said: 'It is a lordly subtlety by which the soul perceives… etc.' And it was said: it is a power of the soul. … His statement: (a spiritual secret) meaning: of the kind of spirits which are subtle, substantial bodies, not accidental, as is the truth indicated by sound reports: that spirits are subtle bodies that remain after the annihilation of their body, and go and come; either in ‘Illiyyīn or in Sijjīn. The meaning of the intellect being of the kind of spirits is that it is among the angelic [malakūtī] matters. His statement: (its locus is the heart) meaning: there is no impossibility in the indwelling of a substance in a substance, if both are subtle or one of them. What is meant by 'heart' here is the pineal-shaped flesh, and it is also applied to the intellect itself, as in His statement: 'for him who has a heart' (Qaf: 37). His statement: (this is the correct one) – the demonstrative pronoun refers to all that preceded: that it is a substance, that its locus is the heart, that its beginning is from the blowing of the spirit into it, and that its first perfection is puberty. His statement: (and it was said: it is a power of the soul) – this is the meaning of their statement: 'the rational soul', i.e., the potentially thinking one, prepared for acquiring opinions, i.e., beliefs. And it was said: it is of the kind of sciences; al-Qadi [al-Baqillani] said: it is some of the necessary sciences, and it is the knowledge of the necessity of the necessary [obligatory] things, the impossibility of the impossible things, and the permissibility of the possible things… His statement: (prepared) is a passive participle; i.e., readied. His statement: (i.e., beliefs) i.e., the matters that are apt to be believed. His statement: (and it was said: it is of the kind of sciences) i.e., by evidence that an animal which has no knowledge, like the horse and the donkey, has no intellect. His statement: (it is some of the necessary sciences) i.e., not all of them; because the necessary sciences are many and widespread among all rational beings in all places, and it is known that there are necessary sciences present in some rational beings but not others. So if all necessary sciences were intended, it would necessitate that some rational beings who do not know some of them are not rational, which is not the case. His statement: (and it is the knowledge of the necessity of the necessary things… etc.): what is meant is the knowledge that there are matters that are indispensable and inseparable, and that there are other matters that cannot be brought about and do not occur, and that there are matters whose occurrence and non-occurrence are both valid. Otherwise, many people who do not know the reality of the necessary, impossible, and possible would be excluded from being rational, and no one holds that. (He is the scholar, theologian, and legal theorist Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Tayyib al-Baqillani, d. 403).
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage from Hashiyat al-Sawi provides a comprehensive definition of 'aql (intellect/reason) and discusses different scholarly opinions on its nature. The author begins by presenting the primary definition: that 'aql is a 'spiritual secret' (sirr rūḥānī) by which the soul acquires both necessary and theoretical knowledge. He then elaborates on its locus (the heart), its light (in the brain), its beginning (at the blowing of the spirit into the fetus), and its perfection (at puberty, which is the basis for legal obligation). Al-Sawi affirms this as the correct view according to Imam Malik and al-Shafi‘i, noting that it aligns with the expression 'lordly subtlety' (laṭīfa rabbāniyya). He then explains each element of the definition in his commentary. The term 'spiritual secret' is clarified: it belongs to the category of spirits, which are subtle, substantial bodies (ajsām laṭīfa jawhariyya), not accidents. This is supported by sound reports that spirits remain after death and travel. The heart is identified as the locus, and al-Sawi addresses the philosophical issue of one substance indwelling another, deeming it possible when both or one is subtle. The heart is defined as the pineal-shaped flesh, though the term can also refer to the intellect itself, as in the Qur'anic verse 'for him who has a heart' (Qaf:37). Al-Sawi then presents alternative definitions. The first alternative (qīl) is that 'aql is a power (quwwa) of the soul, equivalent to the 'rational soul' (al-nafs al-nāṭiqa) which is potentially thinking and prepared for acquiring beliefs. Another opinion is that 'aql belongs to the category of sciences, with a specific claim by al-Qadi al-Baqillani that it is some of the necessary sciences, namely the knowledge of the necessity of the necessary (wājib), impossibility of the impossible (mustaḥīl), and permissibility of the possible (jā'iz). Al-Sawi explains that this cannot mean all necessary sciences, as that would exclude some rational beings who lack certain necessary knowledge. He also clarifies that the knowledge intended is not detailed technical knowledge but an innate recognition that there are things that are necessary, impossible, and possible – otherwise many people would not be considered rational. The passage concludes with a footnote identifying al-Qadi as Abu Bakr al-Baqillani, the renowned Ash‘ari theologian and legal theorist.

سر روحاني
قلب
قوة للنفس
نفس ناطقة
علوم ضرورية
واجب
مستحيل
جائز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the intellect is merely a faculty or an accident, or that it is identical to all necessary knowledge.
Correction: The correct view according to this passage is that the intellect is a spiritual substance (jawhar) placed in the heart, not a mere accident. Al-Baqillani's view that it is some necessary sciences is not all-inclusive; it refers to the foundational knowledge of necessity, impossibility, and possibility.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the correct definition of 'aql according to Malik and al-Shafi‘i?
A) A power of the soul prepared for acquiring opinions.
B) A spiritual secret by which the soul perceives necessary and theoretical sciences.
C) Some of the necessary sciences, namely knowledge of wajib, mustahil, and ja'iz.
D) The rational soul that thinks potentially.

Key Takeaway
The intellect ('aql) is defined by Al-Sawi as a spiritual substance in the heart, distinct from mere faculties or knowledge, and this definition is foundational for understanding Islamic theology and legal obligation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your intellect is a spiritual substance placed in your heart by Allah, and its perfection is measured by how you use it to know Him and fulfill His commands, then what does your daily intellectual activity—your thoughts, studies, conversations—reveal about the state of that spiritual substance? Are you feeding it with what is true and beneficial, or are you dulling it with neglect and sin? Commit today to one specific change in how you use your intellect for Allah's sake.
?
Al-Sawi tells us that the intellect begins at the blowing of the spirit into the fetus, but its first perfection is at puberty, when you become accountable. Think back to your own puberty: Did you approach that age with any awareness that your intellect was now fully accountable before Allah? Did you realize that your reasoning could either bring you closer to Him or lead you astray? If you could speak to your younger self, what would you say about the gift of 'aql and its responsibility?
?
The correct view of the intellect, according to this lesson, is that it is a spiritual secret in the heart, not merely a faculty for reasoning. Yet many of us live as if the intellect were just a tool for worldly success. When you consider that your intellect is a divine trust (amānah) that perceives necessary truths about Allah, the afterlife, and moral obligation, what is one way you have been treating it as ordinary rather than sacred? How will you honor it from today?

Al-Sawi on 'Aql as a Faculty and Science
Al-Sawi glosses 'aql as a faculty and proves it is a type of science

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Sawi defines 'aql as a faculty and argues that it is a type of science based on the absence of intellect in animals without knowledge.

Core Idea
Intellect is not just a faculty—it is a form of knowledge.

In this short gloss, Al-Sawi unpacks key terms from the base text to clarify the nature of intellect ('aql). He begins by analyzing the word 'prepared' (معدة) as a passive participle, then defines 'beliefs' as matters that are to be believed, and finally presents the argument that intellect belongs to the category of sciences, proved by the fact that animals without knowledge lack intellect. This lesson will walk through each explanation and the logical proof.

Guided Path
1
Parsing the passive participle
Notice how Al-Sawi explains the word 'معدة' as a passive participle meaning 'prepared'. This grammatical point sets the stage for understanding intellect as a prepared faculty.
2
Defining beliefs
Al-Sawi explains that 'اعتقادات' refers to matters that are meant to be believed. This clarifies the object of intellectual knowledge.
3
The proof that intellect is a science
The passage presents a key argument: animals without knowledge (like horses and donkeys) have no intellect. This shows that intellect requires knowledge, placing it in the category of sciences.
Consider why this proof is considered decisive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (معدة) اسم مفعول ؛ أي مهيَّأة. قوله : (أي: الاعتقادات) : أي : المسائل التي شأنها أن تعتقد. قوله : (وقيل: وهو من قبيل العلوم) أي: بدليل أنَّ الحيوان الذي لا علم عنده ؛ كالفرس والحمار لا عقل عنده.
His statement: (معدة) is a passive participle; meaning prepared. His statement: (i.e. beliefs): i.e. the matters that are meant to be believed. His statement: (And it is said: it is of the category of sciences) i.e. by the proof that an animal that has no knowledge, like a horse and a donkey, has no intellect.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage is a gloss explaining terms from the base text. It begins by parsing the word 'معدة' as a passive participle meaning 'prepared'. It then explains 'اعتقادات' as matters that are to be believed. Finally, it presents the view that 'aql (intellect) belongs to the category of sciences, and provides a proof: an animal without knowledge, such as a horse or donkey, has no intellect. This shows that knowledge is a necessary condition for intellect.

Key Terms
معدة
عقل
الاعتقادات
العلوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that 'aql (intellect) is merely a faculty or capacity independent of knowledge.
Correction: Al-Sawi shows that intellect requires knowledge, because animals without knowledge have no intellect. Thus intellect is a type of science, not just a blank faculty.

Checkpoint
What is the proof that 'aql is a type of science?
A) Animals have intellect but no knowledge.
B) Animals without knowledge have no intellect.
C) Knowledge is unnecessary for intellect.
D) All animals have knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Intellect is not merely a faculty; it is a form of knowledge, as proven by the fact that beings without knowledge lack intellect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you act ignorantly, are you truly using your intellect? The passage shows that intellect depends on knowledge. What area of your life are you making decisions in while remaining deliberately ignorant, and how does that diminish your intellect before Allah?
?
The horse and donkey have no intellect because they have no knowledge. You have been given the capacity for knowledge. Are you feeding your intellect with beneficial knowledge or leaving it starved? What will you do today to acquire knowledge that strengthens your intellect for the sake of your deen?
?
If intellect is a form of knowledge, then every gap in your knowledge is a gap in your intellect. Are you content to remain intellectually lacking in matters of Aqeedah? What one thing will you commit to learning this week to increase your 'aql?

Why 'Aql is Only Some Necessary Sciences
Al-Sawi explains why 'aql is only some of the necessary sciences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why 'aql (reason) refers to some necessary sciences rather than all of them, and why this distinction is important in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
'Aql is not a complete encyclopedia of necessary knowledge, but a subset shared by all rational beings.

In defining 'aql (reason), early Muslim theologians distinguished between the necessary sciences that are universal and those that are particular to certain individuals. Al-Sawi clarifies that 'aql cannot mean all necessary sciences, because that would exclude many rational people. This passage unpacks the logical reasoning behind that distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise logical structure.
2
Identify the key claim
Find the statement that 'aql is 'some necessary sciences, not all'. Understand what is being negated.
3
Follow the argument
Trace why all necessary sciences cannot be intended: because they are spread among rational beings and not everyone knows every necessary science.
Pay attention to the hypothetical consequence.
4
Connect to theology
Think about why this matters for defining human reason. It preserves the rationality of those who may not know every necessary truth.
This shows the precision of kalam terminology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (هو بعض العلوم الضَّروريَّة) : أي : لا كلُّها؛ لأنَّ العلوم الضَّروريّة كثيرة منتشرة في سائر العقلاء في جميع الأمكنة، ومن المعلوم أنَّ هناك علوماً ضروريَّة عند بعض العقلاء دون بعض، فلو أريد جميع الضَّروريَّات؛ للزم أنَّ بعض العقلاء الذي لم يعرف بعضها ليس بعاقل، وليس كذلك.
His statement: (It is some of the necessary sciences): i.e. not all of them; because the necessary sciences are many, spread among all rational beings in all places, and it is known that there are necessary sciences with some rational beings that are not with others. So if all necessary sciences were intended, it would necessitate that some rational beings who do not know some of them are not rational, and that is not the case.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage explains a previous statement that 'aql is some of the necessary sciences. It clarifies that 'aql is not all necessary sciences, because necessary sciences are numerous and widely distributed among rational beings, and not every rational being knows every necessary science. If 'aql required knowledge of all necessary sciences, then any rational being lacking some would be considered non-rational, which is false. Therefore 'aql is only some necessary sciences.

Key Terms
العلوم الضَّروريَّة
العقلاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: 'Aql requires knowledge of all necessary sciences.
Correction: The passage shows that 'aql is only some necessary sciences, because if it were all, then rational beings who lack some would be considered irrational, which is false.

Checkpoint
Why is 'aql not all necessary sciences?
A) Because necessary sciences are few.
B) Because some rational beings lack some necessary sciences.
C) Because all rational beings know all necessary sciences.
D) Because necessary sciences are not important.

Key Takeaway
This passage shows that 'aql refers only to a subset of necessary sciences, preserving the rationality of those who may not know every necessary truth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a necessary truth you know that others do not (e.g., a mathematical fact). Does that make you more rational than them? How does Al-Sawi's argument correct any arrogance in your heart?
?
If 'aql is only some necessary sciences, what does that mean for how you view someone who lacks a certain piece of knowledge you consider obvious? Are you tempted to consider them less rational? What does this passage demand from you?
?
Reflect on a time you assumed someone was irrational because they didn't share a belief you considered necessary. How does Al-Sawi's distinction between 'some' and 'all' necessary sciences challenge your assumption? What will you change in your interactions from now?

The Obligation of Knowing Allah: By Revelation, Not Reason
Al-Sawi explains the ruling on knowing Allah and refutes the Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the obligation to know Allah is derived from Sharia, not from reason alone, and how Al-Sawi refutes the Mu'tazila position.

Core Idea
Knowing Allah is a duty from Allah, not from our own minds.

This lesson dives into a key Ash'ari doctrine: that the obligation to know Allah comes from divine revelation, not unaided reason. Al-Sawi clarifies the text's grammar and then confronts the Mu'tazila, who argue that reason alone makes this knowledge obligatory. Understanding this distinction is crucial for appreciating the role of revelation in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the Source Passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how Al-Sawi begins with a grammatical explanation before moving to the doctrinal refutation.
The grammatical discussion is not just technical; it reinforces the doctrinal point.
2
Understand the Core Doctrine
Identify the main claim: knowing Allah is obligatory by Sharia. Contrast this with the Mu'tazila view that it is obligatory by reason.
The phrase 'waajib shar'an' is key.
3
Examine the Grammatical Analysis
Al-Sawi explains the parsing of 'shar'an' as an absolute object or specification. This shows the precision of scholarly language in conveying doctrine.
Don't get bogged down in grammar; focus on how it supports the meaning.
4
Analyze the Refutation of the Mu'tazila
Al-Sawi divides the Mu'tazila into two groups: those who say reason is sufficient and messengers are mere confirmers (sinners), and those who deem messengers unnecessary (disbelievers). See how he classifies them.
This refutation shows the consequences of denying revelation's necessity.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان حكم معرفة الله تعالى. وواجبٌ شَرْعاً أي: وجوب شرع، فحذف المضاف وأقيم المضاف إليه مقامه فانتصب انتصابه، فهو منصوب على أنَّه مفعول مطلق؛ أي: وجوباً مستفاداً من الشَّرع؛ أي: الشَّارع؛ يعني: أنَّه يجب وجوباً شرعيّاً خلافاً للمعتزلة القائلين: إنَّ معرفة الله تعالى واجبة بالعقل. قوله: (وواجب): الأحسن أنَّه خبر مقدَّم، و(معرفة) مبتدأ مؤخّر، ويصح إعرابه مبتدأ، و(معرفة) فاعل سدَّ مسدَّ الخبر، بناء على مذهب من لا يشترط اعتماد الوصف. قوله: (مقامه): بضم الميم؛ لأنَّه من أقام الرباعي، وأما إن كان مصدر الثلاثي؛ فيقال: بفتح الميم، يقال: قام زيد مقام عمرو. قوله: (على أنَّه مفعول مطلق): ويصح أن يكون منصوباً على التمييز؛ أي: من جهة الشَّرع، ولا يصح نصبه على نزع الخافض؛ لأنَّه سماعي. قوله: (أي: الشَّارع): أشار بذلك إلى أنَّه من باب زيد عدل، والمراد بالشارع الله حقيقة، والنَّبيُّ مجازاً. قوله: (خلافاً للمعتزلة ... إلخ) أي: وهم في ذلك فرقتان؛ فرقة تقول: معرفة الله واجبة بالعقل، والرُّسل مؤكّدون للعقل، وهؤلاء فسّاق، وفرقة تقول: لا يحتاج للرُّسل، فإرسالهم عبث، وهؤلاء كفَّار.
Explanation of the ruling on knowing Allah. It is obligatory by Sharia, i.e., an obligation from the Sharia. So the annexed (word) is omitted and the annexing (word) takes its place and receives its inflection, thus it is in the accusative as an absolute object; i.e., an obligation derived from the Law; i.e., the Lawgiver; meaning that it is obligatory by a legal obligation, contrary to the Mu'tazila who say that knowledge of Allah is obligatory by reason. His statement: (waajib) – the best is that it is a predicate in front, and (ma'rifa) is a delayed subject, and it is also valid to parse it as a subject with (ma'rifa) as a predicate that suffices as the predicate, according to the position of those who do not require the descriptor to be dependent. His statement: (maqaamihi) – with a damma on the meem because it is from the quadrilateral verb 'aqaama; as for if it is the masdar of the triliteral, then it is said with a fatha on the meem: one says 'qaama Zaydun maqaama Amrin'. His statement: (as an absolute object) – it is also valid for it to be in the accusative as a specification; i.e., from the aspect of the Sharia, and it is not correct for it to be in the accusative by removing the preposition because that is based on hearsay. His statement: (i.e., the Lawgiver) – he indicated thereby that it is of the category of 'Zaydun 'adlun', and what is meant by 'Lawgiver' is Allah in reality, and the Prophet metaphorically. His statement: (contrary to the Mu'tazila ... etc.) – i.e., they are in that two sects: a sect that says knowledge of Allah is obligatory by reason and the messengers only confirm reason – these are sinners; and a sect that says there is no need for messengers, so sending them is futile – these are disbelievers.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling concerning knowledge of Allah (ma'rifat Allah). It begins by clarifying that the statement 'waajib shar'an' (obligatory by Sharia) means that this obligation is derived from the revealed Law, not from reason alone. The author then explains the grammatical construction of 'waajib' in the text, noting that it functions as a predicate with 'ma'rifa' as subject, and discusses the accusative case of 'shar'an' as an absolute object or specification. He clarifies that 'al-Shari' (the Lawgiver) refers to Allah in reality and to the Prophet metaphorically.

The main doctrinal point is the contrast with the Mu'tazila, who hold that knowledge of Allah is obligatory by reason. The author then divides the Mu'tazila into two subsects: one that says reason alone obligates knowledge and messengers merely confirm it—these are deemed sinners (fussaaq); and another that says there is no need for messengers at all, deeming their sending futile—these are considered disbelievers (kuffaar). Thus the passage reinforces the Ash'ari position that the obligation of knowing Allah is established by Revelation, not by unaided reason, and it refutes the Mu'tazila by exposing the consequences of their view.

?

Definition of Taklif and Conditions of the Mukallaf
Al-Sawi defines taklif and explains the conditions of the mukallaf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two definitions of taklif and the conditions that make a person a mukallaf.

Core Idea
Taklif means either imposing or demanding difficulty, affecting which acts are obligatory.

Al-Sawi begins by defining the term 'taklif' and its application to humans and jinn. He presents two definitions that have practical consequences for what is considered an obligation. This lesson clarifies these definitions and the conditions of the mukallaf.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the literal translation to get a general sense of the content.
The passage is dense with commentary embedded in the text.
2
Identify the two definitions of taklif
Find the two definitions: 'ilzam ma fihi kulfah' (imposing difficulty) and 'talab ma fihi kulfah' (demanding difficulty). Note the difference in scope.
The first definition restricts taklif to wajib and haram; the second includes mandub and makruh.
3
Define the mukallaf
The mukallaf is defined as 'al-baligh al-'aqil' (adult and rational). Note that this applies to humans, while jinn are obligated from creation.
Children are not obligated according to the first definition.
4
Examine the objection and answer
Consider the objection: if children are not mukallaf, why do Malikis consider their apostasy and Islam? The answer distinguishes worldly rulings from religious accountability.
Worldly rulings like funeral rites apply even without religious obligation.
5
Reflect on key terms
Review the key terms: taklif, mukallaf, mandub, makruh, mubah, thaqalayn, etc. and how they relate to the definitions.
Understanding these terms is essential for further study.

تعريف التكليف
( على المكلَّف من الثَّقلين الإنس والجن، والتكليف : إلزام ما فيه كُلفة ، وقيل : طلب ما فيه كلفة فلا تكليف بالمندوب والمكروه على الأول الصحيح، بخلاف الثاني، ولا تكليف بالمباح اتفاقاً .
والمكلّف : البالغ العاقل
قوله : (من الثَّقلين): سمُّوا بذلك لكونهم مثقلون بالتكاليف، أو مثقلون الأرض، فهو اسم مفعول، أو اسم فاعل.
