قوله : (البالغ) : أي : وأمَّا الصَّبيُّ : فليس مكلَّفاً .
إن قلت : إنَّ ردَّة الصَّبي وإسلامه معتبران عند المالكية، فما معنى اشتراط البلوغ؟
أجيب : بأنَّ اعتبار ردّته وإسلامه بالنظر لإجراء الأحكام الدنيوية عليه؛ كتغسيله وتكفينه، والصَّلاة عليه وإرثه، ونحو ذلك .
Definition of taklif: Upon the obligated one from the two weighty ones, humans and jinn. And taklif is: imposing that which involves difficulty. And it is said: demanding that which involves difficulty. So there is no taklif for the recommended and the disliked according to the first correct [definition], contrary to the second. And there is no taklif for the permissible by consensus. And the mukallaf is the adult, rational person. His statement: 'from the two weighty ones': they are called thus because they are burdened with obligations, or they burden the earth, so it is a passive participle or active participle. His statement: 'humans and jinn' meaning specifically, as for the angels, they are not obligated with knowledge, for it is necessary for them like the soul. His statement: 'imposing that which involves difficulty' meaning an action like an obligatory act, or abandonment like the forbidden. His statement: 'demanding that which involves difficulty' meaning an action or abandonment, definite or not. His statement: 'so there is no taklif for the recommended and the disliked' meaning even if they are demanded. His statement: 'according to the first correct [definition]' meaning: accordingly, the child is not obligated. His statement: 'contrary to the second' meaning: it is demanding that which involves difficulty, so the recommended and disliked are obligated by them, and accordingly the child is obligated. And his statement in the definition of the mukallaf: 'the adult, rational person' is either according to the first statement, or a definition of the complete mukallaf. His statement: 'and the mukallaf: the adult, rational person': this is a definition of the mukallaf from humans; as for the jinn, they are obligated from the time of creation. His statement: 'the adult' meaning: as for the child, he is not obligated. If you say: the apostasy of the child and his Islam are considered according to the Malikis, so what is the meaning of stipulating adulthood? He answered: that consideration of his apostasy and Islam is with regard to enacting worldly rulings upon him, like washing him, shrouding him, praying over him, inheritance, and similar.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of taklif (obligation) and the conditions of the mukallaf (obligated person). It begins by establishing that taklif applies to the two weighty groups: humans and jinn. The author then presents two definitions: the first is 'imposing what involves difficulty', which is the preferred one; the second is 'demanding what involves difficulty'. The key difference is that on the first definition, only wajib (obligatory) and haram (forbidden) fall under taklif, whereas mandub (recommended) and makruh (disliked) are not considered obligations because they do not involve a binding imposition. On the second definition, however, any demand (even non-binding) counts as taklif, so mandub and makruh are included. The text then clarifies that mubah (permissible) is not taklif by consensus. Next, the author defines the mukallaf as the adult and rational person. He then exegetes the phrase 'al-thaqalayn', explaining that it refers to humans and jinn because they are burdened with obligations or burden the earth. He notes that angels are not obligated with knowledge (ma'rifah) because it is necessary for them, like the soul. He further explains each part of the definition, including the distinction between the two definitions regarding whether the child is obligated (according to the second definition, yes, but the first says no). The passage concludes with an objection: if the child is not obligated according to the first definition, why do Malikis consider his apostasy and Islam? The answer is that such consideration is for worldly rulings (like funeral rites and inheritance), not for religious accountability. This clarifies that the condition of adulthood pertains to religious obligation, not to all legal effects.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mandub and makruh are never part of taklif.
Correction: According to the first definition, they are not part of taklif because taklif is 'imposing' difficulty. However, the second definition defines taklif as 'demanding' difficulty, so mandub and makruh are included as they are demanded.

Checkpoint

C) Yes, mandub only
D) Yes, makruh only

Key Takeaway
The definition of taklif determines whether actions like mandub and makruh are considered obligations, and being a mukallaf requires adulthood and rationality for humans.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The second definition of taklif includes even recommended and disliked actions. Is your love for Allah so strong that you seek closeness even in what is not obligatory? What is one recommended act you will start doing regularly to draw nearer to Him?

The Definition of Knowledge: Firm Perception
Al-Sawi defines knowledge synonymously with ma'rifa as firm perception (idrak jazim).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classical Sunni definition of knowledge as firm perception (al-idrak al-jazim) and why ma'rifa and ilm are considered synonymous.

Core Idea
Knowledge is firm perception that excludes conjecture, doubt, and illusion.

This lesson begins by establishing that knowledge (al-ilm) and ma'rifa are synonymous according to the correct view, both meaning a firm perception of reality. Al-Sawi then defines this firm perception as a genus that includes both firm and non-firm perception, with the differentia 'firm' excluding all doubt. This foundational definition is the starting point for all subsequent theological discussions.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Notice how the author first states the synonymous nature of ma'rifa and ilm before giving the definition.
The key phrase is: 'وهو : الإدراك الجازم' (it is firm perception).
2
Identify the genus and differentia
The definition is composed of a genus (al-idrak) and a differentia (al-jazim). Understand that 'idrak' alone includes both firm and non-firm perception, while 'jazim' restricts it to firm.
Non-firm perceptions include conjecture (zann), doubt (shakk), and illusion (wahm).
3
Reflect on the synonymity
Consider why the author insists that ma'rifa and ilm are one meaning. This avoids confusion and emphasizes that God's knowledge is perfect and not subject to the limitations of human terminology.
The alternative view that ma'rifa is more specific is mentioned but rejected.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
معرفةُ الله العليّ بالمنزلة، والمعرفة والعلم بمعنى واحد على الصَّحيح ، وهو : الإدراك الجازم. حبا الله الـنَّـبـي مـزيـد فـضـل على فضل وكان به رؤوفا فـأحــيــا أمــه وكــذا أبــاه لإيمان به فضلاً منیفا فسلّم فـالـقـديـم بـذا قدير وإن كان الحدیث به ضعیفا قوله : (العليُّ بالمنزلة) أي علوّاً معنوياً، وهو التَّنزه عن النقائص والاتصاف بالكمالات، لا حسّيّاً لاستحالته في حقه تعالى، والمراد بالمنزلة : المرتبة المعنوية. قوله : (بمعنى واحد :أي: وعليه فعدم اتصافه تعالى بالمعرفة، إمَّا لعدم ورودها، أو لإيهامها سبق الجهل. وقوله : (على الصَّحيح مقابله : أنَّ المعرفة أخصُّ من العلم؛ لتعلقها بالبسائط والجزئيات، وتعلقه بالبسائط والمركبات والجزئيات والكليات، وعليه : فعدم اتصافه تعالى بالمعرفة ظاهر لقصورها قوله : (وهو : الإدراك) : جنس يشمل الجازم وغيره. وقوله : (الجازم) : فصل مخرج لغير الجازم ؛ كالظَّنِّ والشَّكِّ والوهم. حكم معرفة الله تعالى المطابق للواقع لموجب فشمل الضَّروري والنظري. [ ١٣٩ وخرج بقيد (الجازم) الظنُّ، وبـ (المطابق) الاعتقاد الفاسد؛ - كاعتقاد الفلسفي قِدَم العالم، وبقوله : (الموجب) - بكسر الجيم . أي : مقتض من دليل أو حسّ أو وجدان، الاعتقاد الصحيح؛ كاعتقاد سنية صلاة العيدين . وقوله : (المطابق للواقع) : أي : المطابق متعلّقه وهو النسبة، والمعنى: مطابقة النّسبة لما في الواقع، وليس المراد أنَّ الجزم هو المطابق . قوله: (فشمل الضَّروريّ والنَّظريّ ... إلخ) أي: يشمل قوله: لموجب) العلم الضروري، وهو ما كان بالوجدانيات والحواس، والنَّظري : وهو ما كان عن دليل، فمعرفة الله تعالى تكون ضرورية لأهل الكشف والبصيرة النَّيِّرة ، ونظرية لأهل الدَّليل . قوله : (الظَّنُّ ) أي : و (أو) للشَّكُ والوهم . قوله : (الاعتقاد الفاسد) : أي : وهو المسمى بالجهل المركب. قوله : (أو حسّ أي ظاهري بإحدى الحواس الخمس ؛ السَّمع والبصر والشَّمِّ واللمس والذوق. قوله: (أو وجدان أي: وهو الحسُّ الباطني؛ كإدراك الجوع والشبع، والحب والبغض. قوله : (كاعتقاد سنيَّة صلاة العيدين أي مجرَّداً عن دليل، وإلا فهو ,معرفة، وأمَّا اعتقاد مشروعيتها وطلبها ؛ فهو ضروري لتواتره بين العام والخاص .
Knowledge of God Most High is [based on] lofty station, and knowledge (al-ma'rifa) and science (al-ilm) are one in meaning according to the correct [view], and it is: firm perception (al-idrak al-jazim). ... He said: ('Aliyy bil-manzila) i.e., lofty in a spiritual sense, which is being free from deficiencies and being described with perfections, not sensory because its impossibility in His right, may He be exalted. And what is meant by manzila is the spiritual rank. He said: (bi-ma'na wahid) i.e., and upon this, His being devoid, may He be exalted, of ma'rifa is either because it is not mentioned, or because it suggests prior ignorance. And his statement: (correct [view]) its opposite is that ma'rifa is more specific than ilm, because it is related to simple entities and particulars, while ilm relates to simple entities, compounds, particulars, and universals. Upon this, His being devoid of ma'rifa is apparent due to its deficiency. He said: (and it is: perception) a genus that includes firm and other. And his statement: (firm) a differentia that excludes what is not firm, such as conjecture, doubt, and illusion. The ruling of knowing God Most High: corresponding to reality on the basis of a cause, so it includes the necessary and the theoretical. And by the condition (firm) exits conjecture, and by (corresponding) exits corrupt belief, like the philosopher's belief in the eternity of the world. And by his statement: (the cause) - with broken jim - i.e., what necessitates from proof, sense, or inner sense, correct belief, like the belief of a Sunni in the prayer of the two Eids. And his statement: (corresponding to reality) i.e., its object corresponds, which is the relation, and the meaning is: correspondence of the relation to what is in reality, and it is not intended that the firmness itself is the correspondence. He said: (so it includes the necessary and the theoretical... etc.) i.e., his statement: 'a cause' includes necessary knowledge, which is from intuitive perceptions and senses, and theoretical [knowledge], which is from proof. So knowledge of God Most High is necessary for the people of unveiling and luminous insight, and theoretical for the people of proof. He said: (conjecture) i.e., and 'or' for doubt and illusion. He said: (corrupt belief) i.e., which is called compound ignorance. His statement: (or sense) i.e., external sense by one of the five senses: hearing, sight, smell, touch, taste. His statement: (or inner sense) i.e., which is internal sense, like perception of hunger and satiety, love and hate. His statement: (like the belief of a Sunni in the prayer of the two Eids) i.e., bare of evidence, otherwise it is knowledge. As for the belief in its legitimacy and obligation, it is necessary due to its widespread transmission among the elite and common.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of knowledge (al-ilm) and its conditions. The author begins by stating that knowledge of God is based on a lofty spiritual station, and that the terms 'marifa' and 'ilm' are synonymous according to the correct view, both meaning firm perception (al-idrak al-jazim). He then explains that God's attribute of being 'al-Aliyy' (the Most High) is a spiritual, not sensory, elevation, meaning transcendence above imperfections and possession of perfections. The discussion continues with the view that marifa is more specific than ilm, but the correct position is their synonymity; thus, God's description with marifa is avoided either due to lack of textual basis or because it might imply prior ignorance. The definition of knowledge is then unpacked: 'perception' is a genus covering both firm and non-firm perception, and 'firm' excludes conjecture, doubt, and illusion. The knowledge of God is defined as 'that which corresponds to reality based on a cause' (al-mutabiq li al-waqi' li-mujib), which includes both necessary (daruri) and theoretical (nazari) knowledge. The condition 'firm' excludes conjecture, and 'corresponding' excludes false beliefs like the philosopher's claim of the world's eternity. The 'cause' (mujib) refers to what necessitates belief, whether from proof (dalil), external senses, or internal senses (wijdan). The author gives examples: a Sunni's belief in the Eid prayer without evidence is still correct belief, but if based on evidence it becomes knowledge; the belief in its legitimacy is necessary due to widespread transmission (tawatur). The passage thoroughly explains the components of the classical Sunni definition of knowledge as 'firm belief corresponding to reality based on evidence'.

Key Terms
المعرفة (al-ma'rifa)
العلم (al-ilm)
الإدراك الجازم (al-idrak al-jazim)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that knowledge (ilm) and ma'rifa are different in meaning, with ma'rifa being more specific.
Correction: The correct view is that they are synonymous, both meaning firm perception. The alternative view is mentioned but rejected.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the correct view on the relationship between 'ma'rifa' and 'ilm'?
A) They are synonymous.
B) Ma'rifa is more specific than ilm.
C) Ilm is only of universals.
D) They are unrelated.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge is defined as firm perception, and the terms ilm and marifa are synonymous in this definition.

When you say you 'know' Allah exists, is that knowledge a firm perception in your heart, or do you sometimes entertain doubt? What steps can you take to strengthen that firmness?
?
If the definition of knowledge excludes conjecture, how much of what you claim to know about your religion is actually built on firm evidence? Will you commit to examining one belief this week to ensure it is truly firm perception?

What Is Kalam?

Conditions of Valid Knowledge: Correspondence and Cause
Al-Sawi explains the conditions of knowledge: correspondence to reality and being based on a cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two essential conditions for valid knowledge: that it corresponds to reality (al-mutabiq li al-waqi') and that it is based on a cause (al-mujib).

Core Idea
True knowledge must both match reality and be grounded in evidence.

Having defined knowledge as firm perception, Al-Sawi now explains what makes that perception true. He adds two conditions: the perception must correspond to what is actually the case (al-mutabiq li al-waqi'), and it must be based on a cause or evidence (al-mujib). These conditions exclude false beliefs and mere conjecture, ensuring that knowledge is not just subjective certainty but objective truth.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the conditions
Read the Arabic passage carefully, noting how the author first states the definition with conditions and then explains each condition.
The key phrase is 'المطابق للواقع لموجب'.
2
Understand what 'mutabiq' excludes
The condition 'corresponding to reality' excludes corrupt beliefs (al-i'tiqad al-fasid), like the philosophical belief in the eternity of the world. It ensures the belief matches reality.
Corrupt belief is also called compound ignorance (al-jahl al-murakkab).
3
Understand what 'mujib' excludes
The condition 'based on a cause' excludes mere opinion or belief without evidence. The cause can be proof (dalil), external senses, or inner senses (wijdan). A Sunni's belief in the Eid prayer without evidence is still correct belief but not knowledge unless based on a cause.
The word 'mujib' is with a broken jim, meaning 'that which necessitates'.

[ ١٣٩ وخرج بقيد (الجازم) الظنُّ، وبـ (المطابق) الاعتقاد الفاسد؛ - كاعتقاد الفلسفي قِدَم العالم، وبقوله : (الموجب) - بكسر الجيم . أي : مقتض من دليل أو حسّ أو وجدان، الاعتقاد الصحيح؛ كاعتقاد سنية صلاة العيدين . وقوله : (المطابق للواقع) : أي : المطابق متعلّقه وهو النسبة، والمعنى: مطابقة النّسبة لما في الواقع، وليس المراد أنَّ الجزم هو المطابق . قوله: (فشمل الضَّروريّ والنَّظريّ ... إلخ) أي: يشمل قوله: لموجب) العلم الضروري، وهو ما كان بالوجدانيات والحواس، والنَّظري : وهو ما كان عن دليل، فمعرفة الله تعالى تكون ضرورية لأهل الكشف والبصيرة النَّيِّرة ، ونظرية لأهل الدَّليل . قوله : (الظَّنُّ ) أي : و (أو) للشَّكُ والوهم . قوله : (الاعتقاد الفاسد) : أي : وهو المسمى بالجهل المركب. قوله : (أو حسّ أي ظاهري بإحدى الحواس الخمس ؛ السَّمع والبصر والشَّمِّ واللمس والذوق. قوله: (أو وجدان أي: وهو الحسُّ الباطني؛ كإدراك الجوع والشبع، والحب والبغض. قوله : (كاعتقاد سنيَّة صلاة العيدين أي مجرَّداً عن دليل، وإلا فهو ,معرفة، وأمَّا اعتقاد مشروعيتها وطلبها ؛ فهو ضروري لتواتره بين العام والخاص .
Knowledge of God Most High is [based on] lofty station, and knowledge (al-ma'rifa) and science (al-ilm) are one in meaning according to the correct [view], and it is: firm perception (al-idrak al-jazim). ... He said: ('Aliyy bil-manzila) i.e., lofty in a spiritual sense, which is being free from deficiencies and being described with perfections, not sensory because its impossibility in His right, may He be exalted. And what is meant by manzila is the spiritual rank. He said: (bi-ma'na wahid) i.e., and upon this, His being devoid, may He be exalted, of ma'rifa is either because it is not mentioned, or because it suggests prior ignorance. And his statement: (correct [view]) its opposite is that ma'rifa is more specific than ilm, because it is related to simple entities and particulars, while ilm relates to simple entities, compounds, particulars, and universals. Upon this, His being devoid of ma'rifa is apparent due to its deficiency. He said: (and it is: perception) a genus that includes firm and other. And his statement: (firm) a differentia that excludes what is not firm, such as conjecture, doubt, and illusion. The ruling of knowing God Most High: corresponding to reality on the basis of a cause, so it includes the necessary and the theoretical. And by the condition (firm) exits conjecture, and by (corresponding) exits corrupt belief, like the philosopher's belief in the eternity of the world. And by his statement: (the cause) - with broken jim - i.e., what necessitates from proof, sense, or inner sense, correct belief, like the belief of a Sunni in the prayer of the two Eids. And his statement: (corresponding to reality) i.e., its object corresponds, which is the relation, and the meaning is: correspondence of the relation to what is in reality, and it is not intended that the firmness itself is the correspondence. He said: (so it includes the necessary and the theoretical... etc.) i.e., his statement: 'a cause' includes necessary knowledge, which is from intuitive perceptions and senses, and theoretical [knowledge], which is from proof. So knowledge of God Most High is necessary for the people of unveiling and luminous insight, and theoretical for the people of proof. He said: (conjecture) i.e., and 'or' for doubt and illusion. He said: (corrupt belief) i.e., which is called compound ignorance. His statement: (or sense) i.e., external sense by one of the five senses: hearing, sight, smell, touch, taste. His statement: (or inner sense) i.e., which is internal sense, like perception of hunger and satiety, love and hate. His statement: (like the belief of a Sunni in the prayer of the two Eids) i.e., bare of evidence, otherwise it is knowledge. As for the belief in its legitimacy and obligation, it is necessary due to its widespread transmission among the elite and common.

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of knowledge (al-ilm) and its conditions. The author begins by stating that knowledge of God is based on a lofty spiritual station, and that the terms 'marifa' and 'ilm' are synonymous according to the correct view, both meaning firm perception (al-idrak al-jazim). He then explains that God's attribute of being 'al-Aliyy' (the Most High) is a spiritual, not sensory, elevation, meaning transcendence above imperfections and possession of perfections. The discussion continues with the view that marifa is more specific than ilm, but the correct position is their synonymity; thus, God's description with marifa is avoided either due to lack of textual basis or because it might imply prior ignorance. The definition of knowledge is then unpacked: 'perception' is a genus covering both firm and non-firm perception, and 'firm' excludes conjecture, doubt, and illusion. The knowledge of God is defined as 'that which corresponds to reality based on a cause' (al-mutabiq li al-waqi' li-mujib), which includes both necessary (daruri) and theoretical (nazari) knowledge. The condition 'firm' excludes conjecture, and 'corresponding' excludes false beliefs like the philosopher's claim of the world's eternity. The 'cause' (mujib) refers to what necessitates belief, whether from proof (dalil), external senses, or internal senses (wijdan). The author gives examples: a Sunni's belief in the Eid prayer without evidence is still correct belief, but if based on evidence it becomes knowledge; the belief in its legitimacy is necessary due to widespread transmission (tawatur). The passage thoroughly explains the components of the classical Sunni definition of knowledge as 'firm belief corresponding to reality based on evidence'.

Necessary and Theoretical Knowledge
Al-Sawi divides knowledge into necessary and theoretical, applying it to knowledge of God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between necessary (daruri) and theoretical (nazari) knowledge, and how it applies to the knowledge of God.

Core Idea
Knowledge of God can be either necessary for the illumined or theoretical for those who rely on proof.

Al-Sawi now builds on the definition by dividing knowledge into two types: necessary knowledge, which is immediate and based on intuition or senses, and theoretical knowledge, which requires reasoning from proof. He applies this to knowledge of God, explaining that spiritual unveiling yields necessary knowledge, while for most people it is theoretical. This distinction is crucial for understanding different paths to certainty in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the division
Read the Arabic passage that explains the division of knowledge into necessary and theoretical, and then the examples.
Notice that the passage also explains the sources of necessary knowledge: intuition (wijdaniyyat) and senses (hawas).
2
Apply to knowledge of God
Understand that for people of unveiling (ahl al-kashf), knowledge of God is necessary and immediate, while for people of proof (ahl al-dalil), it is theoretical.
This shows that both paths lead to valid knowledge.
3
Examine the example of Eid prayer
The passage ends with an example: belief in the Eid prayer without proof is correct but not knowledge, but belief based on tawatur (widespread transmission) is necessary knowledge.
Tawatur provides certainty without individual reasoning.

What Is Kalam?
beginner
6 min
Definition of Knowledge of Allah and Its Three Conditions
Al-Sawi defines knowledge of Allah with three conditions: certainty, correspondence, and cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three essential conditions for valid knowledge of Allah: certainty, correspondence to reality, and being based on a cause.

Core Idea
Knowledge of Allah requires certainty, correspondence, and a cause.

Before we can properly study the attributes of Allah, we need a precise definition of what it means to truly 'know' Allah. Al-Sawi provides a technical definition with three conditions that distinguish genuine knowledge from mere opinion, false belief, or blind imitation. This passage lays the groundwork for all theological inquiry.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Start by reading the definition of knowledge of Allah: a firm belief (jāzm) that corresponds to reality (muwāfiq lil-wāqiʿ) and is based on a cause (mūjib). Notice that it covers both necessary and theoretical knowledge.
The phrase 'so it includes the necessary and the theoretical' will be explained later.
2
Identify the three conditions and their exclusions
Al-Sawi unpacks each condition: 'firm' excludes mere opinion (ẓann), 'corresponding' excludes false belief (iʿtiqād fāsid), and 'cause' excludes belief without a valid basis. Find the examples for each.
Note that 'cause' includes proof, external sense, and inner sense.
3
Understand the clarification on 'correspondence'
Al-Sawi clarifies that correspondence is about the mental relation (nisba) matching reality, not the firm belief itself being the correspondence. This prevents misunderstanding.
4
Review the example of the Eid prayers
The Sunni belief that the two Eid prayers are sunnah is only knowledge if based on evidence; otherwise it is mere imitation. This illustrates the 'cause' condition.
If the obligation of the prayers is known by tawatur, it becomes necessary knowledge.
5
Connect to the broader study of kalam
Reflect on why defining knowledge precisely matters: it sets the standard for what counts as valid theological arguments and prevents error.
This lesson is foundational for all subsequent discussions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حكم معرفة الله تعالى المطابق للواقع لموجب فشمل الضَّروري والنَّظري.
وخرج بقيد (الجازم) الظنُّ، وبـ (المطابق) الاعتقاد الفاسد؛ -
كاعتقاد الفلسفي قِدَم العالم، وبقوله : (الموجب) - بكسر الجيم . أي : مقتض من دليل أو حسّ أو وجدان، الاعتقاد الصحيح؛ كاعتقاد سنية صلاة العيدين .
وقوله : (المطابق للواقع) : أي : المطابق متعلّقه وهو النسبة، والمعنى: مطابقة النّسبة لما في الواقع، وليس المراد أنَّ الجزم هو المطابق .
قوله : (الظَّنُّ ) أي : و (أو) للشَّكُ والوهم .
قوله : (الاعتقاد الفاسد) : أي : وهو المسمى بالجهل المركب.
قوله : (أو حسّ أي ظاهري بإحدى الحواس الخمس ؛ السَّمع والبصر والشَّمِّ واللمس والذوق.
قوله: (أو وجدان أي: وهو الحسُّ الباطني؛ كإدراك الجوع والشبع، والحب والبغض.
قوله : (كاعتقاد سنيَّة صلاة العيدين أي مجرَّداً عن دليل، وإلا فهو معرفة، وأمَّا اعتقاد مشروعيتها وطلبها ؛ فهو ضروري لتواتره بين العام والخاص .
The ruling of knowledge of Allah Most High: it is a firm belief corresponding to reality based on a cause, so it includes the necessary and the theoretical. And by the restriction (the firm belief) is excluded mere opinion, and by (corresponding to reality) is excluded false belief – like the philosopher's belief in the eternity of the world, and by his statement (the cause) – with kasr of the jim – i.e., that which necessitates from a proof or sense or inner sense, correct belief; like the Sunni belief in the two Eid prayers. And his statement: (corresponding to reality): i.e., its object is the relation, and the meaning is the correspondence of the relation to what is in reality, and it is not intended that the firm belief itself is the corresponding one. His statement: (mere opinion) i.e., and (or) is for doubt and illusion. His statement: (false belief): i.e., it is what is called compound ignorance. His statement: (or sense) i.e., external sense by one of the five senses: hearing, sight, smell, touch, taste. His statement: (or inner sense) i.e., it is the internal sense; like perceiving hunger and satiety, and love and hate. His statement: (like the Sunni belief in the two Eid prayers) i.e., merely without proof, otherwise it is knowledge. As for belief in its legality and obligation, it is necessary due to its tawatur among the common and the specific.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage defines the nature of valid knowledge (ma'rifah) of Allah and sets out its essential conditions. The author begins by stating the definition: knowledge of Allah is a firm belief (jāzm) that corresponds to reality (muwāfiq lil-wāqiʿ) and is based on a cause (mūjib). The phrase 'so it includes the necessary and the theoretical' is part of the definition but will be explained in the next lesson. The author then unpacks each condition. The word 'firm' excludes mere opinion (ẓann), which lacks certainty. The word 'corresponding' excludes false belief (iʿtiqād fāsid), such as the philosophers' belief that the world is eternal—this belief does not match reality. The word 'cause' (mūjib) is explained: it is what necessitates belief, whether from a proof (dalīl), external sense (ḥiss), or inner sense (wijdān). An example of correct belief based on a cause is the Sunni belief that the two Eid prayers are sunnah—provided it is based on evidence; otherwise it is mere imitation, not knowledge. The author then clarifies that 'corresponding to reality' refers to the relation (nisba) in the mind matching the relation in reality; it is not that the firm belief itself is the correspondence. Next, he explains that 'ẓann' in the text is a category including doubt (shakk) and illusion (wahm). 'False belief' is identified as compound ignorance (jahl murakkab). He then details the sources: external senses are the five senses, and inner sense (wijdān) includes feelings like hunger and love. Finally, he revisits the example of the Eid prayers: if believed without proof, it is not knowledge; but the widespread tawatur of its obligation makes it necessary knowledge for those aware of that consensus.

جهل مركب
حس ظاهري

نظري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think any firm belief qualifies as knowledge of Allah, even if it contradicts reality or has no basis.
Correction: Knowledge requires all three conditions: firmness, correspondence to reality, and being based on a valid cause (proof, sense, or inner sense). A firm belief that is false or baseless is not knowledge.

Checkpoint
What does the condition 'mūjib' (cause) exclude?
A) Mere opinion (ẓann)
B) False belief (iʿtiqād fāsid)
C) Belief without any basis, like blind imitation
D) Belief based on sense perception

Key Takeaway
Genuine knowledge of Allah must be certain, match reality, and be grounded in a valid cause—otherwise it is mere opinion, false belief, or imitation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think about your belief in Allah's existence. Is it a firm conviction (jāzm) that leaves no room for doubt, or do you sometimes waver? What would it take for you to reach that level of certainty described in this passage?
?
Consider a belief you hold about an Islamic ruling—perhaps about prayer or fasting. Is that belief based on a valid cause (proof, reliable sense, or inner conviction), or is it simply inherited from your family or culture? Be honest: if asked to provide evidence, could you?
?
The passage says knowledge must correspond to reality. Is there any belief you hold about Islam that might not align with objective truth? How would you find out, and are you willing to let go of a comfortable but false belief?

Definition of Taqlid and the Infallible Exception
Al-Sawi defines taqlid and clarifies the infallible exception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of taqlid in Islamic theology and why accepting statements from an infallible person is not considered taqlid but rather certain knowledge.

Core Idea
Taqlid is blind following without proof; following an infallible is knowledge, not imitation.

Al-Sawi begins by defining taqlid as accepting another’s statement without evidence. This may seem straightforward, but a crucial nuance follows: following an infallible person, like a living prophet, is not taqlid at all. Instead, it produces necessary knowledge. This lesson clarifies the boundary between blind imitation and genuine knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of taqlid
Read the Arabic and translation, noting that taqlid is defined as taking someone else's statement without proof. Notice the three key words: الأخذ بقول الغير من غير حجة.
2
Understand the implied question
The commentator explains that this definition answers whether taqlid suffices for knowledge of God. Consider why one might ask this.
3
Distinguish the infallible case
The passage then clarifies that hearing from an infallible during his lifetime is not taqlid but yields necessary knowledge. Reflect on why this distinction matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمَّا التّقليد، وهو الأخذ بقول الغير من غير حجّة؛ أي: الاعتقاد الجازم المتمسك فيه بمجرد قول الغير. قوله : (وأمَّا التَّقليد): جواب عن سؤال مقدَّر، حاصله قد ذكرت المعرفة، وما يتعلَّق بها فهل يكتفي بالتّقليد أو لا؟ فأجاب بما ذكر . قوله : (بقول الغير ) أي : وهو غير معصوم ، وأمَّا سماع المعصوم في حال حياته؛ فلا يسمى تقليداً، بل هو معرفة وتحقيق، فيفيد العلم الضَّروري. قوله : (أي: الاعتقاد الجازم) : أي : بحيث لو رجع مقلّده لا مقلّده لا يرجع
As for taqlid (imitation), it is taking the statement of another without proof; i.e., firm belief that clings solely to the statement of another. His saying: 'As for taqlid' is an answer to an implied question: the gist of which is: you have mentioned knowledge and what pertains to it; so is taqlid sufficient or not? He replied with what was mentioned. His saying: 'the statement of another' means: and he is non-infallible (ghayr ma'sūm). As for hearing from an infallible during his lifetime, it is not called taqlid, but rather knowledge (ma'rifa) and verification (taḥqīq), and it yields necessary knowledge (al-'ilm al-ḍarūrī). His saying: 'i.e., firm belief' means: such that if his follower recants? (the text is unclear).
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage defines taqlid and clarifies a crucial distinction. The author first defines taqlid as accepting another's statement without proof—that is, a firm conviction based solely on someone else's assertion. Then the commentary explains that this definition is given in response to a hypothetical question about whether taqlid suffices for knowledge of God. Importantly, the commentator distinguishes between following a non-infallible person (which is taqlid) and receiving knowledge from an infallible (like a prophet) during his lifetime, which is not taqlid but true knowledge that gives necessary certainty. The definition of 'firm belief' is explained as such that if the follower were to return (or recant?), the last part is unclear in the text.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Following a prophet during his lifetime is also taqlid.
Correction: The passage clarifies that following an infallible is not taqlid but true knowledge that yields necessary certainty.

Checkpoint

B) No, it is knowledge and verification
C) It depends on the context
D) Only if the prophet is not present

Key Takeaway
Taqlid is blind imitation of non-infallible persons; following an infallible yields certainty, not imitation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own journey of faith, which beliefs have you accepted simply because someone you trust said them, without seeking evidence? Are you willing to test those beliefs against the Qur'an and Sunnah today, even if it means admitting you were following taqlid?
?

?
Imagine you discover that a belief you held firmly for years was based solely on taqlid of a non-infallible person. Will you have the courage to abandon that belief and seek the truth, even if it costs you relationships or a sense of security? What is stopping you from making that decision right now?

Faith's Two Levels and the Muqallid
Distinguishes perfect faith from basic faith, validating taqlid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between perfect faith and the basis of faith, and why the faith of a muqallid (one who follows without proof) is considered valid in the Hereafter.

Core Idea
Faith has two levels: perfect faith requires knowledge; the basis requires only decisive belief.

In this passage, the commentator addresses a key debate about the faith of a muqallid (one who accepts religious beliefs without analytical proof). He clarifies that while perfect faith is defined as inner speech following knowledge, the basis of faith is defined as inner speech following decisive belief, which includes taqlid. This distinction resolves the controversy and underscores the mercy of Allah in accepting simple faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the literal translation to understand the meaning. Note how the commentator first addresses the ruling on the muqallid.
The passage has two clear parts: the worldly and afterlife rulings for the muqallid, and the two definitions of faith.
2
Identify the two definitions of iman
Look for the phrase 'inner speech following knowledge' (perfect faith) and 'inner speech following decisive belief' (basis of faith). Notice that the second definition is broader and includes taqlid.
The basis of faith does not require analytical knowledge; it only requires a firm conviction.
3
Understand the muqallid's validity
The commentator states that according to the majority (contrary to Abu Hashim), the muqallid's faith is valid in the Hereafter. In this world, pronouncing the shahadas is sufficient for Muslim status.
This is an important point: the basis of faith is valid even without deep knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (فإيمان المقلّد (صحيح) : أي خلافاً لأبي هاشم الجبائي القائل : بأنه كافر، وكل هذا بالنظر لما عند الله في الآخرة، وأما في الدُّنيا : فمن نطق بالشَّهادتين؛ فهو مسلم اتفاقاً تجري عليه أحكام المسلمين. وقولهم في تعريف الإيمان : هو حديث النَّفس التابع للمعرفة، محمول على الإيمان الكامل، وأما تعريف أصل الإيمان؛ فهو حديث النَّفس التّابع للاعتقاد الجازم فيشمل التّقليد .
His saying: (The faith of the muqallid is valid) meaning: contrary to Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i who said that he is a disbeliever. And all of this is with regard to what is with Allah in the Hereafter. As for in this world: whoever pronounces the two shahadas is a Muslim by consensus, and the rulings of Muslims apply to him. And their statement in the definition of faith: it is inner speech following knowledge, carried upon perfect faith. As for the definition of the basis of faith, it is inner speech following decisive belief, so it includes taqlid.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage first explains the validity of the muqallid's faith in the next world, in contrast to Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i who denied it, and notes that in this world, pronouncing the two shahadas suffices for Muslim status. Then it discusses the definition of iman. The definition as 'inner speech (hadith al-nafs) following knowledge' applies to perfect faith (al-iman al-kamil). But the definition of the basis of faith (asl al-iman) is 'inner speech following decisive belief (i'tiqad jazim)', which includes taqlid because a muqallid can have decisive belief without analytical knowledge.

اعتقاد جازم (decisive belief)
تقليد (taqlid)
كامل (perfect)
أصل الإيمان (basis of faith)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that all definitions of iman require knowledge (ma'rifa).
Correction: The commentator shows that the basis of faith only requires decisive belief (i'tiqad jazim), so taqlid is acceptable as the foundation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the definition of the basis of faith (asl al-iman)?
A) Inner speech following knowledge
B) Inner speech following decisive belief
C) Pronouncing the two shahadas
D) Knowledge of Allah

Key Takeaway
The basis of faith requires decisive belief, not necessarily knowledge, so the faith of a muqallid is valid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The lesson distinguishes between perfect faith (with knowledge) and the basis of faith (with decisive belief). Which level honestly describes your current state, and what one step will you take today to move toward perfect faith?
?
Abu Hashim judged the muqallid's faith as invalid. Have you ever dismissed someone's faith because they couldn't articulate it? How does this passage correct that judgment in your heart?

The Correct Meaning of Knowing Allah
Al-Sawi defines knowledge of Allah and clarifies its scope and ruling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that knowledge of Allah (ma'rifah) consists of knowing what is necessary, impossible, and permissible for Him, and that this obligation is derived from revelation, while knowledge of His essence is beyond human capacity.

Core Idea
Knowledge of Allah is knowing His attributes, not His essence.

This passage from al-Sawi's Hashiya clarifies what it truly means to 'know Allah.' Many mistakenly think it requires penetrating the divine essence, but the scholars teach that it is about understanding Allah's necessary, impossible, and permissible attributes. The passage also explains that this knowledge is only obligatory because of revelation, and that the highest form of knowledge is realizing our inability to comprehend His essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of ma'rifah
Carefully read the opening sentence where al-Sawi defines knowledge of Allah as knowledge of the three rational categories: necessary, impossible, and permissible.
Notice he explicitly says 'not knowledge of the reality of the Supreme Essence.'
2
Understand the source of obligation
The passage states that this knowledge is obligatory by the Law (shar'), not by reason alone. This counters the Mu'tazilite view that reason independently mandates it.
Al-Sawi emphasizes that before revelation, there is no ruling at all.
3
Analyze the refutation of essence-knowledge
Read the section starting from 'Not knowledge of the reality...' to 'the correct view is that it is not permissible by reason.' Understand why knowing the essence is impossible.
The argument: the contingent cannot comprehend the Necessary Being.
4
Reflect on the transmitted proofs
Go through the poetic quote, the hadiths, and the sayings of Abu Bakr and Ali. They all reinforce the theme that Allah is veiled from perception and that acknowledging this incapacity is itself a form of knowledge.
The famous statement 'al-'ajzu 'an idraki idrakun' is key.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولهذا كانت معرفة الله تعالى عبارة عن معرفة ما يجب في تعالى وما يستحيل وما يجوز، لا معرفة حقيقة الذات العليَّة ... قوله : (واجبة بالشَّرع) : أي : إنَّ وجوب المعرفة لم يدرك إلَّا من الشَّرع، ولم يعلم إلا منه، فلا حكم قبل الشرع أصلاً لا أصليّاً ولا فرعياً. قوله: (لا معرفة حقيقة الذَّات العليَّة . . . إلخ) : لأنَّها ليست من الواجبات فضلاً عن كونها من أوَّلها، بل لا تعرف لأحد ولو ارتفعت درجته، وإن أمكنت معرفتها عقلاً كذا قيل، والأصح أنَّها لا تجوز عقلاً كما لا تجوز شرعاً، كما في شرح الكبرى» عن الإمام الغزالي(۱)، فإنَّ الحادث يقصر بالطبع عن عظيم هذا المقام، قال الشَّريف المقدسي في مفاتيح الكنوز (۲) : [ من البسيط] ظننت جهلاً بأنَّ الله تدركه ثواقبُ الفِكْر أو تدريه إيقانا أو العقول أحاطته بديهتها أو هل أقامت به لولاه برهانا الله أعظم قدراً أن يُحيط به عِلم وعقل ورأي جل سلطانا هذا اعتقادي فإن قصرتُ في عملي فأسأل الله توفيقاً وغفرانا وفي الحديث : إن الله احتجب عن البصائر كما احتجب عنِ الأبصار، وإنَّ الملأ الأعلى يطلبونه كما تطلبونه (۱). وعن أبي هريرة عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال: «تفكَّروا في الخلقِ ولا تفكَّروا في الخالقِ ؛ فإنَّه لا تحيط به الفكرة» (۲) . وسئل أبو بكر الصِّدِّيق : بمَ عرفتَ ربَّك؟ قال: (عرفتُ ربِّي بربي، ولولا ربي ما عرفت ربي)، فقيل له : هل يتأتّى لبشر أن يدركه؟ فقال: (العجز عن الإدراك إدراك)(۳) وسئل علي بن أبي طالب : بِمَ عَرفتَ ربَّك؟ قال : (عرفته بما عرَّفني ! نفسه، لا يُدرك بالحواس، ولا يقاس بالقياس، ولا يُشبه بالنَّاس، قريب في بعده، بعيد في قربه فوق كلِّ شيء، ولا يقال : تحته شيء، وأمام كلِّ شيء، ولا يقال: أمامه شيء، وهو في كلِّ شيء لا كشيء في شيء، فسبحان من هو كذا، ولا هكذا أحد سواه) (٤) وفي الحديث : «إِنَّ اللهَ  َخلقَ خلقَهُ في ظلمة، ثم رش عليهم من نوره؛ فمن أصابه ذلك النُّورُ هدي، ومن أخطأه ذلك النُّورُ ضلَّ (٥).
And therefore knowledge of Allah, the Exalted, is an expression of knowledge of what is necessary (wajib) for the Exalted, what is impossible (mustahil), and what is permissible (ja'iz), not knowledge of the reality of the Supreme Essence. His statement: 'Obligatory by the Law' meaning: indeed the obligation of knowledge is not perceived except from the Law, and is not known except from it; so there is no ruling before the Law at all, neither primary nor secondary. His statement: 'Not knowledge of the reality of the Supreme Essence etc.' because it is not among the obligations, let alone being the first of them; rather, it is not known to anyone even if his rank is elevated, and if its knowledge is possible by reason, it is said as such, but the correct view is that it is not permissible by reason just as it is not permissible by Law, as in Sharh al-Kubra from Imam al-Ghazali. For the contingent is naturally deficient before the grandeur of this station. Al-Sharif al-Maqdisi said in Mafatih al-Kunuz: 'I ignorantly thought that penetrating thoughts could perceive Allah or attain Him with certainty, or that minds could encompass Him with their intuition, or that they could establish a proof of Him without Him. Allah is too great to be encompassed by knowledge, intellect, or sight—exalted is His authority. This is my belief; if I fall short in my deeds, then I ask Allah for success and forgiveness.' And in the hadith: 'Verily Allah has veiled Himself from inner sights just as He has veiled Himself from outer sights, and the highest assembly seeks Him as you seek Him.' And on the authority of Abu Hurayra from the Prophet (peace be upon him) who said: 'Reflect on the creation and do not reflect on the Creator, for thought cannot encompass Him.' And Abu Bakr al-Siddiq was asked: 'By what did you know your Lord?' He said: 'I knew my Lord by my Lord; were it not for my Lord, I would not know my Lord.' And it was said to him: 'Can a human attain perception of Him?' He said: 'Incapacity to perceive is perception.' And Ali ibn Abi Talib was asked: 'By what did you know your Lord?' He said: 'I knew Him by what He made me know of Himself. He is not perceived by the senses, nor measured by analogy, nor compared to people. Near in His distance, far in His nearness. Above everything, and it is not said: something beneath Him. Before everything, and it is not said: something before Him. He is in everything, not as a thing in a thing. Glory be to Him who is such, and no one else is like this.' And in the hadith: 'Verily Allah created His creation in darkness, then sprinkled upon them from His light; so whoever was struck by that light is guided, and whoever missed that light goes astray.'
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage defines the nature of knowledge of Allah (ma'rifat Allah) and its ruling. The author begins by stating that knowledge of Allah consists of knowing what is necessarily attributable to Him (wajib), what is impossible for Him (mustahil), and what is permissible for Him (ja'iz). It does not consist of knowing the reality of His essence (dhat), which is impossible. Then he explains the statement 'wajib bi al-shar'' (obligatory by the Law): the obligation to seek this knowledge is known only through revelation, not through reason alone, contrary to the Mu'tazila. There is no ruling before revelation—neither primary nor secondary. He then elaborates that knowledge of the essence is not obligatory, nor even possible, whether by reason or by revelation. He supports this with a quote from al-Ghazali stating that the contingent (al-hadith) is naturally incapable of comprehending the Divine. Poetry by al-Sharif al-Maqdisi reinforces the point. Then he cites hadith and sayings of the Companions (Abu Bakr and Ali) and a hadith about the creation in darkness to emphasize that Allah is veiled from perception, and that the highest form of knowledge is to acknowledge one's inability to comprehend His essence. The passage thus establishes the limits of human knowledge concerning Allah: it is obligatory to know His attributes but impossible to know His essence.

Key Terms
معرفة الله
واجب بالشرع
الذات العليّة
ما يجب
ما يستحيل
ما يجوز
العجز عن الإدراك إدراك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That knowledge of Allah means knowing His essence or being able to fully comprehend Him.
Correction: Knowledge of Allah is limited to knowing His necessary, impossible, and permissible attributes, and the highest knowledge is to acknowledge one's inability to grasp His essence.

Checkpoint
According to al-Sawi, what does 'knowledge of Allah' (ma'rifat Allah) consist of?
A) Knowing the reality of the divine essence
B) Knowing what is necessary, impossible, and permissible for Allah
C) Having a direct vision of Allah
D) Repeating the names of Allah

Key Takeaway
True knowledge of Allah is to know what is necessary, impossible, and permissible for Him, and to acknowledge the impossibility of knowing His essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to know Allah, but do you truly know that He is beyond your senses and intellect? When you try to imagine Him, do you stop yourself and humble your heart? Will you commit to reflecting only on His signs and not on His essence starting today?
?
Abu Bakr said: 'Incapacity to perceive is perception.' Have you ever felt that your inability to comprehend Allah is itself a form of knowledge? How will you cultivate this humble awareness in your daily life?
?
The Prophet taught you not to reflect on the Creator. Where is your mind wandering when you pray? Are you trying to grasp the Unseen with your thoughts? Will you train yourself to focus only on His attributes and signs?

Defining Knowledge of Allah as Knowledge of the Necessary Being
The author defines knowledge of Allah as knowledge of the Necessary Being.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that 'knowing Allah' (ma'rifat Allah) in Islamic theology is defined as knowledge of the Necessary Being (al-wajib), not mystical intuition.

Core Idea
Knowing Allah means knowing that the Necessary Being exists.

This lesson clarifies the fundamental definition of ma'rifat Allah in Islamic theology. The author first states the ruling, then interprets knowledge as the recognition of the Necessary Being. He provides grammatical and logical reasons for this interpretation, ensuring the student understands that theology begins with affirming a necessary existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get a sense of its flow. Then read the translation. Notice how the author moves from a general ruling to a specific definition.
The passage uses a grammatical example to illustrate its point.
2
Identify the definition
Locate the sentence that says 'knowledge of Allah the Most High is your knowledge of the Necessary Being'. This is the core definition.
3
Understand the grammatical tool
The author uses the explicative 'ay' to interpret 'ya'rifu' as a verbal noun. This shows that the intended meaning is 'to know' in the sense of recognition, not a specific act.
The example 'tasma'u bi al-Mu'aydi khayrun min taraahu' illustrates how a verb can be interpreted as a source.
4
Reflect on the importance
Ask yourself: Why is it important to define ma'rifah this way? How does this definition shape the study of kalam?
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حكم معرفة الله تعالى
ولعدم تكليفنا بذلك، فسَّرَ المعرفة بما هو المراد فقال:
(أي: يعرف) هو وإن كان مرفوعاً لتجرُّده من ناصب وجازم إلا أن المعنى على تقدير (أن) المصدرية نحو : (تسمع بالمُعيدي خير من تراه) أي معرفة الله تعالى هي معرفتك (الواجب) :
قوله : (ولعدم تكليفنا بذلك) : عطف علة على معلول، كذا قرَّره الشَّرح ، ولعلَّ الأظهر أنَّه عطف معلول على علَّة.
قوله : (إِلَّا أَنَّ المعنى ... إلخ): وجه ذلك أنَّ ما بعد أي التَّفسيريَّة يكون عطف بيان لما قبله، وما قبله مصدر صريح، فيجب تأويل هذا بمصدر .
قوله : (تسمع) مبتدأ وخبر خبره والمبتدأ لا يكون إلَّا اسمًا
The ruling of knowing Allah the Most High. And because we are not tasked with that, he interpreted knowledge as what is intended and said: (i.e., he knows) it is in the nominative due to being devoid of a subject and predicate, except that the meaning is on the basis of the subjunctive (an) like: 'You hear of al-Mu'aydi is better than you see him' i.e., knowledge of Allah the Most High is your knowledge of the Necessary Being. His saying: (And because we are not tasked with that) is a conjunction of a cause upon an effect, as the commentary determined, and perhaps the more apparent is that it is a conjunction of an effect upon a cause. His saying: (Except that the meaning ... etc.): The reason for that is that what comes after the explicative 'ay' is an apposition to what precedes it, and what precedes it is an explicit verbal noun, so it is necessary to interpret this as a verbal noun. His saying: (You hear) is a subject and predicate, and a subject can only be a noun.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage defines the ruling (hukm) of knowing Allah. The author first states that because we are not obligated to attain the deep, intuitive knowledge described earlier (the light in the heart), he interprets knowledge (al-ma'rifa) in the legal sense as what is intended: it is the knowledge that the Necessary Being (al-wajib) exists. He then provides a grammatical analysis of the phrase "يعرف" (he knows), explaining that it is in the nominative case because it is not preceded by a subjunctive or jussive particle, but the intended meaning is with the subjunctive 'an' (to know). He gives an example: 'You hear of al-Mu'aydi is better than you see him' — this illustrates how a verb can be interpreted as a verbal noun. From this, he concludes that knowledge of Allah is your knowledge of the Necessary Being. Then he comments on the author's phrase 'Because we are not tasked with that,' analyzing it grammatically as either a cause connected to an effect or vice versa. Finally, he notes that after the explicative 'ay', the following word must be an apposition, and that 'you hear' functions as a subject and predicate, with the subject being a noun.

Key Terms
معرفة
واجب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'knowing Allah' means mystical or intuitive knowledge, or direct vision of Allah.
Correction: The passage clarifies that 'knowing Allah' in this context is simply knowledge that the Necessary Being exists.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is knowledge of Allah (ma'rifat Allah)?
A) A spiritual light revealed to the heart
B) Knowledge of the Necessary Being (al-wajib)
C) Knowledge of all divine attributes
D) The ability to see Allah directly

Key Takeaway
Knowledge of Allah (ma'rifat Allah) is defined as the intellectual recognition that the Necessary Being (al-wajib) exists, not mystical experience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'I know Allah,' is your knowledge anything more than acknowledging that a Necessary Being exists? What concrete evidence in your daily actions shows that you truly know Him?
?
The author reduces ma'rifah to an intellectual recognition. Yet you might feel that this is not enough. What is missing in your own relationship with Allah—and what will you do today to seek that deeper knowledge?
?
If someone were to judge your life based solely on whether you behave as if a Necessary Being exists, would they see that belief reflected in your choices? Where does your life contradict the very definition of ma'rifah you just read?

The World as a Sign and the Three Sophist Sects
Author explains naming of 'world', affirms fixed realities, and classifies three sophist sects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the world is called a sign, that realities are fixed and knowable, and the three groups of sophists—the 'Inadiyya, 'Indiyya, and La-adriyya.

Core Idea
The world is a sign; realities are fixed; sophists deny this in three ways.

In this lesson, the author begins by explaining the meaning of 'world' ('alam) as a sign pointing to its Maker. He then asserts that the realities of things are fixed and knowable, a view held by all religious communities except the Sophists, whom he classifies into three sects. Understanding these categories helps clarify the Islamic epistemological stance against skepticism and relativism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: definition, affirmation, exception, classification.
The author uses a demonstrative pronoun to reinforce the point about fixed realities.
2
Identify the three sophist groups
List the three groups and their key claims as stated in the passage. See how each denies fixed reality in a different way.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why the author mentions the sophists here. This sets the stage for later refutations in the discipline of kalam.
The full refutation is deferred to longer works.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سُمِّي بذلك لأنَّه علامة؛ أي دليل، على وجود صانعه . وفي التعبير باسم الإشارة إشارة إلى أنَّ حقائق الأشياء ثابتة، وأنَّ العلم بها متحقق، وهو كذلك عند جميع الملل إلا السوفسطائية، فقد خالفوا في ذلك، وهم فرق ثلاثة : عنادية يقولون : لا ثبوت لحقيقة من الحقائق، وإنَّما هي أوهام وخيالات كالذي يرى في المنام . وعنديَّة يقولون الشَّخص عند اعتقاده، حتَّى لو اعتقد أنَّ النَّار جَنَّة أو بالعكس لكان كذلك. واللاأدرية يقولون في كل شيء: لا أدري، حتى إنَّه يشكُ نفسه وفي شكّه . وتوضيح الرَّدِّ عليهم مذكور في المطولات.
It was named thus because it is a sign, i.e., a proof, of the existence of its Maker. And in the expression with the demonstrative pronoun is an indication that the realities of things are fixed, and that knowledge of them is actual, and it is so according to all religious communities except the Sophists, for they have opposed in that, and they are three sects: the 'Inadiyya say: there is no establishment for any reality, rather they are delusions and fantasies like what one sees in a dream. And the 'Indiyya say: a person is according to his belief, so that if he believes that fire is Paradise or the opposite, it would be so. And the La-adriyya say about everything: I do not know, so much so that they doubt themselves and their doubt. And the clarification of the refutation against them is mentioned in the longer works.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification. It first explains why the world (العالم) is so called: it is a sign (علامة) pointing to its Creator. Then it uses the demonstrative pronoun in the expression to argue that the realities of things are fixed and knowable, which is universally accepted except by the Sophists (السوفسطائية). The author then classifies the Sophists into three groups: the 'Inadiyya (adversarial) who deny any fixed reality and consider everything as illusions; the 'Indiyya (subjectivists) who hold that truth is relative to individual belief; and the La-adriyya (agnostics) who doubt everything, including their own doubt. The passage concludes by referring the full refutation to longer compositions. The explanation follows the author's order: name, then the point about reality, then the exception, then the groups.

سوفسطائية
عنادية
عندية
لاأدرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Definition of the World and Refutation of Sophists
Definition of the world and sophist refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the world in Islamic theology and the Islamic response to sophist denial of objective truth.

Core Idea
The world is everything besides Allah; sophists who deny truth cannot be reasoned with.

This passage from Sanusi's introduction defines the world as 'all that is other than Allah' and then addresses the challenge of sophists who deny the possibility of knowledge. The definition sets the foundation for Islamic cosmology, while the discussion of sophist sects shows the limits of rational debate when interlocutors reject basic principles of reason.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of the world
Start with the phrase 'ما سوى الله' and notice the grammatical notes on the attribute. Understand that the world is everything created, distinct from the Creator.
The word 'عالم' is defined here as all that is other than Allah.
2
Understand the verdict on sophists
The author states that sophists have 'departed from the discourse of rational people.' This is a strong accusation—they are outside the realm of rational argument.
3
Learn the names of sophist sects
The passage identifies three sects: 'Inadiyya (from obstinacy), 'Indiyya (from subjective belief), and La-adriyya (agnostics). Understand the etymology of each.
These names reflect their core errors.
4
Consider the quoted refutation
The author quotes a view that debate is useless with La-adriyya; instead, they must be punished with fire. Reflect on why this extreme measure is proposed.
This shows the seriousness of rejecting all objective knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثمَّ فسره بقوله : (أي ما ) أي : الشَّيءُ الذي هو (سوى الله العلي العالما) - نعت الله تعالى على القطع، فهو منصوب على المدح، وألفه للإطلاق - من الجواهر . الحقُّ أنَّهم خرجوا عن قول العقلاء، كذا قرَّره المؤلّف. قوله : (عناديَّة) : سمُّوا بذلك لعنادهم ومكابرتهم لأهل الحقِّ . قوله : (يقولون : الشَّخص عند اعتقاده بيان لتسميتهم عنديَّة، وكذا يقال فيما بعده. قوله : (وتوضيح الرَّدِّ عليهم مذكور في المطوّلات): قال صاحب العقائد بعد كلام طويل: (الحقُّ أنَّه لا طريق إلى المناظرة معهم خصوصاً اللا أدرية ؛ لأنَّهم لا يعترفون بمعلوم ليثبت به مجهول، بل الطريق تعذيبهم بالنار ليعترفوا أو يحترقوا).
Then he explained it by his saying: 'i.e., what' meaning the thing that is 'other than Allah, the High, the All-Knowing' – an attribute of Allah Almighty in the absolute sense, so it is accusative for praise, and its alif is for unrestrictedness – from the substances. The truth is that they have departed from the discourse of rational people, thus the author decided. His saying: 'Inadiyya': they were named thus because of their obstinacy and opposition to the people of truth. His saying: 'they say: a person is according to his belief' is an explanation of their naming 'Indiyya, and similarly it is said in what follows. His saying: 'And the clarification of the refutation against them is mentioned in the longer works': the author of al-'Aqa'id said after a long speech: 'The truth is that there is no way to debate with them, especially the La-adriyya, because they do not acknowledge any known thing by which an unknown can be established; rather the way is to torture them with fire so that they may acknowledge or burn.'

Commentary
This passage provides the technical definition of the world (العالم) as 'what is other than Allah' (ما سوى الله), with grammatical notes: the phrase 'the High, the All-Knowing' is an attribute of Allah in the absolute sense, accusative for praise, with an alif for unrestrictedness. It then affirms that the Sophists have left the realm of rational discourse (قول العقلاء). The author then explains the names of the Sophist sects: 'Inadiyya from obstinacy and opposition, 'Indiyya from the principle of subjective belief, and then quotes a refutation from the author of al-'Aqa'id arguing that such people cannot be debated rationally and must be forced by punishment with fire. The commentary follows the order: definition, verdict on sophists, etymology of names, and final refutation.

Substance and Accident: Foundations of Kalam
Sanusi defines جوهر and عرض and asserts the certainty of the world's temporal origination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of substance and accident as foundational terms in kalam, and the rational certainty of the world's temporal origination.

Core Idea
Substance subsists by itself; accident subsists by another; the world's beginning is undoubted.

Before entering deeper proofs, Sanusi establishes the basic vocabulary: everything that exists is either a substance or an accident. A substance is self-subsisting, while an accident inheres in a substance. From these definitions he moves directly to a central claim: the world's temporal origination is beyond doubt for any reflective person.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's definitions and claim come through.
Notice the concise style.
2
Identify the two definitions
Find the definitions of جوهر and عرض. Note the key phrases: ما قام بنفسه and ما قام بغيره.
The example of colors helps clarify accident.
3
Examine the certainty claim
Look at the phrase 'من غير شك'. It modifies 'حادث' (temporal). Ask yourself: why is this certainty emphasized?
This shows the author's confidence in the argument.
4
Reflect on the structure
Why start with definitions before proving temporality? Consider that clear terms are necessary for a sound proof.
This lesson lays the groundwork for later proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأعراض، والجوهر: ما قام بنفسه والعَرَض : ما قام بغيره من الجواهر كالألوان (من غير شكٍّ) متعلّق بقوله : (حادثُ) أي : موجود بعد عدم، وهو خبر (أنَّ) أي : إنَّ حدوثه غير مشكوك فيه لمن تأمل،
And the accidents, and the substance: that which subsists by itself, and the accident: that which subsists by other than itself from the substances, like colors, (without doubt) related to His statement: (temporal) i.e., existent after non-existence, and it is the predicate of (that) i.e., that its temporal origination is not doubtful to one who reflects.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage is a definition and assertion. The author defines the substance (الجوهر) as that which subsists by itself, and the accident (العرض) as that which subsists by other than itself, giving colors as an example. He then states that the temporal origination of the world is without doubt for anyone who reflects, linking it to the statement of the author of the original text. The phrase 'without doubt' emphasizes the certainty of the argument.

Key Terms

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Perception of Accidents and the Veil from Sight
Sanusi enumerates sensory and intellectual perception of accidents, explains the veil, and defines accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how accidents are perceived through the senses and intellect, why some accidents are not actually seen due to a divine veil, and the definition of accident that excludes divine attributes.

Core Idea
Accidents are perceived by senses or intellect; some are veiled from sight, and none are eternal.

In this passage from his commentary, Imam Sanusi explains the various ways accidents are perceived—through taste, hearing, sight, smell, touch, and intellect—and why despite being visible in principle, some accidents like smells and sounds are not actually seen. He also clarifies the term 'accident' and why it cannot apply to God's attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to get the overall meaning.
Pay attention to how each sense is given a specific example.
2
Identify the five senses and examples
List the five senses mentioned and the accidents perceived by each. Note that some accidents are perceived only by intellect.
3
Understand the veil from sight
Notice that all accidents exist and can be seen in principle, but some are not actually seen because of a veil created by God. Reflect on examples like smells and sounds.
The reality of this veil is unknown to us.
4
Grasp the definition of accident
Read the definition: an accident is what subsists by another. Understand why this leads to the conclusion that divine attributes are not accidents because they are eternal.
The passage also criticizes those who claim the world is eternal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (والأعراض) : اعلم أنَّ بعضها يدرك بالذوق؛ كالحلاوة والملوحة والمرارة، وبعضها يدرك بالسَّمع ؛ كالأصوات، وبعضها بالبصر؛ كالألوان، وبعضها بالشَّم ؛ كالروائح، وبعضها باللمس؛ كالحرارة والبرودة، والنُّعومة والخشونة. وأمَّا مثل القدرة والإرادة والعلم الحادثة : فإنَّما تدرك بالعقل، وكذا بقيَّة المعاني، وهذه الأعراض كلُّها موجودة يصح رؤيتها، وبعضها يرى بالفعل ؛ كالألوان والأجسام، وبعضها لم ير بالفعل؛ لوجود مانع وحجاب خلقه الله تعالى من رؤيتها بالفعل لا اطلاع لنا على حقيقة ذلك الحجاب، وذلك كبعض صفات المعاني القائمة بنا والرَّوائح والأصوات ونحو ذلك . قوله : (والعرض : ما قام بغيره : سمّي عرضاً ؛ لأنَّه يعرض لما قام به ويطرأ عليه، ومن ثمَّ لا يقال في صفات الله تعالى : أعراض؛ لأنَّها أزليَّة يستحيل عليها الطُّرقُ . وقوله : (من) الجواهر) بيان لغيره. قوله : (لمن تأمل) : فيه تعريض بمن يقول : إنَّ العالم قديم؛ فإنَّه لم يتأمل .
His statement: (And the accidents): Know that some of them are perceived by taste, like sweetness, saltiness, and bitterness; some are perceived by hearing, like sounds; some by sight, like colors; some by smell, like odors; some by touch, like heat, cold, softness, and roughness. As for the likes of power, will, and temporal knowledge, they are perceived only by the intellect, and likewise the rest of the intelligible meanings. These accidents all exist and it is valid to see them, but some are actually seen, like colors and bodies, and some are not actually seen due to the existence of an obstacle and a veil created by God Almighty from actually seeing them; we have no knowledge of the reality of that veil. That is like some attributes of meanings subsisting in us, and odors, sounds, and the like. His statement: (The accident: that which subsists by other than itself): it is called accident because it befalls that which it subsists in and occurs to it; hence it is not said of God's attributes that they are accidents because they are eternal and it is impossible for them to occur. And His statement: (from) the substances is an explanation of 'other than itself.' His statement: (for one who reflects) contains a criticism of those who say that the world is eternal, for they have not reflected.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory commentary (تفسير وتحقيق) on the categories of accidents and the rationale behind naming. The author begins by listing the sensory modalities through which accidents are perceived: taste, hearing, sight, smell, and touch, giving examples for each. He then notes that some accidents like power, will, and temporal knowledge are only perceived by the intellect, along with other intelligible meanings. He states that all accidents exist and are potentially visible, but while some are actually seen (like colors and bodies), others are not actually seen due to an obstacle and veil created by God, whose reality is unknown to us – examples include some mental attributes in us, smells, sounds, etc. He then provides the rationale for calling accidents 'عراض' (from عرض meaning 'to occur' or 'to happen'), emphasizing that they are temporal and occur to substances. Therefore, divine attributes are not called accidents because they are eternal. Finally, he interprets the phrase 'for one who reflects' as a indirect criticism of those who claim the world is eternal, for they have not reflected.

اللمس

الإرادة
العلم الحادثة
الحجاب
الطُّرق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think that divine attributes are accidents because they are attributes of God.
Correction: The passage clarifies that God's attributes are not accidents because they are eternal, while accidents are temporal events that occur in substances.

Checkpoint
Why are God's attributes not called 'accidents'?
A) Because they are not perceived by the senses
B) Because they are temporal
C) Because they are eternal and cannot be described as occurring
D) Because they are substances

Key Takeaway
Accidents are temporal phenomena perceived by senses or intellect; God's eternal attributes are not accidents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The distinction between accidents (temporal) and divine attributes (eternal) guards against equating creation with Creator. In what ways do you sometimes mistakenly treat created things as if they were eternal or ultimate? What step will you take today to reorient your heart towards the Eternal?

The Obligation to Know God's Attributes and the Definition of Knowledge
Commentary on the obligation to know God's attributes and the definition of knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that every legally responsible person must know the three categories of divine attributes (necessary, impossible, permissible) and what constitutes true knowledge in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
True knowledge is certain belief, based on evidence, that matches reality.

Before diving into detailed discussions of God's attributes, the commentator first establishes the foundational obligation: every mukallaf must know what is necessary, impossible, and permissible for God. He then clarifies the path to that knowledge—the temporality of the world—and defines the very nature of knowledge itself. This lesson unpacks these essential starting points.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the commentator's style: he is glossing key phrases from an earlier text.
The passage begins with a chapter heading and then comments on parts of a statement.
2
Identify the three categories of attributes
The passage states that every mukallaf must know three things about God: what is wajib (necessary), mustahil (impossible), and ja'iz (permissible). These are the three rational judgments in kalam.
These categories are the backbone of all discussions about God's attributes.
3
Understand the path to knowledge
The commentator identifies 'the path leading to knowledge' as the temporality of the world (huduth al-alam). This means reflecting on the created nature of the universe leads to knowledge of God.
This is a key methodological point: knowledge of God is attained through rational proof, not blind faith.
4
Grasp the definition of knowledge
The passage defines knowledge (al-ilm) as 'certain belief conforming to truth derived from evidence' (al-jazzm al-mutabiq li-l-haqq 'an mujib). Break down each component: certainty, correspondence, and evidence.
This definition distinguishes true knowledge from mere opinion or tradition.
5
Reflect on the misconception
The common misunderstanding is that any belief suffices. However, the commentator stresses that only certain knowledge based on evidence is acceptable in this art.
This underlines the rational and evidential nature of Islamic theology.

The Three Divisions of Kalam Studies
Classification and prioritization in kalam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three-fold classification of kalam studies (ilahiyyat, nubuwwat, sam'iyyat) and the rationale for beginning with divine matters and specifically with existence.

Core Idea
Kalam divides into divine, prophetic, and revealed matters, starting with God's existence.

This passage from al-Kharida provides a clear outline of the topics covered in kalam. It introduces the three main categories and explains why the author begins with divine matters and within them, with existence. Understanding this structure is key to following the rest of the text.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the three categories listed at the beginning.
2
Identify the three divisions
Find the three Arabic terms: الإلهيات, النبوءات, السمعيات. Understand what each covers.
Ilahiyyat deals with God, nubuwwat with prophets, sam'iyyat with revealed doctrines.
3
Understand the priority
Notice that the author prioritizes ilahiyyat because it pertains to God, and within that, he starts with the necessary attributes, giving precedence to existence.
Existence is the most fundamental attribute.
4
Reflect on the methodology
Think about why kalam is organized this way. How does starting with existence shape the rest of the discussion?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما كانت مباحث هذا الفنّ ثلاثة : إلهيَّات : وهو ما يتعلَّق بالإله من واجب وجائز ومستحيل. ونبوءات: وهو ما يتعلق بالأنبياء مما يجب لهم وما يستحيل وما يجوز . وسمعيات : وهي ما دلَّ عليها النَّقل فقط، ولا مدخل للعقل فيها ؛ كالحشر والنشر والصراط والجنَّة والنار . وتقدَّم ذكرها إجمالاً في قوله (۱): وواجب شرعاً على المكلف ... إلخ . مطلب في الإلهيات] . . . . . شرع الآن يفصل ما أجمله مقدماً للإلهيَّات، لتعلقها بالحقِّ، وما تعلق به مقدَّم على غيره، وبدأ من الإلهيَّات بالواجب لشرفه، مقدماً للوجود لأصالته؛ فإنَّ ما سواه مفرَّع عليه .
Since the investigations of this art are three: ilāhiyyāt—which pertains to the Divine in terms of what is necessary, permissible, and impossible; nubuwwāt—which pertains to the prophets in terms of what is necessary for them, what is impossible, and what is permissible; and samʿiyyāt—which are those matters indicated only by transmission and reason has no entry in them, such as the gathering, the resurrection, the bridge, Paradise, and Hell. And their mention preceded in summary in his saying: 'And it is legally obligatory upon the legally responsible person... etc.' [Now he begins] to detail what he summarized, giving precedence to ilāhiyyāt due to its connection with the Truth, and what is connected to Him takes precedence over others. And he began from ilāhiyyāt with the necessary due to its honor, giving precedence to existence due to its primacy; for whatever is other than it is derived from it.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage classifies the topics of kalam into three categories. First, ilāhiyyāt (divine matters) concerning God, covering the necessary, permissible, and impossible. Second, nubuwwāt (prophetic matters) concerning the prophets and what is necessary, impossible, and permissible for them. Third, samʿiyyāt (audible matters) which are based solely on transmitted revelation and reason has no independent role; examples include the events of the afterlife such as gathering, resurrection, the bridge (ṣirāṭ), Paradise, and Hell. The author notes that these were summarized earlier. Now, the author begins a detailed exposition, prioritizing ilāhiyyāt because they pertain directly to God. Within ilāhiyyāt, he starts with the necessary attributes, beginning with existence (wujūd) because it is the most fundamental and everything else is derived from it.

السمعيات
