الحق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sam'iyyat (revelation-based matters) are less important than the other two categories.
Correction: The passage includes sam'iyyat as a necessary third division of kalam, showing that matters like the afterlife are integral to the discipline, even though reason cannot independently establish them.

Key Takeaway
Kalam is structured into three areas: divine matters, prophetic matters, and revealed doctrines; the study begins with God's existence as the foundation.

The Three Subdivisions of Kalam
Sanusi enumerates the three branches of kalam: Ilahiyyat, Nubuwwat, Sam'iyyat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three branches of kalam (Ilahiyyat, Nubuwwat, Sam'iyyat) and what each one studies.

Core Idea
Kalam is organized around God, prophets, and revealed eschatology.

Before diving into theological arguments, Sanusi first maps the entire field of kalam into three distinct domains. This classification tells us what topics we will encounter and how each is approached. The passage below lays out this tripartite structure clearly.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation to get a sense of the structure.
Notice the three categories are introduced by a colon and separated by periods.
2
Identify the three categories
Find the three terms: Ilahiyyat, Nubuwwat, Sam'iyyat. Note how each is defined with examples.
Each category has its own set of rational judgments (necessary, possible, impossible).
3
Understand the uniqueness of Sam'iyyat
Sam'iyyat are matters that only revelation can inform us about. Reason alone cannot access them. This is a key epistemic point.
Examples given are resurrection, bridge, paradise, hellfire.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما كانت مباحث هذا الفنّ ثلاثة : إلهيَّات : وهو ما يتعلَّق بالإله من واجب وجائز ومستحيل. ونبوءات: وهو ما يتعلق بالأنبياء مما يجب لهم وما يستحيل وما يجوز . وسمعيات : وهي ما دلَّ عليها النَّقل فقط، ولا مدخل للعقل فيها ؛ كالحشر والنشر والصراط والجنَّة والنار . وتقدَّم ذكرها إجمالاً في قوله (١): وواجب شرعاً على المكلف ... إلخ .
And since the topics of this art are three: Ilahiyyat (divine matters): which is what relates to the Divine in terms of necessary, possible, and impossible. And Nubuwwat (prophetic matters): which is what relates to the prophets in terms of what is necessary for them, what is impossible, and what is possible. And Sam'iyyat (scriptural matters): which are those indicated only by transmitted revelation, with no role for reason in them; like the resurrection, the scattering, the bridge, paradise, and hellfire. And their mention has preceded in summary in his statement: 'And it is legally obligatory upon the responsible person... etc.'
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage classifies the subject matter of kalam into three categories. First, Ilahiyyat deals with God and what is necessary (wajib), possible (ja'iz), and impossible (mustahil) for Him. Second, Nubuwwat deals with prophets and similar categories of what is necessary, impossible, and possible for them. Third, Sam'iyyat deals with matters known only through revelation and not by reason alone, such as the events of the afterlife. The author notes that these subdivisions were previously summarized in the opening lines of the text.

The First Definition of the Self-Subsistent Attribute
The commentator explains the first definition of al-sifa al-nafsiyya and distinguishes it from al-ma'ani and al-ma'nawiyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first definition of al-sifa al-nafsiyya and how it excludes the categories of al-ma'ani and al-ma'nawiyya.

Core Idea
The self-subsistent attribute describes the essence itself, without any added meaning.

This lesson introduces the foundational definition of the self-subsistent attribute (al-sifa al-nafsiyya). The commentator unpacks the definition phrase by phrase, showing exactly which kinds of attributes are excluded. Understanding this distinction is key to grasping the classification of divine attributes in Ash'ari kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Notice the two key phrases: 'upon the essence itself' and 'without an additional meaning'.
2
Identify what each phrase excludes
The passage explains that 'upon the essence itself' excludes al-ma'ani (like power and will), and 'without an additional meaning' excludes al-ma'nawiyya. Understand why each category fails the definition.
Al-ma'ani are existential attributes, al-ma'nawiyya are affirmative relational states.
3
Reflect on the commentator's remark
The commentator notes that the second phrase is technically redundant because the first phrase already excludes both categories. Consider why it was included anyway (for clarification).
This shows the precision and pedagogical care of the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصفة النفسية على نفس الذات دون معنى زائد عليها. قوله : ( على نفس الذَّات :أي: لا على معنى زائد عليها، وخرج به المعاني ؛ نحو القدرة والإرادة ؛ فإنَّ الوصف بها يدلُّ على معنى زائد على الذات. وقوله : (دون معنى زائد عليها خرج به المعنوية، وفي الحقيقة خرج بقوله: (على نفس الذات المعاني والمعنوية؛ لأَنَّ كلّاً منهما لا يدلُّ الوصف به على نفس الذَّات ، ولا دلالة لهما عليها، وإنما يدلان على معنى زائد عليها، إلَّا أنَّ هذا المعنى الزَّائد في المعاني وجودي، وفي المعنوية ثبوتي إذا علمت ذلك فقوله : (دون معنی زائد عليها مستدرك لا حاجة إليه إلَّا أن يقال : أتى به للإيضاح .
The self-subsistent attribute (al-sifa al-nafsiyya) is [that which] is upon the essence itself without an additional meaning. His saying: (upon the essence itself) means: not upon an additional meaning, and by it the meanings (al-ma'ani) are excluded, such as power and will; for the description by them indicates an additional meaning upon the essence. And his saying: (without an additional meaning) excludes the meaning-related (al-ma'nawiyya). In reality, by his saying: (upon the essence itself) both the meanings and the meaning-related are excluded; because each of them does not indicate by the description upon the essence itself, nor do they indicate upon it, but rather they indicate upon an additional meaning upon it, except that this additional meaning in the meanings is existential (wujudi), and in the meaning-related is affirmative (thubuti). If you know that, then his saying: (without an additional meaning) is redundant (mustadrak), no need for it except that it is said: he brought it for clarification.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage explains the first definition of the self-subsistent attribute (al-sifa al-nafsiyya). The definition states that the self-subsistent attribute is that which describes the essence itself without any additional meaning. The commentator then analyzes two phrases from the definition. First, 'upon the essence itself' means the attribute does not point to a meaning separate from the essence. This excludes the 'meanings' (al-ma'ani), like power and will, because describing God by power indicates an additional meaning (the attribute of power) beyond the essence. Second, 'without an additional meaning' excludes the 'meaning-related' (al-ma'nawiyya) attributes, which are relational states caused by the meanings. The commentator notes that, strictly speaking, the phrase 'without an additional meaning' is redundant because the phrase 'upon the essence itself' already excludes both meanings and meaning-related attributes. However, it is added for clarification. The commentator also distinguishes between existential (wujudi) and affirmative (thubuti) aspects of these additional meanings.

Key Terms
الصفة النفسية (al-sifa al-nafsiyya)
المعاني (al-ma'ani)
المعنوية (al-ma'nawiyya)
وجودي (wujudi)
ثبوتي (thubuti)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'without an additional meaning' means the attribute has no reality or is purely negative.
Correction: It means the attribute does not point to a separate meaning beyond the essence; it still affirms a real attribute that is identical with the essence in existence.

Checkpoint
According to the first definition of al-sifa al-nafsiyya, what does 'upon the essence itself' exclude?
A) The attributes of meanings (al-ma'ani)
B) The meaning-related attributes (al-ma'nawiyya)
C) Both al-ma'ani and al-ma'nawiyya
D) Only negative attributes

Key Takeaway
The self-subsistent attribute is defined by its direct reference to the essence, distinguishing it from attributes that indicate an added meaning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If describing God by attributes like 'power' can subtly imply addition to His essence, what does that reveal about the limits of human language? Will you pause to purify your thoughts when reciting His names?
?
The commentator calls the second phrase 'redundant but clarifying.' In your own faith, which teachings about God do you accept even though they seem redundant? Does your heart rest in that redundancy or rebel against it?

Second Definition of the Self-Subsistent Attribute
Commentator explains the second definition of al-sifa al-nafsiyya with phrase-by-phrase commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second definition of al-sifa al-nafsiyya as a necessary state not caused by a separate attribute, and how it differs from meaning-related attributes (al-ma'nawiyya).

Core Idea
A self-subsistent attribute is a necessary state of the essence, not caused by a separate meaning.

This lesson unpacks the second definition of the self-subsistent attribute (al-sifa al-nafsiyya) as presented by later theologians. The definition emphasizes that such an attribute is a state necessary for the essence as long as the essence exists, and it is not caused by a separate cause. The commentator explains each phrase and clarifies how this definition excludes meaning-related attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Pay attention to the definition and examples given.
Notice the examples: existence, spatial occupation, substantiality, thingness.
2
Understand the key phrases
The commentator breaks down the definition: 'state necessary for the essence', 'as long as the essence exists', 'not caused'. Understand what each part adds.
'Not caused' is crucial to exclude meaning-related attributes.
3
Distinguish nafsiyya from ma'nawiyya
The commentator explains that 'not caused' excludes al-hal al-ma'nawiyya (like being knowing or powerful). These are caused by the subsistence of separate attributes (knowledge, power).
The commentary also notes that 'as long as the essence exists' already excludes ma'nawiyya, creating a nuance.
4
Ponder the subtlety
The commentator ends with 'so let one ponder'. Reflect on why 'not caused' might seem redundant and how the commentator resolves this.
The resolution is that 'caused' here means 'concomitant' (talazum), not efficient causation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقال أيضاً: الحال الواجبة للذات ما دامت الذات معللة غير بعلة، وذلك كالوجود والتَّحيز للجرم، وكون الجوهر جوهراً، والشّيء شيئاً، فهذا تعريف للنّفسيَّة مطلقاً، قديمة كانت أو حادثة. قوله : (ويقال أيضاً) : هذا التّعريف هو المشهور بين المتأخرين ؛ كالسنوسي وغيره. قوله : (هي الحال الواجبة للذَّات) : أي : الثَّابتة لها خرج السَّلبيَّة والمعاني . قوله : (ما دامت الذَّات أي مدَّة دوامها فما مصدرية ظرفية، وهذا الدوام واجب بالنّسبة للقديم جائز بالنسبة للحادث. وقوله في التعريف الثاني : (غير معللة) بالنصب على أنَّه حال من الحال، أو من الضَّمير في (واجبة)، واحترز به من الحال المعنوية، ككون الذات عالمة أو قادرة أو مريدة ؛ فإنَّها معللة بقيام العلم والقدرة والإرادة بالذات، فليتأمل. قوله : (واحترز به عن المعنوية فيه شيء؛ لأنَّ المعنوية خارجة بقوله : (ما دامت الذات .... إلخ؛ فإنَّ المعنوية هي الحال الواجب للذات ما دامت المعاني قائمة بالذات. قوله : (فإنَّها معلَّلة بقيام العلم) : أي : ملازمة لها، فالمراد بالتعليل التَّلازم ؛ أي: أنَّ المعنويَّة ملازمة للمعاني، فيلزم من قيام القدرة بالذات كون تلك الذات قادرة، وهكذا. قوله : ( فليتأمل): أمر بالتأمل لدقة المقام.
And it is also said: it is the state (al-hal) necessary for the essence as long as the essence [exists], [while being] not caused by a cause (ghayr mu'allala). Examples include existence (al-wujud) and spatial occupation (al-tahayyuz) for a body, the substantiality of a substance, and the thingness of a thing. So this is a definition of al-nafsiyya absolutely, whether it is eternal or originated. His saying: (and it is also said): this definition is the well-known one among the later scholars, such as al-Sanusi and others. His saying: (it is the state necessary for the essence): i.e., established for it; it excludes the negative attributes (al-salbayya) and the meanings (al-ma'ani). His saying: (as long as the essence exists): i.e., the duration of its continuance; 'ma' is a circumstantial infinitive particle. This continuance is necessary for the eternal and possible for the originated. And his saying in the second definition: (not caused) (ghayr mu'allala) – it is in the accusative as a circumstantial qualifier of 'the state' or of the pronoun in 'necessary'. He guarded by it against the meaning-related state (al-hal al-ma'nawiyya), such as the essence being knowing or powerful or willing; for these are caused by the subsistence of knowledge, power, and will in the essence. So let one ponder (falyatadabbar). His saying: (and he guarded by it against the meaning-related) – there is something [to note] about this; for the meaning-related is already excluded by his saying: (as long as the essence exists)... etc.; for the meaning-related is the state necessary for the essence as long as the meanings subsist in the essence. His saying: (for these are caused by the subsistence of knowledge): i.e., they are concomitant to it; so by causation is meant concomitance (talazum); i.e., the meaning-related is concomitant to the meanings, so from the subsistence of power in the essence it follows that that essence is powerful, and similarly. His saying: (so let one ponder): he commands pondering due to the subtlety of the station.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage presents and explains the second definition of the self-subsistent attribute (al-sifa al-nafsiyya). The definition states that it is a state (hal) that is necessary for the essence as long as the essence exists, and it is not caused by a cause. Examples include existence for a body, spatial occupation, substantiality, and thingness. This definition applies to both eternal and originated attributes. The commentator then explains key phrases. First, 'it is also said' indicates this is the well-known definition among later theologians like al-Sanusi. Second, 'the state necessary for the essence' means that which is established for it, excluding negative attributes and the meanings (attributes that denote separate meanings). Third, 'as long as the essence exists' indicates the duration of the essence's continuance. Fourth, 'not caused' (ghayr mu'allala) excludes the meaning-related states (like being knowing) that are caused by the subsistence of knowledge, power, etc. The commentator then notes that the phrase 'not caused' might seem redundant because the meaning-related states are already excluded by 'as long as the essence exists' since they depend on the subsistence of meanings. He clarifies that by 'caused' here is meant 'concomitant' (talazum), so the meaning-related state is concomitant to the meaning attribute. Finally, the commentator urges pondering this subtlety.

Key Terms
الحال (al-hal)
واجبة (wajiba)
الذات (al-dhat)
معللة (mu'allala)
بعلة (bi-'illa)
معنوية (ma'nawiyya)
تلازم (talazum)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'not caused' means the attribute has no cause at all.
Correction: It specifically excludes attributes caused by a separate meaning subsisting in the essence. The commentator clarifies that 'caused' here means 'concomitant' (talazum), not efficient causation.

Checkpoint
What does the phrase 'not caused' (ghayr mu'allala) exclude in the second definition?
A) Negative attributes
B) The meaning-related states (al-ma'nawiyya)
C) The attribute of existence
D) The essence itself

Key Takeaway
The second definition of al-sifa al-nafsiyya clarifies that it is a necessary state of the essence itself, not caused by a separate attribute, distinguishing it from meaning-related attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your own attributes, do you see some as inherently part of you (like your existence) and others as dependent on external factors? How does this distinction help you understand that Allah's attributes are not caused by anything outside Himself?
?
The commentator says 'let one ponder' because of the subtlety of differentiating between the essence and its attributes. In your daily life, what subtle distinctions about yourself do you tend to overlook that could lead to wrong self-understanding?
?
Imagine you are tempted to think that Allah's knowledge is like human knowledge, caused by learning something. How does the concept of 'ghayr mu'allala' correct that assumption, and what does that mean for how you relate to Allah's omniscience?

Defining Sifa Nafsiyya: The Phrase 'غير معللة' and Its Implications
Commentator explains the phrase 'غير معللة' in the definition of sifa nafsiyya and its distinction from sifa ma'nawiyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the significance of the phrase 'غير معللة' in distinguishing the essential attribute (sifa nafsiyya) from the meaning-based state (sifa ma'nawiyya) in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
A sifa nafsiyya is not caused by a separate meaning, unlike sifa ma'nawiyya.

The commentator al-Sawi delves into the precise wording of the definition of the essential attribute. He explains that the phrase 'غير معللة' serves a critical role in excluding the meaning-based attributes, but a careful reading reveals a subtle redundancy. This lesson unpacks the grammatical and theological nuances of this passage.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to get a sense of the overall meaning.
Pay attention to the technical terms like حال, معنوية, تعليل.
2
Understand the grammatical case of 'غير معللة'
Notice that the commentator explains 'غير معللة' is in the accusative case because it is a circumstantial accusative from 'the state' or from the pronoun in 'necessary'.
This grammatical point is essential for the correct reading of the definition.
3
Identify what the phrase guards against
The phrase guards against the meaning-based state (sifa ma'nawiyya). The commentator gives examples: knowing, powerful, willing.
The commentator then notes a potential redundancy: the meaning-based state is already excluded by another phrase in the definition.
4
Grasp the redefinition of 'تعليل' as 'تلازم'
The commentator clarifies that 'causation' here means 'concomitance': the meaning-based state is not caused in a true sense but is concomitant to the meanings.
This resolves the apparent redundancy and deepens the understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله في التعريف الثاني : (غير معللة) بالنصب على أنَّه حال من الحال، أو من الضَّمير في (واجبة)، واحترز به من الحال المعنوية، ككون الذات عالمة أو قادرة أو مريدة ؛ فإنَّها معللة بقيام العلم والقدرة والإرادة بالذات، فليتأمل. قوله: (واحترز به عن المعنوية فيه شيء؛ لأنَّ المعنوية خارجة بقوله : (ما دامت الذات .... إلخ؛ فإنَّ المعنوية هي الحال الواجب للذات ما دامت المعاني قائمة بالذات. قوله : (فإنَّها معلَّلة بقيام العلم) : أي : ملازمة لها، فالمراد بالتعليل التَّلازم ؛ أي: أنَّ المعنويَّة ملازمة للمعاني، فيلزم من قيام القدرة بالذات كون تلك الذات قادرة، وهكذا. قوله : ( فليتأمل): أمر بالتأمل لدقة المقام.
His saying in the second definition: (not caused) in the accusative as a circumstantial accusative from 'the state', or from the pronoun in 'necessary', and he guarded thereby against the meaning-based state, such as the essence being knowing or powerful or willing; for they are caused by the subsistence of knowledge, power, and will in the essence, so let it be pondered. His saying: (He guarded against the meaning-based state) there is something in it; because the meaning-based state is excluded by his saying: (as long as the essence ... etc.; for the meaning-based state is the state necessary for the essence as long as the meanings subsist in the essence. His saying: (For they are caused by the subsistence of knowledge) meaning: concomitant to it, so the intended meaning of causation is concomitance; i.e., the meaning-based state is concomitant to the meanings, so from the subsistence of power in the essence follows that that essence is powerful, and so on. His saying: (So let it be pondered) is a command to ponder because of the subtlety of the station.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of the essential attribute (al-ṣifa al-nafsiyya). It explains the phrase 'غير معللة' (not caused) in the second definition. The commentator states that 'غير معللة' is in the accusative case because it is a circumstantial accusative from 'the state' or from the pronoun in 'necessary' (واجبة). The phrase serves to exclude the meaning-based state (al-ḥāl al-maʿnawiyya), such as the essence being knowing, powerful, or willing. These are caused (معللة) by the subsistence of knowledge, power, and will in the essence. Then the commentator notes that there is something to consider in this exclusion: the meaning-based state is actually already excluded by the phrase 'ما دامت الذات' (as long as the essence). The meaning-based state is the state necessary for the essence as long as the meanings subsist in the essence. Then the commentator explains that the 'causation' (تعليل) here means concomitance (تلازم): the meaning-based state is concomitant to the meanings. For example, from the subsistence of power in the essence, it necessarily follows that the essence is powerful. Finally, the commentator commands us to ponder (فليتأمل) because of the subtlety of this topic.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'غير معللة' means the attribute has no cause at all, implying absolute independence.
Correction: The commentator clarifies that it means not caused by a separate meaning as an internal cause; rather, the attribute is concomitant to the essence without requiring a distinct cause.

Checkpoint

B) The meaning-based state (ma'nawiyya)

D) The state of being non-existent

Key Takeaway
The phrase 'غير معللة' is crucial to distinguish sifa nafsiyya from sifa ma'nawiyya, but it also introduces a subtle redefinition of causality as concomitance.

The commentator reinterprets causation as concomitance to avoid redundancy. Have you ever glossed over a contradiction or redundancy in your own beliefs instead of seeking a deeper understanding? Resolve to be honest with yourself.

Al-Qiyam bi al-Nafs: Definition and Fourfold Division
Al-Sawi defines self-subsistence and presents a fourfold classification of beings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise meaning of al-qiyam bi al-nafs (self-subsistence) and how beings are classified into four categories based on their dependence on a locus and a specifier.

Core Idea
Self-subsistence means being free from need for both a locus and a specifier.

Al-Sawi begins by defining a key theological term: al-qiyam bi al-nafs, often rendered as 'self-subsistence.' He clarifies that it does not merely mean 'existing independently' but has a precise technical meaning: the negation of need for a locus (mahall) in which the thing inheres, and for a specifier (mukhasis) that makes it particular. After stating the definition, he immediately applies it to classify all entities into four categories, from the divine essence to created accidents. This passage is foundational for understanding the ontological distinction between God, His attributes, and creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage slowly, then its English translation. Notice how the definition is stated, and then the fourfold division enumerated.
The passage is short but dense. Read it twice: once for the definition, once for the classification.
2
Understand the key terms
Identify the three key terms: al-qiyam bi al-nafs, al-mahall (locus), and al-mukhasis (specifier). Understand that 'negation of need' is the core idea.
A locus is something a quality inheres in; a specifier makes something distinct.
3
Analyze the fourfold division
Break down the four categories: independent of both (God's essence), independent of specifier only (God's attributes), dependent on both (our attributes), dependent on specifier only (our essences). Ask why each category is placed where it is.
God's attributes are independent of a specifier because they are not contingent, but they do not need a locus because they are not accidents inhering in a subject? Actually, the text says they are independent of specifier only, implying they do not need a specific cause to individuate them, but they are not independent of a locus? Wait: re-read carefully: 'MustaGhnin 'an al-mukhaSSiS faqaT' – independent of specifier only. That means they are not independent of a locus? But the attributes of God are not accidents inhering in a locus; they are eternal attributes of the essence. This needs careful explanation in commentary.
4
Connect to broader theology
Reflect on how this classification safeguards divine transcendence and provides a vocabulary for speaking about God and creation.
This classification is used to refute pantheism and anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القيام بالنفس بمعنى : سلب الافتقار إلى المحل أو المخصص ؛ أي: الفاعل. قوله : (بمعنى سلب الافتقار إلى المحل أو المخصص) : واعلم : أنَّ القسمة رباعية : مستغن عن المحل والمخصص معاً، وهو ذات الله، ومستغن عن المخصّص فقط، وهو صفات الله تعالى، ومفتقر إليهما معاً، وهو صفاتنا، ومفتقر إلى المخصّص فقط، وهو ذواتنا .
Self-subsistence means: negation of need for a locus or a specifier, i.e., an agent. His statement: 'meaning negation of need for a locus or a specifier': Know that the division is fourfold: independent of both locus and specifier, and that is the essence of God; independent of the specifier only, and that is the attributes of God (Exalted is He); in need of both together, and that is our attributes; in need of the specifier only, and that is our essences.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage defines the technical term al-qiyam bi al-nafs (self-subsistence) and then classifies all possible entities into four categories based on their need for a locus (mahall) and a specifier (mukhasis). The author first states the definition: self-subsistence means the thing's not being in need of either a locus in which it inheres or a specifier that makes it particular. Then he comments on the phrase 'meaning negation of need for a locus or a specifier' by presenting a fourfold division. (1) That which is independent of both locus and specifier: this is the essence (dhat) of God. (2) That which is independent of a specifier only, but does not need a locus: these are the attributes of God. (3) That which is in need of both locus and specifier: these are our attributes (the accidents of created beings). (4) That which is in need of a specifier only, but not a locus: these are our essences (created substances). The passage thus clarifies the ontological status of God, His attributes, and created entities with respect to dependence.

Key Terms
القيام بالنفس
المحل
المخصص
القسمة الرباعية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think 'self-subsistence' simply means 'existing by itself' without further qualification, leading to confusion about how created substances can also be self-subsistent in a sense.
Correction: The passage clarifies that al-qiyam bi al-nafs is not merely independent existence; it specifically negates need for both a locus and a specifier. This prevents anthropomorphism and clarifies that only God's essence is fully self-subsistent.

Checkpoint
According to al-Sawi's division, which category includes the attributes of God?
A) Independent of both locus and specifier
B) Independent of specifier only
C) In need of both
D) In need of specifier only

Key Takeaway
Self‑subsistence (al‑qiyam bi al‑nafs) is defined as independence from a locus and a specifier, and all beings fall into one of four categories based on this definition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that only God's essence is absolutely independent—needing neither a locus nor a specifier. Everything else, including your very existence, depends on a specifier (God) every moment. When you wake up tomorrow, what will you consciously do to remind yourself that you are not self-sufficient, and that your next breath is given, not owned? Be specific about a practice you will start or change.
?
Your attributes—like your intelligence, health, or ability to reason—are dependent on both a locus (your body and soul) and a specifier (God's ongoing creation). If you were to truly accept this, how would it change the way you react to praise or to failure? What concrete thought can you rehearse the next time you feel proud of an achievement?
?
This passage divides all reality into four categories, and you fall into the fourth: in need of a specifier only (your essence) and in need of both (your attributes). Only One is in the first category. Does your heart actually rest in that One, or do you functionally seek security in things that are themselves dependent (your job, relationships, health)? What is one practical step you can take this week to transfer your trust from dependent things to the Independent One?

Definition of Ikhtiyār and Jabr in Human Actions
Sanusi defines ikhtiyār and jabr and states the basis for attributing actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari definitions of ikhtiyār (free choice) and jabr (compulsion) and how human actions are attributed to the servant.

Core Idea
Ikhtiyār is concurrent power without causal influence; jabr is action without concurrent power.

Sanusi begins by clarifying two key terms that form the basis of the Ash'ari position on human agency. He explains that the 'attachment' of power to an act is merely a concomitance, not a causal relation. This leads to the distinction between the free-chooser (mukhtār) and the compelled (majbūr).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify definitions
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Focus on the definitions of 'attachment', 'free-chooser', and 'compelled'.
2
Understand 'attachment'
Note that 'ta'alluq' is defined as muqārana (concomitance) without ta'thīr (influence). This is the core of Ash'ari occasionalism.
The power does not cause the act; it merely accompanies it.
3
Examine the scriptural basis
The Quranic verse (al-Baqara 2:286) is cited to show that actions are attributed to the servant based on this concurrent power.
The verse uses 'kasaba' and 'iktasaba' implying earning.
4
Contrast mukhtār and majbūr
Understand when the servant is called mukhtār (concurrent power) vs. majbūr (no concurrent power). This distinction underpins reward and punishment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فعلم أنَّ هذا التعلُّق عبارة عن مقارنة القدرة الحادثة من غير تأثير، وبحسبه تضاف الأفعال للعبد، كقوله تعالى لَهَا مَا كَسَبَتْ وَعَلَيْهَا مَا اكْتَسَبَتْ (١) ، ويترتب الثَّواب والعقاب بمحض الفضل أو العدل، ويسمى العبد حينئذ : مختاراً . وعند خلق الله تعالى الفعل في العبد بلا قدرة له مقارنة يسمى مجبوراً ومضطراً، وقد تفضَّل الله سبحانه علينا في هذه الحالة بإسقاط التكليف، ولو شاء لكلَّفنا عندها أيضاً. وما الخلق في التمثال إلَّا كثلجة لها صورة لكن تبدت عن الماء فذو الكشف لم يشهد سوى الماء وحده تبدى بوصف الثّلج من غير إخفاء إذا ظهرت شمس الوجود تُذيبها فترجعها ماءً يُجاء مع الياء ومن حجته صورةُ الثَّلج جاهل تغطّي عليه الأمر من لمع أضواء قوله : ( ويترتّب الثَّواب والعقاب) : لف ونشر مرتّب، وكذا قوله : (بمحض الفضل والعدل)، أمَّا الفضل في الثَّواب فظاهر؛ لأنَّ العبد لا يستحقُ عند الله شيئاً، وأمَّا العدل في العقاب؛ فلأنَّ الله تعالى مالك، والمالك يتصرّف في ملكه كيف يشاء، فتعذيبه عدل لا ظلم. جائز . قوله : (ولو شاء لكلَّفنا عندها أيضاً ) أي : لأنَّ التكليف بما لا يطاق
Thus it is known that this attachment is an expression of the concomitance of the originated power without influence, and according to it the actions are attributed to the servant, as His statement (may He be exalted): 'To it what it earned and against it what it acquired', and reward and punishment are arranged by pure favor or justice, and the servant is then called: free-chooser. And when Allah (may He be exalted) creates the action in the servant without a power concomitant with him, he is called compelled and constrained, and Allah (glory be to Him) has favored us in this case by dropping the obligation, and if He willed He would have obligated us also at that time. And the creation in the likeness is only like a snowball having a form but it appeared from water, so the one with unveiling saw only water alone appearing with the description of snow without hiding. When the sun of existence appears to melt it, it returns it to water... And from his evidence, the image of snow ignorant one covers upon him the matter from the luster of lights. His statement: 'And reward and punishment are arranged': it is an ordered folding and spreading, and likewise his statement: 'by pure favor and justice'. As for favor in reward, it is apparent because the servant does not deserve anything from Allah. And as for justice in punishment, because Allah (may He be exalted) is the owner, and the owner disposes in His property as He wills, so His punishment is justice, not injustice. It is permissible. His statement: 'And if He willed He would have obligated us also at that time', meaning: because obligation of what cannot be borne..
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage defines the Ash'ari view of human action. It begins by stating that the attachment of power to an act is merely concomitance, not causal influence. The act is attributed to the servant based on this concurrent power. The verse from Sura al-Baqara is cited to show that acts are ascribed to the servant. Reward and punishment are based purely on divine favor or justice. When an act is created by God without any power in the servant, the servant is called compelled, and God may drop obligation in such cases. The poetic lines illustrate the relationship: just as a snowball is essentially water, acts are essentially from God. The commentary then explains that the order of reward and punishment is a 'folding and spreading' (laf wa nashr): reward is pure favor because the servant deserves nothing, punishment is justice because God as owner may dispose as He wills. The final note says that obligating what cannot be borne is possible if God wills.

Key Terms
تعلّق
مقارنة
قدرة حادثة
تأثير
مختار
مجبر
مضطر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That ikhtiyār implies that the servant's power causally influences the act.
Correction: In Ash'ari theology, the servant's power is merely concurrent (muqārana), not causal.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the term 'attachment' (ta'alluq) mean?
A) The power causally influences the act.
B) The power is concurrent with the act without influence.
C) The act is created by the servant.
D) The servant has no power.

Key Takeaway
Ikhtiyār is defined by the presence of concurrent power, not by causal efficacy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin, do you truly believe that your created power was merely concurrent with God's creation of that act—that you had no independent causal influence? How does that realization change your attitude toward repentance and reliance on God's mercy?
?
If your every action is created by God and your 'choice' is only the presence of a concurrent power, does that make you feel trapped or liberated? How can this understanding increase your gratitude for the blessings of guidance and obedience, and deepen your fear of being left without that concurrent power?

Reward, Punishment, and Divine Justice
Sanusi explains the basis for reward and punishment and the possibility of obligating what cannot be borne.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ash'ari theology explains reward as pure favor and punishment as justice, and why God could obligate what cannot be borne.

Core Idea
Reward is favor, punishment is justice, and God's obligation is not limited by human capacity.

Building on the definitions of ikhtiyār and jabr, Sanusi addresses the theological implications regarding reward and punishment. He explains that the servant does not inherently deserve anything from God, so reward is pure favor (faḍl), while punishment is justice (ʿadl) because God is the absolute owner. He also touches on the controversial topic of obligating what cannot be borne (taklīf mā lā yuṭāq).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the key statements about reward, punishment, and obligation.
2
Analyze the basis of reward and punishment
Identify why reward is described as 'pure favor' and punishment as 'justice'. The commentary provides the reasoning.
Reward: servant deserves nothing. Punishment: God is owner.
3
Reflect on the possibility of obligating the impossible
Understand the implication of God's statement 'if He willed He would have obligated us' and how it relates to divine power and justice.
4
Review the poetic analogy
Consider the snowball analogy as a metaphor for the relationship between divine action and human action.
Snowball is water in disguise; all acts are from God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فعلم أنَّ هذا التعلُّق عبارة عن مقارنة القدرة الحادثة من غير تأثير، وبحسبه تضاف الأفعال للعبد، كقوله تعالى لَهَا مَا كَسَبَتْ وَعَلَيْهَا مَا اكْتَسَبَتْ (١) ، ويترتب الثَّواب والعقاب بمحض الفضل أو العدل، ويسمى العبد حينئذ : مختاراً . وعند خلق الله تعالى الفعل في العبد بلا قدرة له مقارنة يسمى مجبوراً ومضطراً، وقد تفضَّل الله سبحانه علينا في هذه الحالة بإسقاط التكليف، ولو شاء لكلَّفنا عندها أيضاً. وما الخلق في التمثال إلَّا كثلجة لها صورة لكن تبدت عن الماء فذو الكشف لم يشهد سوى الماء وحده تبدى بوصف الثّلج من غير إخفاء إذا ظهرت شمس الوجود تُذيبها فترجعها ماءً يُجاء مع الياء ومن حجته صورةُ الثَّلج جاهل تغطّي عليه الأمر من لمع أضواء قوله : ( ويترتّب الثَّواب والعقاب) : لف ونشر مرتّب، وكذا قوله : (بمحض الفضل والعدل)، أمَّا الفضل في الثَّواب فظاهر؛ لأنَّ العبد لا يستحقُ عند الله شيئاً، وأمَّا العدل في العقاب؛ فلأنَّ الله تعالى مالك، والمالك يتصرّف في ملكه كيف يشاء، فتعذيبه عدل لا ظلم. جائز . قوله : (ولو شاء لكلَّفنا عندها أيضاً ) أي : لأنَّ التكليف بما لا يطاق
Thus it is known that this attachment is an expression of the concomitance of the originated power without influence, and according to it the actions are attributed to the servant, as His statement (may He be exalted): 'To it what it earned and against it what it acquired', and reward and punishment are arranged by pure favor or justice, and the servant is then called: free-chooser. And when Allah (may He be exalted) creates the action in the servant without a power concomitant with him, he is called compelled and constrained, and Allah (glory be to Him) has favored us in this case by dropping the obligation, and if He willed He would have obligated us also at that time. And the creation in the likeness is only like a snowball having a form but it appeared from water, so the one with unveiling saw only water alone appearing with the description of snow without hiding. When the sun of existence appears to melt it, it returns it to water... And from his evidence, the image of snow ignorant one covers upon him the matter from the luster of lights. His statement: 'And reward and punishment are arranged': it is an ordered folding and spreading, and likewise his statement: 'by pure favor and justice'. As for favor in reward, it is apparent because the servant does not deserve anything from Allah. And as for justice in punishment, because Allah (may He be exalted) is the owner, and the owner disposes in His property as He wills, so His punishment is justice, not injustice. It is permissible. His statement: 'And if He willed He would have obligated us also at that time', meaning: because obligation of what cannot be borne..
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage defines the Ash'ari view of human action. It begins by stating that the attachment of power to an act is merely concomitance, not causal influence. The act is attributed to the servant based on this concurrent power. The verse from Sura al-Baqara is cited to show that acts are ascribed to the servant. Reward and punishment are based purely on divine favor or justice. When an act is created by God without any power in the servant, the servant is called compelled, and God may drop obligation in such cases. The poetic lines illustrate the relationship: just as a snowball is essentially water, acts are essentially from God. The commentary then explains that the order of reward and punishment is a 'folding and spreading' (laf wa nashr): reward is pure favor because the servant deserves nothing, punishment is justice because God as owner may dispose as He wills. The final note says that obligating what cannot be borne is possible if God wills.

Correction: According to Ash'ari theology, the servant does not deserve anything; reward is pure divine favor.

The Salaf and Khalaf Approaches to Ambiguous Verses
Al-Sawi explains confusion and two methods of response: tafwid and ta'wil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between the Salaf and Khalaf responses to anthropomorphic interpretations and why both affirm Allah's transcendence.

Core Idea
The Salaf delegate meaning to Allah; the Khalaf interpret symbolically—both guard divine transcendence.

In this passage, al-Sawi addresses a critical theological confusion: some people, clinging to the literal wording of scripture, wrongly attributed direction or body to Allah. He then presents the two orthodox responses—tafwid and ta'wil—showing how both protect Allah's transcendence while engaging with the revealed texts. Understanding this distinction is key to avoiding anthropomorphism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice al-Sawi's concise style and the strong exclamation 'ta'ala Allah...'.
2
Identify the confusion
Find what some people said (jiha, jism) and the necessary implications (hulul, ittisal, infisal).
These implications show why the claims are problematic.
3
Distinguish the two responses
Contrast the Salaf's tafwid (delegation) with the Khalaf's ta'wil (interpretation).
Both groups affirm tanzih (transcendence).
4
Examine the examples
Note how the Khalaf interpreted 'hand' as power and 'face' as essence.
These are sound interpretations consistent with reason and revelation.
5
Reflect on the unity
Why do both methods lead to the same conclusion?
Both protect Allah from being likened to creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واشتبه الأمر على أقوام وقوفاً مع الأمور العادية، وتمسكاً بظواهر نصوص شرعية، فقال قوم بالجهة، وقال آخرون بالجسميَّة، ويلزم منهما الحلول والاتصال أو الانفصال، تعالى الله عن ذلك علوّاً كبيراً. وأجاب أئمتُنا سَلَفُهم بأنَّ الله تعالى منزّه عن صفات الحوادث، مع تفويض معاني هذه النصوص إليه تعالى إيثاراً للطَّريق الأسلم، وما يعلم تأويله إلا الله، وخَلَفُهم بتعيين محامل صحيحة إبطالاً لمذهب الضَّالين وإرشاداً للقاصرين، فحملوا اليد على القدرة، والوجه على الذَّات.
And the matter became confused for some people, due to their adherence to customary matters and their holding fast to the apparent meanings of sacred texts. So some said [He is in a] direction, and others said [He is] corporeal, and from these two necessarily follow indwelling and conjoining or separation. Exalted is Allah above that, a great exaltation. And our imams, their predecessors (Salaf), answered that Allah, exalted is He, is transcendent above the attributes of contingents, while delegating the meanings of these texts to Him, exalted, preferring the safer path, and none knows its interpretation except Allah. And their successors (Khalaf) [answered] by specifying sound interpretations, to invalidate the doctrine of the misguided and to guide the deficient. So they interpreted 'hand' as power, and 'face' as essence.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage is a classification of two methodological approaches to the ambiguous verses (mutashabihat) about Allah's attributes. Al-Sawi begins by stating the problem: some people, due to sticking to customary (physical) matters and holding to the apparent meanings of scriptural texts, fell into confusion. They claimed that Allah is in a direction (jiha) or that He is a body (jism). From these claims necessarily follow either indwelling (hulul) or conjunction (ittisal) or separation (infisal)—all of which are impossible for Allah. Al-Sawi then exclaims: 'Exalted is Allah above that, a great exaltation.' Next, he presents the response of the scholars. He distinguishes two groups: the Salaf (the early generations, including the four imams) and the Khalaf (later scholars). The Salaf affirmed that Allah is transcendent above the attributes of created beings, and they delegated (tafwid) the true meaning of these texts to Allah, taking the safer path (al-tariq al-aslam). They held that 'none knows its interpretation except Allah' (Qur'an 3:7). The Khalaf, on the other hand, assigned specific sound interpretations (ta'wil) to these texts, in order to refute the doctrine of the misguided and to guide those who are deficient in understanding. For example, they interpreted 'hand' (yad) as power (qudra) and 'face' (wajh) as essence (dhat). Thus, both groups affirm divine transcendence, but they differ in method: the Salaf avoid interpretation altogether, while the Khalaf provide interpretations that are consistent with reason and revelation.

ta'wil
mutashabihat
jiha
jism
hulul
ittisal
infisal
qudra
dhat
Salaf
Khalaf

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the Salaf and Khalaf contradict each other.
Correction: Both affirm divine transcendence; they only differ in method of handling ambiguous verses.

Checkpoint
What is the difference between the Salaf and Khalaf approaches to the ambiguous verses about Allah's attributes according to this passage?
A) The Salaf reject the verses while the Khalaf accept them.
B) The Salaf delegate the meaning to Allah (tafwid), while the Khalaf specify sound interpretations (ta'wil).
C) The Salaf claim Allah is a body, while the Khalaf deny it.
D) The Salaf interpret literally, while the Khalaf interpret metaphorically.

Key Takeaway
Both the Salaf and Khalaf protect Allah's transcendence, but the Salaf delegate meaning to Allah while the Khalaf offer sound interpretations of ambiguous verses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read verses that describe Allah's 'hand' or 'face' in the Qur'an, do you feel a tension between the apparent meaning and your belief in His transcendence? How do you personally resolve that tension?
?
Are you more inclined to follow the path of tafwid (delegating to Allah) or ta'wil (seeking an interpretation)? What does your choice reveal about your desire for certainty versus your trust in Allah alone?
?
Consider the confusion that arises from clinging to customary, physical concepts when understanding Allah. In what areas of your own faith might you be unconsciously 'mixing' Allah's attributes with created qualities? Ask Allah to purify your understanding.

Definition and Conditions of Self-Attribute
Sanusi defines the self-attribute and explains its conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of a self-attribute (al-ṣifah al-nafsiyyah) and its two essential conditions: necessity for the essence and being uncaused by a cause.

Core Idea
A self-attribute is a positive, inseparable concomitant of the essence, not caused by an efficient cause.

In this passage, Sanusi carefully delineates what qualifies as a self-attribute. He specifies two critical conditions: the attribute must be necessary for the essence as long as the essence exists, and it must not be caused by an external cause. This definition distinguishes self-attributes from negative attributes and clarifies that they are not the result of causal influence but are simply concomitant. The text also addresses grammatical nuances to prevent misunderstanding.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage slowly, then its translation. Notice the technical terms 'صفة نفسية' and 'حال نفسية'.
The passage is dense; focus on the two conditions.
2
Identify the two conditions
Identify the phrases that specify the conditions: 'واجبة للذات ما دامت الذات' and 'غير معللة بعلة'. Understand what each condition means.
The first condition is necessity, the second is uncausedness.
3
Understand the grammatical clarification
Pay attention to the commentator's explanation of 'ما دامت الذات' and 'غير معللة بعلة' as grammatical analysis that removes ambiguity.
The phrase 'غير معللة بعلة' is a circumstantial accusative, not a predicate.
4
Grasp the theological point on causation
Understand that 'غير معللة بعلة' does not mean the attribute has no cause at all, but that it is not efficiently caused. The intended meaning is concomitance (تلازم), not causal influence.
This avoids the Ash'ari rejection of efficient causality in attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن لم يكن مدلولها عَدَماً ، فإن كانت واجبة للذات ما دامت الذات غير معلَّلة بعلَّة سُمِّيت صفة نفسيّة وحالاً نفسيَّة ;كالوجود عنه ، وإلا لزم أن يثبت له الحدوث وطرقُ العدم والمماثلة للحوادث مثلاً، بل المراد بكونها سلبيَّة أنَّ كلَّ واحدة منها سلبت أمراً لا يليق به جلّ وعزّ . قوله : (فإن كانت واجبة للذَّات) أي ثابتة لها على طريق الوجوب، بحيث لا يمكن انفكاكها عن الذات، ولما كان هذا يوهم القصر على النَّفسيَّة القديمة وعدم شموله للنَّفسيَّة الحادثة; أتي بقوله: (ما دامت الذات) دفعاً لذلك الإبهام، والمراد بالذات مطلق الشَّيء، سواء كان قائماً بنفسه; كالجوهر، أو قائماً بغيره ;كالعرض، ألا ترى أنَّ اللون عرض قائم بغيره ومع ذلك له صفة نفسيَّة لا يمكن انفكاكها عنه ما دام موجوداً، وهي قيامه بالغير . قوله : (ما دامت الذَّات) : (ما) مصدريَّة ظرفية معمولة لقوله : (واجبة للذات)، ودام تامَّة لا خبر لها ; أي: مدَّة دوام الذات، وفيه إشارة إلى أنَّ الأمر النَّفسيَّ لا يتخلَّف عن الذات التي ذلك الأمر نفسي لها . قوله : (غير معللة بعلة ) : ليس خبراً لـ(دام)، لما علمت أنَّها تامة لا خبر لها، بل هو حال من الضمير في (واجبة)، ولا يصح أن تكون ناقصة، معللة خبرها ; لأنَّ الذَّات لا تعلَّل ; أي : لا تلزم غيرها، فالمراد بالتَّعليل التَّلازم وليس المراد به التَّأثير فى المعلول إذ لا يقول به أهل السنة .
And if its implication is not non-existence, then if it is necessary for the essence as long as the essence is uncaused by a cause, it is called a self-attribute and a psychic state; such as existence... otherwise it would entail that it is subject to origination, ways of non-existence, and similarity to originated things, for instance; rather the intent of being negative is that each of them negates a matter unbefitting to Him, may He be glorified and exalted. His saying: ('For if it is necessary for the essence') means it is established for it in the way of necessity, such that it cannot be separated from the essence. And since this might suggest restriction to the eternal self-attribute and not include the originated self-attribute, he added: ('as long as the essence') to remove that ambiguity. And 'the essence' means any thing, whether it is subsistent by itself, like a substance, or subsistent by another, like an accident. Do you not see that color is an accident subsistent by another, yet it has a self-attribute that cannot be separated from it as long as it exists, which is its subsistence by another. His saying: ('as long as the essence'): ('ma') is a verbal noun indicating time, governed by ('necessary for the essence'), and 'dāma' is complete (tāmah) with no predicate, i.e., the duration of the essence's continuance. In it is an indication that the psychic matter does not lag behind the essence for which that matter is psychic. His saying: ('uncaused by a cause') is not a predicate of ('dāma'), because you know it is complete with no predicate, rather it is a circumstantial accusative (ḥāl) from the pronoun in ('necessary'). It is not valid that ('dāma') be incomplete with ('mu'alla) as its predicate, because the essence is not caused; i.e., it does not necessitate another. Thus the intended meaning of causation (ta'līl) is concomitance (talāzum), not influence (ta'thīr) in the effect, for the People of the Sunnah do not affirm that.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed definition of the self-attribute (al-ṣifah al-nafsiyyah) and its conditions. The author begins by stating that if an attribute's implication is not non-existence (i.e., it is a positive attribute), and if it is necessary for the essence (wājibat li-l-dhāt) as long as the essence endures, and if it is not caused by an external cause (ghayr mu'allala bi-'illah), then it is called a self-attribute and a psychic state (ḥāl nafsiyyah). The example given is existence (al-wujūd). The author then clarifies that if this condition were not met, it would lead to origination, non-existence, or similarity to originated things. He explains that the phrase 'necessary for the essence' means the attribute is inseparable from the essence, and the addition of 'as long as the essence' is to include both eternal and originated self-attributes (e.g., the attribute of 'subsistence by another' for an accident like color). The commentator then analyzes the grammar: 'mā' in 'mā dāmat al-dhāt' is a verbal noun indicating time, and 'dāma' is a complete verb (tāmah) with no predicate, meaning 'the duration of the essence's continuance'. Finally, he explains that 'ghayr mu'allala bi-'illah' is a circumstantial phrase (ḥāl) meaning the attribute is not caused. The term 'ta'līl' (causation) here means concomitance (talāzum), not efficient causality (ta'thīr), because the Ash'aris deny that attributes have efficient causal influence. This ensures that self-attributes are simply necessarily concomitant, not causally produced.

Key Terms

حال نفسية
وجوب للذات
تعلية
تلازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'غير معللة بعلة' means the attribute has no cause whatsoever, i.e., it is absolutely uncaused.
Correction: The author clarifies that 'غير معللة بعلة' means the attribute is not efficiently caused (تأثير) by an external cause. The intended meaning is that the attribute is necessarily concomitant (تلازم) with the essence, not that it has no cause at all.

Checkpoint

B) The attribute is not caused by an efficient cause, but is necessarily concomitant
C) The attribute is conditioned by a cause
D) The attribute is eternal

Key Takeaway
A self-attribute is a positive attribute that is necessary for the essence and not efficiently caused, but merely concomitant.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage teaches that existence is a self-attribute for God—necessary and uncaused. Yet your own existence is a gift that could be withdrawn at any moment. When was the last time you truly felt the weight of that contingency? Will you pause today to thank the One whose existence is necessary for your fleeting breath?
?
If 'uncaused' here means not efficiently caused, consider how often you act as if your good qualities (patience, generosity) are self-attributes—permanent and self-generated. What would it look like to rely on God as the only true cause of any good in you?
?
The passage distinguishes between attributes that negate (negative) and those that affirm (positive). Is your relationship with God built more on negating what He is not (like limiting Him) or on affirming what He is (like His necessary existence)? Which shift would change your worship today?

Defining Divine Knowledge: Dispute and Soundest Definition
Sanusi's commentator discusses whether knowledge can be defined and gives the soundest definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why some scholars argue knowledge cannot be defined and what the soundest definition of divine knowledge is according to Sanusi's commentator.

Core Idea
Divine knowledge is self-evident yet definable; its soundest definition is an eternal comprehensive attribute.

This passage from Sanusi's commentator addresses a foundational question in kalam: can divine knowledge be defined? The author presents both sides of the dispute and then gives the most authoritative definition. Understanding this will clarify how Islamic theology approaches the attributes of God.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic text slowly, then the literal translation. Notice the structure: it starts with a general statement about disagreement, then gives reasons for the rejection of definition, and finally provides the soundest definition.
The passage is a commentary on the phrase 'an eternal attribute' from the original matn.
2
Identify the two positions on defining knowledge
The author mentions that people differ: some say knowledge cannot be defined, others say it can. Identify the key arguments for each side.
The rejectionists offer two reasons: self-evidence and difficulty.
3
Examine the reasons for rejecting definition
Why do some claim knowledge cannot be defined? Because it is self-evident (it reveals others, so needs no revelation) and because every attempted definition has been disputed.
This shows the challenge of defining self-evident concepts.
4
Learn the soundest definition
The author states that the soundest definition is: 'It is an eternal attribute subsisting in His essence, pertaining to the necessary, the possible, and the impossible with a comprehensive and uncovering relation.' Break down each component.
This definition covers the scope of divine knowledge.
5
Reflect on the importance
Consider why this definition matters. It affirms that God's knowledge is all-encompassing and eternal, which is central to Islamic theology.
This definition guards against reductionist views of God's knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (صفة أزلية ... الخ ) : اعلم : ان الناس مختلفوا ، في العلم هل يحد أو لا؟ فقال بعضهم : إنه لا يحد؛ لظهوره انه كاشف لغيره، فهو غني أن يظهره غيره ولعسره إذ لم يُحد بحدّ إلا نوزع فيه، والقائلون بحده لهم فيه تعاريف كثيرة وأكثرها مدخول ، وأصحها قولنا : هو صفة أزلية قائمة بذاته تعالى تتعلق بالواجبات والجائزات والمستحيلات تعلق إحاطة وانكشاف
His saying: (An eternal attribute... etc.): Know that the people differ as to whether knowledge can be defined or not. Some say it cannot be defined because it is evident that it reveals others, so it is beyond needing another to reveal it, and because it is difficult—whenever it is defined with a definition, it is disputed. Those who do define it have many definitions, most of which are flawed, and the soundest is our saying: It is an eternal attribute subsisting in His essence, pertaining to the necessary, the possible, and the impossible with a comprehensive and uncovering relation.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of divine knowledge. The commentator begins by noting the scholarly disagreement over whether knowledge can be defined. Some argue it cannot be defined because it is self-evident—it reveals other things and does not need to be revealed—and because any attempt to define it has been contested. Others propose definitions, many of which are weak. The commentator then gives the soundest definition: knowledge is an eternal attribute of God that encompasses all necessary, possible, and impossible things in a comprehensive and disclosing manner.

Correction: The commentator affirms that while it is difficult, a sound definition exists: an eternal attribute encompassing all categories.

Key Takeaway
The soundest definition of divine knowledge is an eternal attribute of God that comprehensively encompasses all necessary, possible, and impossible things.

The Objection Against 'Unveiling' and Its Resolution
Commentator explains 'unveiling' and responds to the objection about temporal implication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the verb 'unveils' in the definition of knowledge raises an objection about temporality and how scholars respond with the principle that verbs in definitions are timeless, though the commentator notes a residual passive implication.

Core Idea
Verbs in definitions are abstracted from time, but 'unveiling' still implies passivity.

In defining knowledge, scholars use the term 'unveils' (yankashifu). However, this verb's present tense form suggests temporality, which seems incompatible with God's eternal knowledge. This lesson examines the objection and the nuanced response, revealing deeper theological sensitivity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the literal translation. Notice the objection and answer structure.
Pay attention to the verb 'ينكشف' and its form.
2
Understand the objection
The objector says the present tense verb implies occurrence in the present or future, which is not befitting Allah's knowledge.
The key is that 'المضارع' indicates time.
3
Grasp the answer
The answer states that verbs in definitions are stripped of temporal indication; they denote the essence of the action, not its occurrence in time.
This is a standard principle in kalam.
4
Appreciate the commentator's critique
Even after accepting the answer, the commentator notes that 'unveiling' is a passive verbal noun implying occurrence after hiddenness, which is still problematic for divine knowledge.
This shows the depth of theological precision.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ينكشف: المراد بالانكشاف التمييز والاتضاح.
إن قلت : التَّعبير بـ ( ينكشف) يوهم حدوث الانكشاف؛ لأن المضارع يدلُّ على الحال والاستقبال وهو لا يناسب علم الله تعالى.
أجيب بأن الأفعال الواقعة في التعاريف مجرَّدة عن الزمان، ولا دلالة لها عليه، فكأنه قيل : صفة يحصل بها انكشاف ما تعلقت به كذا قيل
وأنت خبير بأن الفعل وإن كان الملاحظ منه المصدر وهو الانكشاف إلَّا أنَّ التَّعبير بالانكشاف هنا غير لائق من جهة أنَّه انفعال يوهم حدوث إيضاح بعد خفاء .
His saying: (unveils): What is meant by unveiling is distinction and clarity. If you say: The expression 'unveils' suggests the occurrence of unveiling because the present tense indicates the present and future, which is not befitting God's knowledge. The answer: The verbs used in definitions are abstracted from time and have no temporal indication, as if it was said: an attribute by which an unveiling of what it pertains to occurs. It was said thus. And you are aware that although the verb is considered as the verbal noun, i.e., unveiling, nevertheless the expression 'unveiling' here is unsuitable because it is a passive notion implying the occurrence of clarification after hiddenness.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage explains the term 'unveils' (yankashifu) used in the definition of knowledge. The commentator clarifies that 'unveiling' means distinction and clarity. An objection is raised: the verb 'unveils' implies temporality because its present tense form suggests present or future action, which contradicts God's eternal knowledge. The response is that verbs in definitions are stripped of temporal meaning; they simply indicate the action as a concept. However, the commentator then critiques even this response, noting that 'unveiling' itself is a passive construct, implying that knowledge occurred after a state of hiddenness, which is inappropriate for God. Thus, while the definition is accepted, the terminology is not fully ideal.

Correction: In kalam definitions, verbs are abstracted from time and only convey the conceptual essence of the action.

Key Takeaway

Definition of Ta‘alluq and Its Obligatory Perpetual Nature
Author defines ta‘alluq and states it is wajib necessarily and perpetually for attributes of ma‘ani.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of ta‘alluq and why it is intellectually obligatory for the attributes of meanings to be perpetually related to their objects.

Core Idea
Ta‘alluq is the attribute's inherent requirement for an extra object beyond itself.

In this passage, the author clarifies a fundamental concept in kalam: the relation (ta‘alluq) of the divine attributes. He defines ta‘alluq as the attribute's requiring an extra thing beyond its subsistence in the essence. Using examples like knowledge requiring a known object, he establishes that this relation is intellectually necessary and perpetual. Let's examine the definition and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation to grasp the meaning.
Pay attention to the structure: definition first, then examples, then ruling.
2
Understand the definition of ta‘alluq
The author defines ta‘alluq as 'the attribute's requiring an extra thing beyond its subsisting in the essence.' Note that it is not the attribute itself but a relation it has to something else.
The extra thing is called the object of the attribute (e.g., known, willed, powerful).
3
Identify the examples
Three examples are given: knowledge requires a known object; will requires a willed object; power requires an object of power. See how the pattern holds.
The phrase 'and so on' indicates the pattern applies to all attributes of meanings.
4
Reflect on the obligatory and perpetual nature
The author states that the relation is intellectually obligatory and perpetual. 'Wajib' means necessary, and 'dawam' means perpetual. This emphasizes that the relation is not accidental but essential.
This prevents the misconception that the relation might cease or be optional.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان تعلق الصفات. والتعلُّق: اقتضاء الصفة أمراً زائداً على قيامها بالذات؛ كاقتضاء العلم معلوماً ينكشف به واقتضاء الإرادة مراداً يتخصص بها، واقتضاء القدرة مقدوراً، وهكذا. فقال: (وواجب) عقلاً (تعليقُ ذي) أي: هذه الصفاتِ) أي: صفات المعاني ( حَتْماً ) أي: لزوماً، (دواماً) أي: على سبيل الدوام، والاستمرار وهذا من زيادة التأكيد؛
Explanation of the relation of attributes. And al-ta‘alluq (relation) is: the attribute's requiring an extra thing beyond its subsisting in the essence; like knowledge requiring a known thing that is disclosed by it, and will requiring an object of will that is specified by it, and power requiring an object of power, and so on. So he said: 'And it is intellectually obligatory (the relation of the possessor of these attributes), i.e., the attributes of meanings, necessarily, i.e., perpetually, perpetually, i.e., in the manner of perpetuity and continuity, and this is from the increase of emphasis.'
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage defines a key technical term in kalam: al-ta‘alluq (relation) of attributes. The author states that ta‘alluq is the attribute's 'requiring an extra thing beyond its subsisting in the essence.' To illustrate, he gives examples: knowledge requires a known object that is disclosed by it; will requires an object of will that is specified by it; power requires an object of power. Then he quotes the original text: 'And it is intellectually obligatory (the relation of the possessor of these attributes), i.e., the attributes of meanings, necessarily, perpetually.' He explains that 'necessarily' is an emphasis, and 'perpetually' is an extra emphasis, reinforcing the idea that the relation of these attributes is continuous and unceasing.

Correction: Ta‘alluq is not a separate entity; it is the attribute's state of requiring something beyond itself, inherent to the attribute.

Definition of Divine Speech: Kalam Nafsi
Ahl al-Sunna define divine speech as a self-subsistent attribute not composed of letters and sounds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ahl al-Sunna definition of divine speech as a self-subsistent attribute (kalam nafsi) distinct from created verbal speech.

Core Idea
Divine speech is not letters and sounds; it is a meaning within God's essence.

The Mu'tazila argued that speech requires letters and sounds, so God cannot be described as speaking. Ahl al-Sunna responded by distinguishing between two types of speech: verbal and self-subsistent. This lesson explains that distinction and defends the orthodox position.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazila objection
Read the first part of the passage where the Mu'tazila claim that speech is impossible for God because it consists of created letters and sounds.
Notice how they reinterpret scriptural verses to avoid attributing speech to God directly.
2
Learn the Ahl al-Sunna response
Focus on the response: they reject the restriction of speech to letters and sounds by dividing speech into two types: verbal (lafzi) and self-subsistent (nafsi).
The key point is that Allah's attribute of speech is of the second type, which is not composed of elements.
3
See the conclusion
Understand why this division prevents the absurd consequence that God would be created or temporal.
The eternal attribute of speech is a meaning in God's essence, not a temporal phenomenon.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
معنى الكلام عند أهل السنة
ولمَّا ذهب المعتزلة إلى استحالة الكلام عليه تعالى، لأنَّه إنَّما يكون بحروف وأصوات، وتقديم وتأخير، وغير ذلك، وهذه كلُّها حادثة، ولا يصحُ اتصافه تعالى بالحوادث، وإلا لكان حادثاً. وصرفوا ما ورد في الكتاب والسُّنَّة، من أنَّه تعالى متكلّم، عن ظاهره على معنى أنَّه خالق الكلام في غيره ؛ كالشَّجرة التي كلمت موسى عليه السَّلام مثلاً ، فالكلام صفة غيره لا صفته تعالى. أجاب أهل السُّنَّة بمَنْع حصر الكلام في الحروف والأصوات، بجَعل الكلام قسمين: لفظي ونفسي، والثاني هو المراد، كما أشار إليه بقوله : ثمَّ الكلام أي كلامه ،تعالى الذي هو صفة ذاته، نفسي، (ليس بالحروف والأصوات، (وليس) متلبساً (بالترتيب) من تقديم وتأخير، ( كـ) الكلام الحادث (المألوف لنا، وحينئذ فلا يلزم المحال .
The meaning of speech according to Ahl al-Sunna. And when the Mu'tazila held the impossibility of speech for Him, Most High, because it only occurs with letters and sounds, and previous and subsequent arrangement, and other things, and all these are created (haditha), and it is not correct that He, Most High, be described by created things, otherwise He would be created. And they interpreted what is related in the Book and the Sunnah, that He, Most High, is speaking (mutakallim), away from its apparent meaning as meaning that He is the creator of speech in another; like the tree that spoke to Moses, peace be upon him, for example, so speech is an attribute of another, not His attribute, Most High. Ahl al-Sunna answered by preventing the restriction of speech to letters and sounds, by making speech two types: verbal (lafzi) and self-subsistent (nafsi), and the second is intended, as he indicated by his saying: then speech, i.e., His speech, Most High, which is an attribute of His essence, self-subsistent, (not with letters and sounds), (and not) clothed (with arrangement) of previous and subsequent, (like) created speech (the familiar to us), and then the absurd does not follow.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof. It begins by presenting the Mu'tazila position: they deny that speech can be attributed to God because speech consists of letters, sounds, and temporal arrangement, all of which are created. Attributing such created things to God would imply that He is subject to change and thus created. Hence they reinterpreted scriptural verses describing God as 'speaking' to mean that He creates speech in something else (e.g., the tree that spoke to Moses), so speech becomes an attribute of that created thing, not of God. Ahl al-Sunna's response is to reject the restriction of speech to letters and sounds. Instead, they divide speech into two kinds: verbal speech (kalam lafzi), which is composed of letters and sounds and is created, and self-subsistent speech (kalam nafsi), which is a meaning subsisting in the soul without letters or sounds. The divine attribute of speech is of the second kind, and it is eternal and not subject to temporality. Therefore, the absurd consequence (that God would be created) does not follow. The author also notes that this definition indirectly refutes the Karramiyya, who affirm a form of divine speech that is temporal.

كلام
معتزلة
لفظي
نفسي
صفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think divine speech must be composed of letters and sounds.
Correction: Divine speech is a self-subsistent meaning in God's essence (kalam nafsi) without letters or sounds.

C) Two types: verbal and self-subsistent; the attribute is self-subsistent
D) One type: self-subsistent (nafsi)

Key Takeaway
Divine speech is a self-subsistent attribute (kalam nafsi) without letters or sounds.

Defense of Internal Speech Against Mu'tazila Denial
Refutation of Mu'tazila denial that internal meanings are called speech, citing Arab usage and a line of poetry.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazila denial of internal speech is refuted by Arab usage and a poetic evidence, affirming the concept of kalam nafsi.

Core Idea
Arabs called inner meaning speech; the tongue is its guide.

In this passage, al-Sawi addresses a key point of contention with the Mu'tazila: can what a person finds within themselves be called 'speech'? The Mu'tazila reduce such internal meanings to will, knowledge, or passing thoughts. Al-Sawi counters by citing the actual usage of the Arabs, including a line from the poet al-Akhtal that explicitly locates speech in the heart. This lesson shows why the Mu'tazila objection fails.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage: first the hypothetical Mu'tazila objection, then the author's refutation. Note the structure.
The 'if you say' signals the objection; 'I say' begins the answer.
2
Examine the Mu'tazila position
Identify what the Mu'tazila claim: that internal meanings are will, knowledge, or thoughts, not speech. Ask why they might reject internal speech.
3
Study the refutation
The author says their view is invalid because it contradicts Arab usage. What is the evidence? A line of poetry. Read the translation and commentary.
The poet al-Akhtal is a pre-Islamic Arab; his usage reflects the language.
4
Connect to kalam nafsi
Understand that this passage defends the Ash'ari doctrine of internal speech (kalam nafsi) as real speech, distinct from uttered words.
This is crucial for the definition of Qur'an as uncreated speech.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن قلت : إنَّ المعتزلة ينكرون تسمية ما يجده الإنسان في نفسه كلاماً، ويردُّون ذلك للإرادة أو العلم أو الخواطر .

I say: Their statement is invalid because it contradicts the Arab usage of calling it speech. Al-Akhtal said: Indeed, speech lies in the heart, and the tongue is made a guide to the heart.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector (a hypothetical Mu'tazila supporter) states the Mu'tazila position: they deny that internal meanings (what one finds in oneself) can be called 'speech'. Instead, they classify such phenomena as will, knowledge, or passing thoughts. The refutation is straightforward: this denial conflicts with how Arabs actually use the term 'speech'. To prove the point, the author quotes a line from the poet al-Akhtal: 'Indeed, speech lies in the heart' – meaning the true, meaningful speech is the internal intention, and the tongue merely expresses it. This shows that the Arabs considered internal meaning as speech, thus establishing the validity of kalam nafsi. The Mu'tazila objection is therefore invalid.

Types of Logical Opposition for Prophetic Attributes
Al-Sawi defines 'opposite' and classifies logical relations for prophetic attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Sawi defines 'opposite' in the context of prophetic attributes and classifies the logical relations between each attribute and its opposite.

Core Idea
The logical relation between a prophetic attribute and its opposite varies—trustworthiness vs. betrayal are contraries, while truthfulness vs. lying are not.

In discussing what is impossible for prophets, Al-Sawi examines the logical relationship between each obligatory attribute and its opposite. He clarifies that 'opposite' here means any contradictory quality, and then shows that the type of opposition differs depending on whether the opposite is a privation or a positive act. This lesson unpacks that classification.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Al-Sawi is commenting on an earlier matn.
2
Identify the four pairs and their definitions
Find the four pairs of attributes and their opposites: truthfulness/lying, trustworthiness/betrayal, conveying/concealment, intelligence/dullness. Note how each opposite is defined.
Betrayal is defined as an act (وجودي), which influences its logical classification.
3
Analyze the type of opposition for each pair
Determine the logical relation for each pair as stated by Al-Sawi. Note the difference: trustworthiness vs. betrayal is opposition of contraries (تقابل الضدين), while the others are opposition of a thing and its equivalent of its opposite (تقابل الشيء والمساوي لنقيضه).
This distinction shows the author's precision in logical terminology.

وأمَّا بين الأمانة والخيانة : فتقابل الضّدَّين؛ لأنَّه فسَّر الخيانة بالفعل
