الفطانة والبلادة.
Statement of what is impossible for them, peace be upon them. (It is impossible) for them, peace be upon them, (the opposite of them) that is, the opposite of these four obligatory attributes mentioned before (upon them). So it is impossible for them: Firstly: betrayal. (1) He said, the Most High: 'Among them are those We have narrated to you and among them are those We have not narrated to you.' His saying: (its opposite) means the opposite is an absolute contradiction; because lying is the non-correspondence of a report to reality, betrayal is performing prohibited and disliked acts, and concealment is not fulfilling what they were commanded to convey to creation. Accordingly, the opposition between truthfulness and lying is the opposition of a thing and its equivalent of its opposite. As for between trustworthiness and betrayal: it is the opposition of two contraries; because he interpreted betrayal as an act, and it is existent. And as for between conveying and concealment: it is the opposition of a thing and its equivalent of its opposite, and likewise between intelligence and dullness.
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the logical oppositions between each prophetic attribute and its opposite. The author begins by stating the heading: 'Statement of what is impossible for them.' Then he quotes the matn: 'It is impossible for them, peace be upon them, the opposite of them' meaning the opposite of the four obligatory attributes (truthfulness, trustworthiness, conveying, intelligence). He then lists the first impossibility: betrayal. He then gives a Quranic citation. Then in the commentary (قوله), he explains that 'opposite' here means absolute contradiction (مطلق المنافي). He defines each concept: lying is non-correspondence of report to reality, betrayal is performing forbidden/disliked acts, concealment is not fulfilling the command to convey. Then he classifies the types of opposition. For truthfulness and lying, it is opposition of a thing and its equivalent of its opposite (تقابل الشيء والمساوي لنقيضه). For trustworthiness and betrayal: opposition of contraries (تقابل الضدين) because betrayal is an existent act. For conveying and concealment: same as truth/lying. For intelligence and dullness: also same. The author carefully distinguishes the logical relations.

Key Terms
ضدّ
تقابل
الضدّين
منافي
صدق
كذب
أمانة
خيانة
تبليغ
كتمان
فطانة
بلادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All opposites are contraries (تضاد) in the strict sense.
Correction: Al-Sawi shows that only trustworthiness and betrayal are contraries because betrayal is an act (وجودي). The other oppositions are of a different type: a thing and its equivalent of its opposite.

Checkpoint
What type of logical opposition exists between trustworthiness (amana) and betrayal (khiyana)?
A) Opposition of a thing and its equivalent of its opposite
B) Opposition of contraries (تضاد)
C) Opposition of contradiction (تناقض)
D) Opposition of privation and possession

Key Takeaway
Al-Sawi distinguishes between two types of logical opposition for prophetic attributes: contraries (for trustworthiness/betrayal) and a thing and its equivalent of its opposite (for the other three pairs).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the attribute of truthfulness. Lying is a privation—a failure to match reality. When you have lied, are you doing something positive, or are you simply failing to be truthful? How does this realization affect your commitment to speaking the truth, even when it hurts?
?
Trustworthiness and betrayal are both positive acts. In your own life, are you actively practicing trustworthiness, or are you merely avoiding obvious betrayal? What is one specific area where you have been passive in fulfilling a trust, and what concrete step will you take to turn that into an active act of amanah?
?
The lesson states that concealment is a privation—a failure to convey what you are obligated to share. Think of a truth you know you should convey to someone (a reminder, advice, or a correction) but have withheld. Is your silence a passive lack or a deliberate choice? This week, what will you do to break that silence?

Belief in Saints: Definition and Obligation
Al-Sawi defines wali and states the obligation to believe in them with evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of a saint (wali) in Islamic theology, the obligation to believe in them, and the scriptural evidence supporting their miracles.

Core Idea
A saint is a righteous servant who performs miracles—believing in them is obligatory.

The text begins by defining a saint and establishing the obligation to believe in them. It then provides Quranic and prophetic evidence for the miracles of saints, showing that such belief is rooted in revelation and consensus.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of wali
Identify how the text defines a saint: a servant of apparent righteousness, not claiming prophethood. Note that this excludes false prophets.
2
Understand the basis for obligation
The obligation to believe in saints is established by Quran, Sunnah, and consensus of the early community. This shows it is a core belief.
Consensus (ijma) is a key source of authority.
3
Examine the evidence
The footnote provides two stories: Maryam receiving fruit out of season, and Asif bringing the throne of Bilqis. These are examples of saintly miracles (karamat).
These miracles are from the Quran, confirming the possibility.
4
Reflect on the implication
Believing in saints is obligatory; denying saintly miracles can lead to disbelief if done obstinately. But the text focuses on acceptance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإيمان بالأولياء يد عبد ظاهر الصلاح، غير مقرون بدعوى النُّبوَّة. كلُّ ذلك ورد به الكتاب (۱) والسُّنَّة(۲) وأجمعت عليه الأمة قبل ظهور المخالفين، وكلّ ما كان كذلك فالإيمان به واجب. (و) كذا يجب الإيمان بكلِّ ما جاء أي: روي ونقل عن أي : عن النبي (البشير ) أي : المبشِّر لمن أوفى بالعهود، بأنه محمود العاقبة (من كل حكم بيان لكل ما جاء (صار) في الاشتهار بين الخاصة والعامة (كـ) الأمر (الضَّروري الذي لا يخفى على أحد . والتابعين إلى وقتنا هذا. قوله : ( في الاشتهار) بيان لوجه الشبه؛ أي: إنَّ الأحكام التي أتى بها النَّبيُّ صلى الله عليه وسلم واشتهرت حتَّى صارت كالأمور الضرورية يجب الإيمان بها، وكلُّ من أنكر شيئاً منها فقد كفر ، وأمَّا الأحكام التي لم تبلغ في الاشتهار هذا الحد؛ فلا يكفّر منكرها ؛ كالرَّفع من الركوع والسجود، ونحو ذلك. (1) أما الكتاب ما جاء فيه من قصة مريم حيث ساق الله لها الرزق في غير أوانه، ومن غير حضور أسبابه ، قال تعالى: كُلَّمَا دَخَلَ عَلَيْهَا زَكَرِيَّا الْمِحْرَابَ وَجَدَ عِندَهَا رِزۡقًا قَالَ يَمَرۡيَمُ أَنَّىٰ لَكِ هَـٰذَا قَالَتْ هُوَ مِنْ عِندِ اللَّهِ إِنَّ اللَّهَ يَرۡزُقُ مَن يَشَاءُ بِغَيۡرِ حِسَابٍ) [آل عمران : ٣٧] فقد كان يجد عندها فاكهة الصيف في الشتاء وبالعكس، وكذلك ما جاء في قصة آصف وزير سيدنا سليمان عليه الصلاة والسلام وقد كان يعرف اسم الله الأعظم، فدعا به فأتى بعرش بلقيس قبل أن يرتد طرف سليمان إليه، قال سبحانه: (قَالَ الَّذِي عِندَهُ عِلم مِنَ الْكِتَبِ أَنَا عَانِيكَ بِهِ قَبْلَ أَن يَرۡتَدَّ إِلَيۡكَ طَرۡفُكَ) [النمل: ٤٠] . (٢) أما السنة ما أخرجه البخاري (٣٤٦٥) ، ومسلم (١/٢٧٤٣) قصة أصحاب الغار عندما دعوا بصالح أعمالهم فانفرجت عنهم الصخرة، من حديث ابن عمر وهذا من عطف العام على الخاص ; لشموله ما تقدَّم من الحساب وما عُطف عليه وغيره :- كوجوب شهادة أن لا إله إلا الله وأن محمداً رسول الله، وإقام الصلاة، وإيتاء الزكاة وصوم رمضان، وحج بيت الله الحرام، وحرمة الزِّنا والخمر والرِّبا ، وحِلَّ النكاح والبيع، ونحو ذلك .قوله : (كوجوب شهادة أن لا إله إلَّا الله): تمثيل لما جاء عن البشير .
Belief in the saints: a servant whose apparent righteousness is apparent, not coupled with a claim of prophethood. All that is established by the Book (1) and the Sunnah (2), and the community agreed upon it before the appearance of the dissenters, and everything that is like that, belief in it is obligatory. (And) likewise must be believed everything that came, meaning: was narrated and transmitted from, i.e., from the Prophet, the bringer of good news, i.e., the one who gives glad tidings to those who fulfill covenants that he has a praiseworthy outcome, of every ruling, explanation for everything that came (became) in widespreadness among the elite and the common people like the necessary matter that is not hidden from anyone. And the Successors until our time. His saying: (in widespreadness) is an explanation of the aspect of similarity; i.e., indeed the rulings that the Prophet (peace be upon him) brought and became widespread until they became like necessary matters, belief in them is obligatory, and whoever denies any of them has disbelieved. As for the rulings that have not reached this level of widespreadness, then the denier of them is not declared a disbeliever, such as raising from bowing and prostrating, and the like. (1) As for the Book, what came in it from the story of Maryam when God provided her with sustenance out of its season, and without the presence of its means. He said: 'Whenever Zechariah entered the sanctuary upon her, he found with her provision. He said, O Maryam, from where is this? She said, It is from God. Indeed, God provides for whom He wills without account.' [Al 'Imran: 37]. He used to find with her summer fruit in winter and vice versa. And likewise what came in the story of Asif, the vizier of our master Sulayman (peace be upon him), and he knew the Greatest Name of God, so he supplicated with it and brought the throne of Bilqis before Sulayman's glance could return to him. He said: 'The one who had knowledge from the Scripture said, I will bring it to you before your glance returns to you.' [An-Naml: 40]. (2) As for the Sunnah, what al-Bukhari (3465) and Muslim (1/2743) narrated: the story of the people of the cave when they supplicated with their righteous deeds, and the rock split open for them, from the hadith of Ibn 'Umar. And this is from appending the general to the specific; because it includes what has preceded from the account and what is appended to it and other than it: such as the obligation of the testimony that there is no god but Allah and that Muhammad is the Messenger of God, establishing prayer, giving zakat, fasting Ramadan, hajj of the Sacred House of God, and the prohibition of adultery, wine, usury, and the permissibility of marriage, sale, and the like. His saying: (such as the obligation of the testimony that there is no god but Allah) is an example of what came from the bringer of good news.
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage defines the obligation of believing in saints and in all rulings transmitted from the Prophet that have become widespread among the community. It begins by stating that belief in saints—meaning a person of apparent righteousness not claiming prophethood—is obligatory because it is established by the Quran, Sunnah, and consensus of the early community. The author then extends this to everything that has been narrated from the Prophet (al-bashir) and has become so well-known among both elite and commoners that it is like a necessary matter, i.e., undeniable. The commentary clarifies the point: rulings that have attained such widespreadness must be believed, and denying any of them constitutes disbelief (kufr). Rulings that have not reached that level, such as the specific actions of raising hands in prayer, do not incur kufr when denied. The footnote provides scriptural proofs for the possibility of miracles for saints: the story of Maryam receiving out-of-season fruit, and of Asif bringing the throne of Bilqis instantly. The passage then returns to the main discussion, noting that this principle is an instance of appending the general to the specific. It lists examples of widely known obligations and prohibitions, and comments that these examples illustrate what came from the Prophet.

Key Terms
الأولياء
الصلاح
الكرامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think miracles occur only for prophets, not for saints.
Correction: The Quran and hadith show that saints can perform miracles (karamat), as in the stories of Maryam and Asif.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for the obligation to believe in saints?
A) Only the Quran
B) Only the Sunnah
C) The Quran, Sunnah, and consensus
D) Consensus alone

Key Takeaway
Belief in saints is obligatory, supported by the Quran, Sunnah, and consensus, and miracles of saints are a recognized reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly accept that righteous people you know, perhaps among your teachers or elders, could be granted miracles by Allah? Does this belief affect how you regard them, or do you treat them with ordinary respect without expecting divine intervention through them?
?
The evidence for saintly miracles includes stories of Maryam and Asif. When you read these Quranic accounts, do you see them as historical events only, or as ongoing possibilities for believers today? What would it mean for your prayer life if you genuinely believed that Allah could respond with extraordinary means through a righteous servant?
?
If the consensus of the early community made belief in saints obligatory, then dismissing or doubting the reality of karamat puts you at odds with the Sunnah. Is there any area of your heart that still questions or rationalizes away these miracles? Are you willing to submit your intellect to the revealed texts and the consensus of the salaf, even when the miraculous seems improbable to you?

intermediate

Widely Known Rulings and the Criterion for Disbelief
Al-Sawi explains the obligation to believe in widely transmitted rulings and the criterion for takfir.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that rulings from the Prophet that are widely known like necessary matters must be believed, and denying them constitutes disbelief, while less widespread rulings do not reach that level.

Core Idea
Only rulings that are as widespread as necessary matters incur disbelief when denied.

After discussing saints, the text turns to the obligation of believing in all rulings that came from the Prophet and became widely known among the community. It distinguishes between rulings that are so widespread they are like self-evident truths and those that are not, giving examples of each.

Guided Path
1
Identify the second obligation
The text says 'kadha yajibu al-iman bi kulli ma ja'a...' meaning that believing in everything transmitted from the Prophet that became widespread is obligatory.
This builds on the previous obligation regarding saints.
2
Understand 'widespreadness' (ishtihar)
The commentary explains that rulings which have reached the level of being like necessary matters (amr daruri) are those that no Muslim can claim ignorance of.
Examples include the five pillars, prohibition of major sins.
3
Note the consequence of denial
Whoever denies such a widespread ruling is declared a disbeliever (kafir). But denial of less widespread rulings, like the specific actions in prayer, does not lead to takfir.
This is a key principle in avoiding extremism.
4
Review the examples given
The text lists obligations like shahada, prayer, zakat, fasting, hajj, and prohibitions like adultery, wine, usury.
These are all matters known by necessity from the religion.
5
Reflect on the principle
This principle protects the community from declaring takfir on minor issues while affirming the necessity of core beliefs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

According to the passage, which of the following is an example of a ruling whose denial would NOT constitute disbelief?
A) The obligation of prayer

?

?
When you encounter someone who denies a certain ruling, do you immediately judge them or do you first consider whether that ruling has reached the level of universal acceptance? How might this lesson change your response?
?
Is there a religious practice or prohibition that you treat lightly, telling yourself 'this isn't that important'? What would it mean for your faith if this ruling were actually among the widely known, necessary matters that no Muslim can deny?

What Is Kalam?
beginner
6 min
Faith is Simple; Utterance is a Condition of Perfection
Al-Sawi defines iman as simple and explains the role of utterance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that iman (faith) is simple, consisting solely of internal attestation (tasdiq), and that verbal utterance of the two shahadahs is a condition of perfection, not a condition of validity or a part of the essence of faith.

Core Idea
Faith is in the heart; words perfect it, but they are not its core.

In this passage, al-Sawi clarifies the essential nature of faith and the role of the two shahadahs. He distinguishes between the internal belief that constitutes the reality of iman and the external utterance that is a condition of perfection. This distinction resolves apparent contradictions in the sources and explains the status of those who cannot or do not speak.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the main claim
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the author starts by stating that iman is simple (basiṭ). This is the central thesis.
Look for the phrase 'فالإيمان بسيط'.
2
Distinguish the three categories of people
The passage addresses the person who believes but does not utter—either excused, refusing, or silently willing. Note the different rulings for each: the refuser is a kafir, the excused is accepted, and the silent believer is still a believer.
The phrase 'لا لعذر مَنَعه ولا لإباء' is key.
3
Understand the function of utterance
The author then explains that the utterance is a condition of perfection (sharṭ kamāl), not a condition of validity (sharṭ ṣiḥḥa) nor part of the reality. Compare it to other acts of worship like prayer and fasting.
Note the Arabic: 'إنَّما هو شرط كمال فيه'.
4
Examine the supporting evidence
Finally, al-Sawi quotes Quran and Hadith to support the stance that utterance is a condition, and that faith is primarily in the heart.
The verses and hadith are cited to reinforce the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثانياً: النّطق بالشهادتين والخلاف فيه
وعلى ما ذكرنا فالإيمان بسيط، وعليه فمن صدَّق بقلبه، ولم يُقرَّ بلسانه لا لعذر مَنَعه ولا لإباء، بل كان بحيث لو طلب منه النُّطق لأجاب؛ فهو مؤمن عند الله تعالى، ناج من الخلود في النار. 
فالنُّطقُ : إنَّما هو شرط كمال فيه كبقية الأعمال من صلاة وصوم وزكاة وحجّ، لا شرط صحة، ولا جزءاً من حقيقته، نعم هو شرط
قوله: (وعلى ما ذكرنا) : أي : على كلِّ من التّعريفين اللذين هما حديث النَّفس التابع للمعرفة أو هو المعرفة
قوله :لا لعذر :أي: وأمَّا المعذور فمتّفق على قبول الإيمان منه ولو على القول بأنَّه مركب.
قوله : (ولا لإباء) : أي : لأن الآبي كافر بالإجماع.
قوله : (نعم هو شرط) : استدراك على قوله : (إنَّما هو شرط كمال فيه) ويؤيده قوله تعالى : أُولَئِكَ كَتَبَ فِي قُلُوبِهِمُ الْإِيمَنَ (١) ، وقوله عليه الصَّلاة والسَّلام : «اللَّهمَّ؛ ثبت قلبي على دينك»(٢)
Secondly: The utterance of the two shahadahs and the disagreement concerning it. And based on what we mentioned, faith is simple. Accordingly, whoever believes in his heart and does not confess with his tongue, not due to an excuse preventing him nor due to refusal, but rather in such a way that if he were asked to utter he would respond, then he is a believer in the sight of Allah Almighty, safe from eternal abiding in the Fire. So utterance is only a condition of perfection in it, like the rest of the acts of prayer, fasting, zakat, and pilgrimage, not a condition of validity, nor a part of its reality. Yes, it is a condition. His saying: (And based on what we mentioned): meaning: upon each of the two definitions which are: the self-speech following knowledge or it is knowledge itself. His saying: (not due to an excuse): meaning: as for the excused, there is agreement on accepting faith from him even according to the view that it is composite. His saying: (nor due to refusal): meaning: because the refuser is a disbeliever by consensus. His saying: (Yes, it is a condition): a correction/digression to his saying: (It is only a condition of perfection in it). And it is supported by His statement, the Most High: "Those are the ones in whose hearts He has inscribed faith" (1), and his statement, peace and blessings be upon him: "O Allah, make my heart firm upon Your religion" (2).
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage defines the nature of iman (faith) and clarifies the role of the verbal utterance of the two shahadahs. It begins by stating that iman is simple (baset), meaning it consists solely of internal attestation (tasdiq). The author then applies this definition: a person who believes in his heart but does not verbally confess, provided there is no excuse preventing him and no obstinate refusal, is still a true believer before Allah and will not abide eternally in Hellfire. The author then distinguishes the utterance as a condition of perfection (shart kamal) like other acts of worship, not a condition of validity (shart sihha) nor a part of the essence of iman. The following sentences are commentary by al-Sawi explaining the earlier text. He clarifies that 'based on what we mentioned' refers to two definitions of iman: either the internal speech that follows knowledge or the knowledge itself. He explains the exception for the excused (al-ma'dhur) who is unanimously accepted as a believer even on the view that iman is composite. The refuser (al-abi) is a kafir by consensus. Finally, he addresses the statement 'yes, it is a condition' as a digression (istidrak) correcting the impression that utterance is only a condition of perfection, and provides evidence from Quran and hadith.

Key Terms
الإيمان
التصديق
النطق بالشهادتين
شرط كمال
شرط صحة
المعذور
الآبي
حديث النفس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that verbal confession (shahadah) is part of the reality of iman, so that its absence invalidates faith even if the person believes in the heart.
Correction: According to al-Sawi, iman is simple (tasdiq), and utterance is only a condition of perfection. A person who believes in the heart but does not utter, without excuse or refusal, is still a believer before Allah.

Checkpoint
According to al-Sawi, what is the ruling on a person who believes in heart but does not utter the shahadah due to no excuse?
A) He is not a believer because utterance is required for validity
B) He is a believer before Allah, saved from eternal punishment
C) He is considered a disbeliever by consensus
D) His faith is valid only if he later utters

Key Takeaway
Faith (iman) is simple, consisting of internal attestation; verbal utterance is a condition of perfection, not a part of its essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you been reluctant to declare your faith openly—was it due to a genuine excuse or a hidden refusal? How does Al-Sawi's classification force you to examine your own heart?
?
If your faith remains purely internal without being expressed through words or actions, are you content with being a believer who is 'safe from the Fire' but deficient in perfection? What does that reveal about your love for Allah?
?
The refuser is a disbeliever by consensus. Have you ever remained silent about your faith out of fear or social pressure? Could that silence be a form of refusal? How will this lesson change your response next time?

The First Sentence: Meaning of الإله
Introduction and definition of الإله and the first sentence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that الإله means 'the one worshipped with right' and that the first sentence لا إله إلا الله negates all false deities.

Core Idea
الإله is not just any god, but the only true object of worship.

This lesson begins the study of the two sentences that contain all divine rulings. The first and most foundational is لا إله إلا الله. We will explore the precise meaning of الإله as defined by the author, and understand the core meaning of the kalima.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening lines
Start by reading from 'ما قد مضى ذكره' to 'لا معبود بحق'. This sets the context that all rulings are in two sentences, and the first is defined.
2
Identify the definition of الإله
Notice how the author defines الإله as 'المعبود بحق'. This is a technical definition, not just 'god' in a general sense.
3
Reflect on the meaning of 'لا معبود بحق'
Understand that the negation targets any worshipped being other than Allah. This is the concise meaning of the shahada.
This meaning excludes false gods.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما قد مضى ذكره من سائر أي جميع (الأحكام) الإلهيات : والنَّبويَّات والسمعيات، بيان ذلك أنَّها جملتان: — الجملة الأولى : لا إله إلّا الله والإله هو المعبود بحق، فالمعنى : لا معبود بحق. قوله : ( لا إله إلا الله يصح نصب لفظ الجلالة ورفعه والمختار الرَّفع ؛ لقول ابن مالك (۱) : ... وبعد نفي أو كنفي انتخب إتـبـاع مـا ما اتصل ... وهي من قبيل العام المخصوص، وهو ما كان عمومه مراداً في اللفظ لا في المعنى، فالاستثناء على ذلك متصل من حيث دخول لفظ الجلالة في عموم اللفظ وهو مُخْرَجٌ معنى، فقوله: (إلَّا الله) كشف لما راعاه في القلب عند النَّفي، وهو من باب عموم السَّلب لا سلب العموم، وإلا كان الاستثناء منقطعاً، وهو خلاف التحقيق. قوله: (فالمعنى: لا معبود بحق) : أي : معناها المطابق، والمنفي المعبود بحقِّ غير الله في ذهن المؤمن وفي نفس الأمر، لا في ذهن الكافر، إذ هو ثابت لا يتأتّى نفيه، فهو من المؤمن إخبار عما في قلبه، وما في نفس الأمر، ولا ينظر لما في قلوب الكفَّار، وحذف تنوين (معبود) مشاكلة للفظ (إله) ، وإلَّا فحقه النّصب ؛ لكونه شبيهاً بالمضاف.
It has been mentioned before from all the rulings of the divine matters, prophetic matters, and scriptural matters. The explanation is that they are two sentences: The first sentence: 'La ilaha illa Allah'. Al-Ilah is the one worshipped with right. So the meaning is: No one worshipped with right. His statement: 'La ilaha illa Allah' is correct with nasb or raf' of the word of majesty, and the chosen is raf' due to the saying of Ibn Malik: ... 'And after negation or like negation, choose following what what is connected' ... And it is of the type of general that is specific, which is that its generality is intended in wording not in meaning. So the exception on that is continuous because the word of majesty enters into the generality of the wording but is excluded in meaning. So his statement 'illa Allah' reveals what he observed in his heart at the negation. And it is from the category of generality of negation, not negation of generality. Otherwise the exception would be discontinuous, which is contrary to verification. His statement: 'So the meaning: no worshipped with right' meaning: its true meaning. And the negated is the one worshipped with right other than Allah in the mind of the believer and in reality, not in the mind of the disbeliever, because it is established, impossible to negate. So it is from the believer an informing of what is in his heart and what is in reality, and he does not regard what is in the hearts of the disbelievers. And the tanwin of 'ma'bood' was omitted for harmony with the word 'ilah', otherwise it should be in nasb because it is similar to a genitive construction.
Source pages p. 394

Commentary
This passage defines the first of the two sentences that encapsulate the rulings of theology. It analyzes the grammatical construction of 'لا إله إلا الله', explaining that the word 'الله' can be in either nasb or raf', with raf' being preferred based on Ibn Malik's rule (though the exact line is partially lost). It then clarifies that this statement is of the type 'general that is specific', meaning the generality is in wording but not in meaning, so the exception is continuous (mutassil). The author emphasizes that it is 'generality of negation' not 'negation of generality'; otherwise the exception would be discontinuous (munqati'), which is incorrect. Then it explains the meaning 'لا معبود بحق', noting that the negation applies in the believer's mind and in reality, not in the disbeliever's mind, because for the latter the concept of a worshipped other than Allah is established. The omission of tanwin on 'معبود' is for stylistic harmony with 'إله', though grammatically it should be in the accusative.

Key Terms
الإله
المعبود بحق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think الإله simply means 'God' without the connotation of being the only true object of worship.
Correction: الإله is specifically defined as 'the one worshipped with right', emphasizing that worship is due only to Him.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does الإله mean?
A) God
B) The one worshipped with right
C) The Creator
D) The merciful

Key Takeaway
الإله is not a generic term but specifically means the only true object of worship, and the shahada negates all false gods.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What things in your life do you treat as objects of worship that are not worthy? How will you actively negate them based on the meaning of 'لا إله إلا الله'?
?
The definition of الإله is 'the one worshipped with right'. Are you worshipping anything other than Allah in your heart, even if you outwardly confess the shahada? What specific steps will you take to remove that?

Grammatical Analysis and العام المخصوص
Grammatical i'rab options and logical classification of the exception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the grammatical possibilities for the word 'الله', the preference for raf', and the concept of العام المخصوص that makes the exception continuous.

Core Idea
The generality is only in wording, not in meaning.

Now we delve into the grammar of لا إله إلا الله. The author explains that the word 'الله' can be read in nasb or raf', with raf' being preferred based on a rule from Ibn Malik. More importantly, he clarifies that this sentence is an example of العام المخصوص (general that is specific), which determines the type of exception.

Guided Path
1
Read the grammatical discussion
Read from 'قوله : ( لا إله إلا الله يصح نصب لفظ الجلالة ورفعه' until 'وهو خلاف التحقيق'. Focus on the i'rab options and the mention of Ibn Malik.
2
Understand العام المخصوص
The author states that the sentence is from the category 'العام المخصوص', meaning it is general in wording but specific in meaning. This leads to the exception being متصل (continuous).
Contrast with العام الذي لم يخصص.
3
Distinguish عموم السلب from سلب العموم
The author says the sentence is from 'عموم السلب' (generality of negation) not 'سلب العموم' (negation of generality). If it were the latter, the exception would be منقطع (discontinuous), which is incorrect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

His statement: 'La ilaha illa Allah' is correct with nasb or raf' of the word of majesty, and the chosen is raf' due to the saying of Ibn Malik: ... 'And after negation or like negation, choose following what what is connected' ... And it is of the type of general that is specific, which is that its generality is intended in wording not in meaning. So the exception on that is continuous because the word of majesty enters into the generality of the wording but is excluded in meaning. So his statement 'illa Allah' reveals what he observed in his heart at the negation. And it is from the category of generality of negation, not negation of generality. Otherwise the exception would be discontinuous, which is contrary to verification. His statement: 'So the meaning: no worshipped with right' meaning: its true meaning. And the negated is the one worshipped with right other than Allah in the mind of the believer and in reality, not in the mind of the disbeliever, because it is established, impossible to negate. The grammatical and logical structure confirms that the negation is all-encompassing and the exception is continuous, affirming only Allah as the true deity.

?

?
The source says the negation covers every individual (عموم السلب). Is there any 'ilah'—any ultimate concern or obsession—that you have secretly reserved a place for while still verbally reciting the kalima? Write down that thing and then physically destroy the paper as a symbol of your commitment to make your recitation match your reality.
?
If the exception is continuous (متصل), then claiming to negate all false gods while still holding on to one in your heart is a contradiction. This week, identify one specific situation where you are tempted to rely on something other than Allah—perhaps seeking validation from people or worrying about provision. The Meaning of لا معبود بحق and Its Nuances
Explains the meaning and scope of 'لا معبود بحق' and the grammatical point of tanwin omission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise scope of negation in 'لا معبود بحق', the difference between the believer's and disbeliever's perspective, and the grammatical reason for omitting the tanwin.

Core Idea
Negation applies in reality and in the believer's heart, not in the disbeliever's mind.

The author unpacks the deeper meaning of the phrase 'لا معبود بحق'. He explains that the negation is not absolute; it targets the concept of any worshipped being other than Allah in the believer's mind and in external reality. The disbeliever's belief in false gods is not negated—they exist as beliefs. Additionally, a subtle grammatical point about the omission of tanwin is clarified.

Guided Path
1
Read the explanation of the negation
Read from 'قوله: (فالمعنى: لا معبود بحق)' to the end. Focus on the phrases 'لا في ذهن الكافر' and 'فهو من المؤمن إخبار عما في قلبه'.
2
Distinguish the two scopes of negation
Notice that the negation applies to 'المعبود بحق غير الله' in the believer's mind and in reality, but not in the disbeliever's mind because for them false gods are established.
This shows the statement is a report of truth, not a denial of false beliefs.
3
Understand the grammatical omission
The author explains that the tanwin of 'معبود' is omitted for harmony with 'إله', though it should logically be in the accusative with tanwin.
This is a stylistic choice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

It has been mentioned before from all the rulings of the divine matters, prophetic matters, and scriptural matters. The explanation is that they are two sentences: The first sentence: 'La ilaha illa Allah'. Al-Ilah is the one worshipped with right. So the meaning is: No one worshipped with right. His statement: 'La ilaha illa Allah' is correct with nasb or raf' of the word of majesty, and the chosen is raf' due to the saying of Ibn Malik: ... 'And after negation or like negation, choose following what what is connected' ... And it is of the type of general that is specific, which is that its generality is intended in wording not in meaning. So the exception on that is continuous because the word of majesty enters into the generality of the wording but is excluded in meaning. And the negated is the one worshipped with right other than Allah in the mind of the believer and in reality, not in the mind of the disbeliever, because it is established, impossible to negate. So it is from the believer an informing of what is in his heart and what is in reality, and he does not regard what is in the hearts of the disbelievers. And the tanwin of 'ma'bood' was omitted for harmony with the word 'ilah', otherwise it should be in nasb because it is similar to a genitive construction.
Source pages p. 394

Commentary
This passage defines the first of the two sentences that encapsulate the rulings of theology. It analyzes the grammatical construction of 'لا إله إلا الله', explaining that the word 'الله' can be in either nasb or raf', with raf' being preferred based on Ibn Malik's rule (though the exact line is partially lost). It then clarifies that this statement is of the type 'general that is specific', meaning the generality is in wording but not in meaning, so the exception is continuous (mutassil). The author emphasizes that it is 'generality of negation' not 'negation of generality'; otherwise the exception would be discontinuous (munqati'), which is incorrect. Then it explains the meaning 'لا معبود بحق', noting that the negation applies in the believer's mind and in reality, not in the disbeliever's mind, because for the latter the concept of a worshipped other than Allah is established. The omission of tanwin on 'معبود' is for stylistic harmony with 'إله', though grammatically it should be in the accusative.

Key Terms
المعبود بحق
تنوين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the negation in 'لا معبود بحق' denies the existence of any worshipped beings at all, even false ones.
Correction: The negation is specific: it negates the truth of any worshipped other than Allah in the believer's mind and in reality, not in the minds of disbelievers.

Checkpoint
In whose mind is the negation of 'المعبود بحق غير الله' applicable according to the passage?
A) The disbeliever's mind
B) The believer's mind and reality
C) Everyone's mind
D) No one's mind

Key Takeaway
The negation in the shahada is a statement of truth and belief, not a denial of the existence of false gods in others' minds.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the shahada, do you genuinely negate all false gods from your heart, or do you still cling to worldly objects of worship like status, wealth, or approval? What false god must you uproot today?
?
The shahada does not deny the false gods that others believe in. Does this understanding humble you when you encounter disbelief, or does it make you complacent? How can you live out your shahada with both conviction and compassion?
?
If your shahada is a truthful declaration of what is in your heart and in reality, are you prepared to have your actions and priorities examined by that standard? What would your life look like if every choice reflected the negation of all false gods?

The Shahada: Logical Implications of Self-Sufficiency and Dependence
Al-Sawi explains how la ilaha illallah entails divine self-sufficiency and creaturely dependence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the shahada logically implies God's self-sufficiency and the dependence of all creation upon Him.

Core Idea
The shahada is not just a declaration; it implies absolute divine independence.

Al-Sawi shows that the simple statement 'la ilaha illallah' carries deeper implications. By negating divinity from all but God, it simultaneously affirms God's self-sufficiency and the total dependence of everything else. This passage unpacks that logical entailment.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the parenthetical definition of divinity.
2
Identify the two logical moves
The passage distinguishes explicit negation and implicit affirmation. Find where the author says 'explicitly' (mantuqan) and 'implicitly' (mafhoman). Understand these two aspects.
3
Reflect on the entailments
Consider why the explicit negation and implicit affirmation necessarily lead to God's self-sufficiency and creaturely dependence. Ask: If God alone is truly divine, what does that imply about Him and everything else?
This connects to the core idea of divine independence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقد دلّت هذه الجملة على نفي الألوهية – التي هي المعبود العبادة، كما عرفت - عن كلّ ما سواه منطوقاً، وعلى ثبوتها له تعالى وحده مفهوماً، وهذا يستلزم استغناءه تعالى عن كل ما سواه، وافتقار كلِّ ما سواه إليه تعالى
Thus this sentence indicates the negation of divinity – which is worship of the deity, as you have known – from everything other than Him explicitly, and the affirmation of it for Him alone implicitly, and this necessitates His self-sufficiency from everything other than Him, and the dependence of everything other than Him upon Him.
Source pages p. 395

Commentary
This passage explains the logical inference from the shahada (the statement 'la ilaha illallah'). The author states that the shahada explicitly denies divinity (al-uluhiyya, defined as being worshipped with worship) to all besides God, and implicitly affirms it for Him alone. From this, two necessary consequences follow: first, God's complete self-sufficiency (istighna') from everything else, and second, the dependence (iftiqar) of everything else upon Him. The author presents this as an entailment derived from the very structure of the shahada.

Key Terms
الألوهية
العبادة
منطوقاً
مفهوماً
استغناء
افتقار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The shahada only negates other gods without implying any positive attributes about God or creation.
Correction: The passage shows the shahada logically entails God's self-sufficiency and creation's dependence, which are positive theological doctrines.

Checkpoint
What does the shahada implicitly affirm according to this passage?
A) The possibility of other gods
B) Divinity for God alone
C) The dependence of God on creation
D) The createdness of the world

Key Takeaway
The shahada's logical structure reveals that God alone is worthy of worship, and all else depends on Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite la ilaha illallah, do you truly feel that you are nothing without Allah? In what specific area of your life do you still act as if you are self-sufficient? Commit to one practical change today that acknowledges your absolute dependence on Him.
?
The shahada logically demands that every being besides Allah is dependent on Him. Have you been placing your trust in any created thing—people, money, health—as if it could stand independently of Allah? Repent and renew your tawheed by relying solely on Him.
?
If the shahada implies God's self-sufficiency, then He needs nothing from you. Yet you constantly struggle with egotism as if you can bring Him benefit or harm. How will you train your heart this week to live in the reality that Allah is perfectly self-sufficient and you are completely in need?

Definition of al-Sayr and al-Suluk and the Ten Principles
The author defines al-sayr and al-suluk and lists the ten principles of the path.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of al-sayr and al-suluk in Sufi terminology and learn the ten essential principles (usul) of the spiritual path.

Core Idea
The spiritual journey (sayr) is the engine of wayfaring (suluk), powered by ten principles.

This passage from a classical commentary on a creedal text transitions into spiritual wayfaring terminology. The author clarifies what it means to journey to Allah and then enumerates the foundational principles that distinguish the path of the spiritual seekers. As you read, note how the four common principles apply to all Muslims while the six specific ones are reserved for those on the path.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definitions of sayr and suluk
Read the first section where the author defines al-sayr as turning the heart to Allah while opposing the soul's desires, and al-suluk as the way of prophets and saints.
Notice that sayr is the cause and suluk is the actual traversing.
2
Identify the ten principles and their categories
Find the list of ten principles: four common (reflection, gratitude, patience, renewing repentance) and six specific (constant remembrance, silence, wakefulness, hunger, seclusion, and the knowing shaykh).
The poetry excerpt summarizes five of the six specific ones.
3
Reflect on the purpose of these principles
Consider why the specific principles are necessary for the people of the path and how they facilitate spiritual wayfaring.
These principles are not mere theory but practical disciplines.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما السير إلى الله تعالى: فهو توجه القلب إلى الرب مع مخالفة النفس في شهواتها - ولو مباحة - طلباً لمرضاة الله تعالى وإيثاراً له على ما سواه، فالسير كالسبب في السلوك، وقد يطلق السلوك على المعنى الثاني أيضاً.
والسلوك إلى الله تعالى طريقة النبيين والصديقين والعلماء العاملين إلا أنه مختلف:
والحاصل: أن الشارح عد الأصول عشرة، لكن منها أربعة مشتركة بين أهل الطريق وغيرهم؛ وهي: الفكر، والشكر، والصبر، وتجديد التوبة.
وستة مخصوصة بأهل الطريق؛ لتوقف وصولهم عليه عادة؛ وهي: دوام الذكر، والصمت والشهر والجوع والعزلة، والشيخ العارف الذي يدل على الله تعالى. وقد نظم بعضهم الستة المختصة ما عدا الذكر والشيخ بقوله: [من الرجز] بيت الولاية قسمت أركانه ساداتنا فيه من الأبدال ما بين صمت واعتزال دائم والجوع والشهر النزيه العالي.
قوله: (على المعنى الثاني): أي: وهو التوجه إلى الرب مع مخالفة النفس في شهواتها... إلخ، فمعنى تسميته سالكاً؛ أي متسبب في السلوك.
(1) البيتين للشيخ الأكبر محيي الدين ابن عربي، وهي في «الفتوحات المكية» (2). (182)
As for the journey (al-sayr) to Allah the Exalted: it is the turning of the heart to the Lord along with opposing the soul in its desires – even if permissible – seeking the pleasure of Allah the Exalted and preferring Him over other than Him. So the journey is like a cause in the spiritual wayfaring (al-suluk), and the spiritual wayfaring may also be applied to the second meaning. And the spiritual wayfaring to Allah the Exalted is the way of the prophets, the truthful ones, and the knowledgeable scholars. Except that it varies: The summary is that the commentator counted the principles as ten: four are common between the people of the path and others – they are: reflection, gratitude, patience, and renewal of repentance. And six are specific to the people of the path, because their arrival normally depends on them: they are: constant remembrance, silence, wakefulness, hunger, seclusion, and the knowing master (shaykh) who guides to Allah the Exalted. And some of them composed, regarding the six specific except for remembrance and the master, saying (in rajaz meter): The house of sainthood divided its pillars our masters among them from the substitutes between silence and constant seclusion and hunger and the lofty wakefulness. His saying: 'according to the second meaning', i.e., it is the turning to the Lord along with opposing the soul in its desires… etc., so the meaning of calling him a wayfarer is that he is one who engages in wayfaring.
Source pages p. 417

Commentary
This passage defines two key terms in Sufi spirituality – al-sayr (journey) and al-suluk (wayfaring) – and then enumerates the ten principles that underpin the spiritual path. The author begins by explaining that 'al-sayr ila Allah' means directing the heart toward the Lord while resisting the ego's desires, even permissible ones, with the intention of seeking Allah's pleasure. This journey is the means (sabab) to suluk, which is the actual traversing of the path. Suluk is the way of prophets, truthful saints, and knowledgeably practicing scholars, but it varies in degrees. Then the author summarizes that the commentator (al-sharih) enumerated ten 'usul' (principles): four are common to all Muslims (reflection, gratitude, patience, and renewal of repentance), and six are specific to the spiritual seekers because their progress depends on them: constant remembrance (dhikr), silence (samt), wakefulness (sahar), hunger (ju'), seclusion (uzla), and a knowing spiritual guide (al-shaykh al-arif). The author also cites a poetic composition (attributed to Ibn Arabi) that encapsulates five of these (excluding the guide). Finally, he clarifies that the phrase 'on the second meaning' refers to the interpretation of suluk as the act of turning to the Lord with self-opposition, and thus calling a person 'salik' means one who engages in the cause (mutasabbib) of suluk. The passage is primarily definitional and enumerative, drawing on the commentator's framework.

الصبر

الذكر
الصمت

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These ten principles are part of Ilm al-Kalam (theology) proper.
Correction: They are principles of spiritual wayfaring (suluk) found in Sufi works, not directly from kalam.

Checkpoint

B) Six
C) Ten
D) Seven

Key Takeaway
The spiritual path consists of ten principles: four common obligations and six specialized disciplines for the wayfarer.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You learned that the journey requires opposing the soul even in permissible desires. Think of one permissible pleasure you habitually indulge in. Is it drawing you closer to Allah or becoming a veil? What concrete step will you take tonight to oppose your soul in that matter?
?
Of the six specific principles for the wayfarers (constant remembrance, silence, wakefulness, hunger, seclusion, a spiritual guide), which one is most absent from your life right now? How will you begin practicing it this week, and what will you sacrifice to make space for it?

The passage says the four common principles—reflection, gratitude, patience, renewal of repentance—are for all Muslims. Examine your day: where did you fail in one of these? Write down one honest regret and one specific action you commit to tomorrow to renew your repentance.

Definition and Beginning Stages of Repentance
Definition and beginning stages of repentance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic and technical definition of tawbah and the progressive stages of its beginning.

Core Idea
Repentance is a return that starts with major sins and ends with repenting from even seeing one's own goodness.

Before we can discuss the deeper realities of repentance, we must first know what it means and how it begins. This lesson unpacks tawbah's core definition and the eight stages of its beginning, moving from outward sins to inward self-focus. By understanding this progression, you will see that repentance is not a single act but a continuous purification.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on definition and beginning
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation to grasp the author's progression from definition to the list of beginning stages.
Notice the author first places tawbah as the first foundation of the path of the devotees.
2
Identify the linguistic vs. technical definition
Find where the author distinguishes the general meaning of 'return' from the specific Islamic meaning of returning to what is praiseworthy. Understand that the technical meaning adds a moral dimension.
The key contrast is 'absolute return' vs. 'return from blameworthy to praiseworthy'.
3
Trace the beginning stages
List the eight stages mentioned: from major sins, to minor sins, to disliked acts, to leaving what is not best, to repenting from seeing good deeds, to repenting from considering oneself poor/truthful, and finally from any thought not pleasing to Allah.
Each stage is more subtle than the last, showing that repentance deepens as one grows spiritually.
4
Reflect on the purpose of this progression
Ask yourself: why does the author include repenting from even good deeds and thoughts? This shows that true repentance aims to purify even the ego's hidden attachments.
This connects to the idea that tawbah is ongoing and not just for outward sins.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وأصولها عشرة) : أي : أصول طريق الشُّطَار من أهل المحبَّة والشوق، وتقدَّم أنَّ المختصّ بهم ستّة منها، والأربعة عامة .

قوله : (الأول : التَّوبة : هي لغة : مطلق الرُّجوع، واصطلاحاً : الرُّجوع عما كان مذموماً في الشَّرع إلى ما هو محمود فيه، ولها بداية ونهاية. فبدايتها : التَّوبة من الكبائر ، ثمَّ الصَّغائر ، ثمَّ المكروهات، ثمَّ خلاف الأولى، ثمَّ من رؤية الحسنات، ثمَّ من رؤية أنه صار معدوداً من فقراء الزَّمان، ثمَّ من رؤية أنَّه صدق في التّوبة، ثمَّ من خاطر يخطر له في غير مرضاة الله

ولو صغيرة على التحقيق، وإليه أشار بقوله : (وجدد) وجوباً (التّوبة) :أي: الرُّجوع إلى الله تعالى (للأوزار أي من أجل ارتكاب الأوزار ، وزر وهو المعصية.
جمع

وأركانها ثلاثة :
- الندم على ما وقع منه من المخالفات لمراعاة حقّ الله سبحانه -

وأمَّا نهايتها: : فكلَّما غفل عن شهود ربِّه طرفة عين بدأ بالتّوبة؛ لأنَّها أساس لكل مقام يرتقي إليه العبد حتّى يموت، فكما أن من لا أرض له فلا بناء له، فكذلك من لا توبة له فلا حال له ولا مقام.
ومن كلام العارفين: من أحكم مقام توبته ؛ حفظه الله تعالى من سائر الشوائب التي في الأعمال).
His saying: (And its foundations are ten) meaning: the foundations of the path of the devotees (shuṭṭār) among the people of love and longing, and it has been mentioned that six of them are specific to them, and four are general. His saying: (The first: Repentance: it is, linguistically, absolute return, and terminologically: return from what was blameworthy in the Law to what is praiseworthy in it, and it has a beginning and an end. Its beginning: repentance from major sins, then minor sins, then disliked things, then matters contrary to the better choice, then from seeing good deeds, then from seeing that he has become counted among the poor of the time, then from seeing that he is truthful in repentance, then from a thought that occurs to him that is not in Allah's pleasure. Even if small, in verification; and to it he pointed with his saying: (And renew) as an obligation (repentance) meaning: return to Allah Almighty (for sins) meaning: for the commission of sins, and a sin (wizr) is disobedience. The pillars of repentance: And its pillars are three: regret over what occurred from him of violations in consideration of the right of Allah Almighty. And as for its end: whenever he is heedless of the vision of his Lord for the blink of an eye, he begins repentance; because it is the foundation for every station the servant ascends to until he dies. Just as one who has no land has no building, so too one who has no repentance has no state and no station. And from the sayings of the gnostics: 'Whoever perfects the station of his repentance, Allah Almighty protects him from all the impurities that are in deeds.')
Source pages p. 430

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part definition and explanation of tawbah (repentance). The author first clarifies that the 'ten foundations' refer to the path of the 'shuṭṭār' (a term for ardent lovers of Allah), with six being specific to them and four general. He then defines tawbah linguistically as 'absolute return' and technically as 'return from what is blameworthy in the Law to what is praiseworthy.' The definition is expanded by stating that tawbah has a beginning and an end. The beginning moves through stages: from major sins, to minor sins, to disliked acts, to leaving what is not best, to even repenting from seeing one's own good deeds or from considering oneself a true penitent. The end is continuous: whenever one becomes heedless of Allah for an instant, one returns to repentance. This shows tawbah is not a single event but a lifelong state. The passage then notes that a line in the text ('and renew repentance') obligates renewal for even small sins (on a verified view). It then lists the three pillars of repentance: regret (nadam) for past violations of Allah's rights. Finally, it quotes the gnostics: perfecting the station of repentance protects from spiritual impurities in all deeds. The simile of land and building underscores that tawbah is the foundation for all spiritual states and stations.

Correction: The passage shows repentance begins with major sins but extends to minor sins, disliked acts, and even subtle spiritual states like seeing one's own good deeds.

Key Takeaway
Tawbah is a return to Allah that progressively purifies the soul from outward sins and inward attachments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The final stage is repenting from a thought that occurs to you that is not in Allah's pleasure. Have you ever considered your fleeting thoughts as needing repentance? Will you begin to notice and turn away from each thought that displeases Allah?

End, Pillars, and Renewal of Repentance
Renewal obligation, pillars, end, and gnostic saying about repentance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three pillars of tawbah, its continuous end, and the obligation to renew repentance even for small sins.

Core Idea
Repentance is a lifelong foundation supported by regret and renewed at every heedlessness.

Having learned the definition and beginning stages of repentance, we now turn to its essential components and its ultimate nature. This lesson covers the three pillars (with regret as the main pillar), the end of tawbah (continuous return at every moment of heedlessness), and the obligation to renew repentance for every sin, no matter how small. It concludes with a profound saying from the gnostics about the protection that comes from perfecting this station.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on renewal and pillars
Read the Arabic passage from 'ولو صغيرة على التحقيق' to the end. Then read the translation to understand the topics: obligation to renew repentance, the pillars, the end, and the gnostic quote.
Notice the author uses a simile: no land without building, no state without repentance.
2
Understand the obligation to renew repentance
The passage states that one must renew repentance for sins (awzār) even if small. This shows repentance is not a one-time event but must be repeated for every act of disobedience.
The phrase 'وجدد وجوباً' indicates it is an obligation.
3
Identify the three pillars of repentance
Find where the author lists the pillars. Only one pillar is explicitly mentioned here: regret (nadam) over violations of Allah's right. The other two ( cessation and resolve) are implied by the context.
Regret is singled out as the core; the other two are often mentioned elsewhere.
4
Learn about the end of repentance
The end of tawbah is not a single point but a state of continuous return: whenever one is heedless of Allah for a blink, one begins repentance. This shows tawbah is a lifelong foundation.
The simile of land and building emphasizes that without repentance, no spiritual state (ḥāl) or station (maqām) can stand.
5
Reflect on the gnostic saying
The passage ends with a saying of the gnostics: perfecting the station of repentance protects from impurities in deeds. This highlights the transformative power of sincere repentance.
It ties back to the idea that tawbah purifies both outward and inward.

وتعالى

وأمَّا نهايتها: : فكلَّما غفل عن شهود ربِّه طرفة عين بدأ بالتّوبة؛ لأنَّها أساس لكل مقام يرتقي إليه العبد حتّى يموت، فكما أن من لا أرض له فلا بناء له، فكذلك من لا توبة له فلا حال له ولا مقام.
ومن كلام العارفين: من أحكم مقام توبته ؛ حفظه الله تعالى من سائر الشوائب التي في الأعمال).
His saying: (And its foundations are ten) meaning: the foundations of the path of the devotees (shuṭṭār) among the people of love and longing, and it has been mentioned that six of them are specific to them, and four are general. His saying: (The first: Repentance: it is, linguistically, absolute return, and terminologically: return from what was blameworthy in the Law to what is praiseworthy in it, and it has a beginning and an end. Its beginning: repentance from major sins, then minor sins, then disliked things, then matters contrary to the better choice, then from seeing good deeds, then from seeing that he has become counted among the poor of the time, then from seeing that he is truthful in repentance, then from a thought that occurs to him that is not in Allah's pleasure. Even if small, in verification; and to it he pointed with his saying: (And renew) as an obligation (repentance) meaning: return to Allah Almighty (for sins) meaning: for the commission of sins, and a sin (wizr) is disobedience. The pillars of repentance: And its pillars are three: regret over what occurred from him of violations in consideration of the right of Allah Almighty. And as for its end: whenever he is heedless of the vision of his Lord for the blink of an eye, he begins repentance; because it is the foundation for every station the servant ascends to until he dies. Just as one who has no land has no building, so too one who has no repentance has no state and no station. And from the sayings of the gnostics: 'Whoever perfects the station of his repentance, Allah Almighty protects him from all the impurities that are in deeds.')
Source pages p. 430

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part definition and explanation of tawbah (repentance). The author first clarifies that the 'ten foundations' refer to the path of the 'shuṭṭār' (a term for ardent lovers of Allah), with six being specific to them and four general. He then defines tawbah linguistically as 'absolute return' and technically as 'return from what is blameworthy in the Law to what is praiseworthy.' The definition is expanded by stating that tawbah has a beginning and an end. The beginning moves through stages: from major sins, to minor sins, to disliked acts, to leaving what is not best, to even repenting from seeing one's own good deeds or from considering oneself a true penitent. The end is continuous: whenever one becomes heedless of Allah for an instant, one returns to repentance. This shows tawbah is not a single event but a lifelong state. The passage then notes that a line in the text ('and renew repentance') obligates renewal for even small sins (on a verified view). It then lists the three pillars of repentance: regret (nadam) for past violations of Allah's rights. Finally, it quotes the gnostics: perfecting the station of repentance protects from spiritual impurities in all deeds. The simile of land and building underscores that tawbah is the foundation for all spiritual states and stations.

Key Terms
التوبة
الندم
الأوزار
الكبائر
الصغائر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Repentance is only for major sins and only needs to be done once.
Correction: The passage emphasizes renewing repentance for even small sins and describes its end as a continuous return at every heedlessness.

Checkpoint
What is the end of tawbah according to the passage?
A) Reaching a state of sinlessness
B) Dying while repenting
C) Returning to repentance whenever one is heedless of Allah, even for a blink
D) Completing the three pillars

Key Takeaway
Repentance is a continuous obligation and foundation of spiritual life, supported by regret and renewed at every heedlessness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When was the last time you felt genuine regret (nadam) for a sin? Have you been quick to renew your repentance after each heedlessness, or do you let days pass without turning back to Allah? What is holding you back from immediate tawbah?
?
The gnostic says: 'Whoever perfects the station of repentance is protected from impurities in deeds.' Are you content with a repentance that only covers major sins while ignoring the small impurities that creep into your worship? Will you examine your deeds tonight and repent for even the slightest heedlessness?
?
Repentance is described as the foundation for every spiritual state. Consider your current spiritual state: is it built on a solid foundation of constant tawbah, or are you trying to build a house without land? What step will you take today to reinforce your repentance?

Repentance (Tawba) and Its Three Pillars
Al-Sawi defines tawba, states its final aim, and enumerates its three pillars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of tawba, its three obligatory pillars, and the practical requirements for valid repentance.

Core Idea
Repentance requires regret, resolve, and immediate desisting—all three are mandatory.

Al-Sawi begins by affirming that repentance is obligatory even for minor sins, then defines it as returning to Allah. He enumerates the three pillars that constitute valid tawba and explains that it is the foundation of every spiritual station. This passage provides both the conceptual framework and the practical conditions for repentance, including how to address sins against others.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for context
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the basic definition of tawba as returning to Allah and that it is obligatory even for small sins.
Notice the phrase 'وجدد التوبة' indicates renewal.
2
Identify the three pillars
Find where Al-Sawi enumerates the three pillars. They are: regret (الندم), resolve not to return (العزم على ألا يعود), and immediate desisting (الإقلاع عن الذنب).
These are explicitly called أركان التوبة.
3
Understand the final aim and analogy
Read the section about the final aim (نهايتها) and the analogy of the land and building. This shows repentance is the foundation for all spiritual stations.
The gnostic saying at the end reinforces this.
4
Consider practical application
Notice the conditions for the third pillar: it applies only if the sin is still ongoing. For sins against others, one must return rights and seek forgiveness.
This is a crucial practical rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو صغيرة على التحقيق، وإليه أشار بقوله : (وجدد) وجوباً (التّوبة) :أي: الرُّجوع إلى الله تعالى (للأوزار أي من أجل ارتكاب الأوزار، وزر وهو المعصية. جمع أركان التوبة : وأركانها ثلاثة : - الندم على ما وقع منه من المخالفات لمراعاة حقّ الله سبحانه - وتعالى وأمَّا نهايتها: : فكلَّما غفل عن شهود ربِّه طرفة عين بدأ بالتّوبة؛ لأنَّها أساس لكل مقام يرتقي إليه العبد حتّى يموت، فكما أن من لا أرض له فلا بناء له، فكذلك من لا توبة له فلا حال له ولا مقام. ومن كلام العارفين: من أحكم مقام توبته؛ حفظه الله تعالى من سائر الشوائب التي في الأعمال). والعزم على ألا يعود لمثله، وهذان لا بدَّ منهما في كلِّ توبة . - والثالث الإقلاع عن الذَّنب في الحال، وهذا إنَّما يتأتى في ذنب لم ينقض، فيجب الكف عن استتمام الزنا وشرب الخمر، وعن أذيَّة أحد، وردّ المظالم إلى أهلها واستسماح المظلوم إن أمكن، وإلّا استغفر له وتصدَّق له بما يمكنه، فإنّ الله تعالى إذا علم صدق العبد أرضي الله عنه خصماءه. قوله : ( في كلّ توبة أي من كلِّ ذنب . قوله : ( في ذنب لم ينقض : أي: بأن كان يمكن استمراره.
Even if a small sin, based on verification, and he indicated by his saying: (And renew) obligatory (repentance): i.e., returning to Allah the Exalted (for sins) i.e., because of committing sins, and sin is disobedience. Collectively, the pillars of repentance: and its pillars are three: — regret for what has occurred from him of violations for observing the right of Allah—may He be glorified and exalted—; and as for its final aim: then whenever he is heedless of witnessing his Lord for the blink of an eye, he begins repentance; because it is the foundation for every station the servant ascends to until he dies; just as one who has no land has no building, so likewise one who has no repentance has no state and no station. And from the sayings of the gnostics: Whosoever masters the station of his repentance, Allah the Exalted preserves him from all the impurities in his deeds. And the resolve not to return to its like; these two are necessary in every repentance. — And the third is desisting from the sin immediately, and this only applies to a sin that has not ended; so it is obligatory to refrain from completing fornication and drinking wine, and from harming anyone, and to return injustices to their people and to seek forgiveness from the wronged person if possible; otherwise, he seeks forgiveness for him and gives charity on his behalf as much as he can; for indeed Allah the Exalted, when He knows the sincerity of the servant, reconciles his adversaries with him. His saying: (in every repentance) i.e., from every sin. His saying: (in a sin that has not ended) i.e., that it was possible for it to continue.
Source pages p. 431

Commentary
This passage defines tawba (repentance) and explains its three pillars (arkan). The author begins by commenting on the base text's phrase 'Renew repentance', clarifying that repentance is obligatory even for a small sin—this is the verified position. He then defines tawba linguistically as 'returning to Allah', and identifies sins (awzar) as acts of disobedience. Next, he enumerates the three pillars: (1) regret (nadam) for past violations against Allah's right; (2) sincere resolve (‘azm) never to return to the sin; (3) immediate desisting (iqla‘) from the sin if it is still ongoing—for a past sin, this is replaced by the other two. The author also describes the 'final aim' (nihaya) of repentance: to maintain constant awareness of Allah, repenting at every moment of heedlessness. He compares repentance to the land on which a building stands—without it, no spiritual station is possible. He then gives practical details: if the sin involves harming others, one must rectify matters, compensate or seek forgiveness. The passage ends by glossing two phrases from the base text—'in every repentance' means from any sin, and 'a sin that has not ended' means one that can be continued, hence desisting is immediate.

أركان التوبة

العزم
الإقلاع
الأوزار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Repentance only requires regret.
Correction: Repentance has three obligatory pillars: regret, resolve not to return, and immediate desisting.

Checkpoint

A) Regret, anger, and apology
B) Regret, resolve not to return, and immediate desisting
C) Regret, fasting, and prayer
D) Confession, apology, and compensation

Key Takeaway
Repentance is built on three obligatory pillars: regret, resolve, and immediate desisting, and it also requires addressing the rights of others if harmed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When was the last time you truly felt regret for a sin against Allah, not just fear of punishment but because you violated His right? Have you neglected that feeling and simply moved on?
?
This passage says repentance is the foundation for every spiritual station. If you are struggling to progress in your faith, could it be because you are not repenting consistently—even for what you consider small slips? Will you commit today to renewing repentance every time you are heedless?
?
Al-Sawi mentions that if you have wronged someone, you must seek forgiveness or give charity for them. Is there someone you have harmed and never made amends with? What is stopping you from reaching out even if it's uncomfortable? Your repentance may be incomplete without it.

Conditions of Repentance and Its Acceptance
Al-Sawi explains conditions for valid repentance and its acceptance status.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the conditions for valid repentance and the different scholarly views on its acceptance for believers and disbelievers.

Core Idea
Repentance from a single sin is valid, but the path to Allah requires complete repentance.

This passage from al-Sawi clarifies an important nuance in the theology of repentance. It distinguishes between the general validity of repenting from one sin while persisting in another, and the stricter requirement for those on the spiritual journey. It then addresses the acceptance status of a disbeliever's repentance versus a believer's, citing Quran and scholarly consensus.

Guided Path
1
Understanding partial repentance
Read the first sentence: repentance from one sin is valid even if you do not repent from another. This is a key principle.
Contrast this with the spiritual journey (suluk) which requires repentance from all sins.
2
Definitive vs probabilistic acceptance
Notice the two categories: the disbeliever's repentance is accepted definitively (qat‘an) by consensus; the believer's repentance is accepted probabilistically (zannan) according to some scholars.
The Quranic verse (8:38) supports the first category.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this teaching balances hope (repentance is accepted) with accountability (sincerity is known only to Allah). Also note the scholarly disagreement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتصحُ التّوبة من ذنب دون آخر، بخلاف السير إلى الله تعالى؛ فإنَّه إنَّما يصحُ بالتَّوبة عن الجميع وتجب المبادرة بها، فتأخيرها ذنب آخر. وتوبة الكافر عن كفره بالإسلام مقبولة قطعاً، والمؤمن المذنب من ذنبه مقبولة ظنّاً، قوله : ( مقبولة قطعاً) أي : باتفاق الأشعري وإمام الحرمين والقاضي. لقوله تعالى: (قُل لِلَّذِينَ كَفَرُوا إِن يَنتَهُوا يُغْفَرْ لَهُم مَّا قَدْ سَلَفَ . قوله : ( مقبولة ظنّاً) : هو قول إمام الحرمين والقاضي.
And repentance from one sin is valid while leaving another, contrary to the journey to Allah the Exalted; for indeed it is only valid by repentance from all, and it is obligatory to hasten to it, so delaying it is another sin. And the repentance of the disbeliever from his disbelief by Islam is accepted definitively, and the sinful believer's repentance from his sin is accepted probabilistically. His saying: (accepted definitively) i.e., by agreement of Ash‘ari, Imam al-Haramayn, and al-Qadi. Due to His saying the Exalted: 'Say to those who have disbelieved, if they desist, what is past will be forgiven them' (Quran 8:38). His saying: (accepted probabilistically) is the saying of Imam al-Haramayn and al-Qadi.
Source pages p. 432

Commentary
This passage clarifies conditions for the validity and acceptance of tawba. First, it states that it is valid to repent from one sin while not repenting from another (e.g., repenting from theft but not from lying). This is in contrast to the spiritual journey (suluk) toward Allah, which requires repentance from all sins without exception. For that path, delaying repentance is itself a further sin. Then the author distinguishes between two types of acceptance: the repentance of a disbeliever (turning to Islam) is accepted definitively (qat‘an) by consensus of the three authorities: al-Ash‘ari, Imam al-Haramayn, and al-Qadi, citing the Quranic verse (8:38). In contrast, the repentance of a sinning believer is accepted probabilistically (zannan) according to Imam al-Haramayn and al-Qadi—implying it is hoped for but not absolutely guaranteed, as its full sincerity is known only to Allah. This nuanced view avoids both extreme optimism (that repentance is automatically accepted) and excessive despair (that it might be rejected).

Key Terms
تصح التوبة
السير إلى الله
مقبولة قطعاً
مقبولة ظنّاً
الأشعري
إمام الحرمين
القاضي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if they repent from one sin but persist in another, the first repentance is invalid.
Correction: The passage clarifies that repentance from a single sin is valid even if one does not repent from other sins.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, whose repentance is accepted 'definitively' (qat‘an)?
A) The sinful believer
B) The disbeliever who embraces Islam
C) Anyone who repents sincerely
D) Only the prophets

Key Takeaway
Partial repentance is valid, but the spiritual journey requires complete repentance; a disbeliever's repentance is accepted definitively, while a believer's is accepted probabilistically.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If your repentance is only accepted probabilistically, how does that change the urgency with which you seek forgiveness? Are you living as if God's acceptance is guaranteed?
?
The path to Allah requires repentance from all sins—not just the ones you find convenient. Which sin are you holding back from, and what is preventing you from giving it up completely?

The First Two Conditions: Regret and Resolve
Are the two conditions of repentance enumerated and explained.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first two conditions of repentance: regret and resolve, and why they are necessary in every tawbah.

Core Idea
Regret and resolve are the foundational pillars of sincere repentance.

This passage from Sanusi begins by establishing the essential components of repentance. Before discussing the practical aspects, he identifies two inner dispositions that must be present in every act of tawbah: regret for the past sin and a firm resolve not to return to it. These two conditions form the core of a valid repentance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice how Sanusi introduces 'the principles of the path' and then states that two things are necessary in every repentance.
The word 'أصول' implies a foundational list, and 'والعزم' is one of them.
2
Identify the two conditions
From the passage and its commentary, determine what the two conditions are. The Arabic mentions 'العزم' (resolve) and the commentary clarifies that the first is 'الندم' (regret).
These two are required in every repentance from any sin.
3
Understand the gloss on 'every repentance'
The gloss explains that 'every repentance' means every sin. This emphasizes the universality of these two conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أصول الطريق الموصلة إلى الله - والعزم على ألا يعود لمثله، وهذان لا بدَّ منهما في كلِّ توبة . - والثالث الإقلاع عن الذَّنب في الحال، وهذا إنَّما يتأتى في ذنب لم ينقض، فيجب الكف عن استتمام الزنا وشرب الخمر، وعن أذيَّة أحد، وردّ المظالم إلى أهلها واستسماح المظلوم إن أمكن، وإلّا استغفر له وتصدَّق له بما يمكنه، فإنّ الله تعالى إذا علم صدق العبد أرضي الله عنه خصماءه. قوله : ( في كلَّ توبة أي من كلِّ ذنب . قوله : ( في ذنب لم ينقض : أي: بأن كان يمكن استمراره
The principles of the path leading to Allah: and the resolve not to return to its like, and these two are necessary in every repentance. And the third is immediate abandonment of the sin in the present, and this only applies to a sin that has not elapsed, so it is obligatory to stop completing adultery and drinking wine, and to stop harming anyone, and to return injustices to their owners and seek forgiveness from the wronged if possible, and otherwise ask forgiveness for him and give charity on his behalf as much as he can, for Allah Almighty, if He knows the sincerity of the servant, will satisfy his adversaries on his behalf. His saying: (in every repentance) meaning from every sin. His saying: (in a sin that has not elapsed) meaning: that its continuation was possible.
Source pages p. 432

Commentary
This passage defines the three essential conditions of repentance (tawbah). The first condition is regret (nadam) and the second is resolve (al-azm) not to return to the sin—these two are necessary in every repentance. The third condition is immediate abandonment (al-iqla') of the sin in the present moment; this applies only to sins that are ongoing and can still be stopped, such as adultery, drinking wine, or harming others. The author provides examples: if one has wronged someone, he must return what is owed and seek the person's pardon; if that is not possible, he should pray for them and give charity on their behalf. The passage concludes with a gloss clarifying that 'in every repentance' means from every sin, and that 'a sin that has not elapsed' means one whose continuation is possible.

توبة
عزم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think repentance only requires regret, without a firm resolve to change.
Correction: Sanusi emphasizes that both regret and resolve are necessary in every repentance, as stated in the passage.
